# Involuntary Architecture

# Chapter 1

The filing charm collapsed on a Tuesday evening, and three years of sorted correspondence slid from its shelves and pooled across Hermione's office floor.

Hermione stared. The charm was hers, a standard administrative enchantment she'd anchored to the oak cabinet in her first week in the Department of Mysteries, the kind of spell that should have sat in the wood and worked without attention for years. She'd refreshed it once, maybe twice, in all that time. It was not supposed to fail.

Kneeling, she picked up the nearest scroll, an internal memo from August with the wax seal still intact, and pushed magic back into the cabinet's grain. The spell caught, wavered, and held, but only thinly. The difference registered at once: same wand, same intention, same clean structure, but the enchantment sat in the wood like something balanced there, not grown through it.

So she filed the memo by hand, then the next one, then the next. By the time the last scroll was back in place, it was past eight and her knees ached from the stone floor. After hours the Department of Mysteries did not sound empty so much as stoppered; the soundproofing charms sealed the silence until her own breathing was the loudest thing in the room.

In the corridor, the lights were thinning again. They weren't flickering. They were just dimming so gradually it was impossible to call the moment of failure. For weeks she'd been logging small degradations: the east archive's preservation charms developing a stutter in September, Section G's diagnostic arrays returning readings she was no longer willing to call anomalous, her office temperature holding two degrees low since mid-October. Anyone else could have ignored it under a jumper. Hermione Granger measured things.

In September she'd written the report: detailed, thorough, exhaustive enough to irritate anyone assigned to read it. Croaker had read it once, asked her to keep monitoring, and said nothing further. The Department of Mysteries did not acknowledge crises. It acknowledged data.

The data said Britain's ambient magical field was declining. The decline was not dramatic. No buildings were collapsing. The Prophet was not issuing special editions. There was just accumulation. A charm that should have lasted three years failed after two and a half. Floo connections dropped mid-sentence. A Healer at St. Mungo's had to cast twice where once used to suffice. Each failure was small enough to dismiss; together they formed a pattern she had stopped pretending not to see. By mid-October her private log ran to thirty-seven pages. People who understood the graph got quiet. People who didn't argued with it.

Every archive available to her had already been searched: the Department's restricted collection, Hogwarts under a special dispensation from McGonagall that came with no questions and no deadline, two private collections, one in Devon and one owned by a Ravenclaw academic who owed her a favor, and everything the Ministry had ever published on ambient-field theory. None of it reached far enough back. The decay was older than the Ministry, older than Hogwarts, rooted in infrastructure that predated modern magical governance, and the records that mattered had mostly stayed in family hands.

Most collections had been catalogued after the war.

One had not.

Three weeks of cross-referencing Devon notes against Hogwarts frameworks had gotten her only this: somewhere in the deep structure of British magic sat an anchoring system no surviving public text described adequately. Its outline showed up in margins and footnotes, in references to anchoring lines and foundational nodes and a network underneath the visible spellwork. The primary sources were missing. Every trail ended at old families. Most had died out, cooperated, or yielded nothing new. One was named Malfoy.

Before leaving, she took the latest incident summaries and went down to Section G because going home on a fresh failure without checking the larger trend would only have meant spending the walk inventing worse numbers. The diagnostic room sat behind three wards that had started requiring a second attempt every few days. Tonight the first recognition charm skimmed over her badge and failed to catch. The second one admitted her with a reluctant click.

The main array looked intact until she started it. Then the brass needles shivered out of sequence and the rune ring on the outer plate lit in patches instead of cleanly around the circumference. Hermione recalibrated by hand, fed the last week's figures into the basin, and waited while the silver surface thickened, steadied, and finally gave her a line graph she disliked on sight. The drop was not catastrophic. It was worse than that. It was consistent.

She checked the incident summaries against the same dates. A Floo relay in Leeds had spat a woman into the wrong grate with her shopping half-charred around her ankles. A charms apprentice in Bath had snapped a levitation sequence halfway through and dropped a crate of bezoar phials hard enough to shatter nine of them. Two accidental-magic reports from Muggle homes had come in that morning, both flagged for unusual force. One six-year-old in Croydon had burst every lightbulb in the kitchen and driven the cutlery into the plaster. Another child in Luton had tried to call a toy broom to hand and blown the sitting-room window outward instead. The Ministry language for these things was restrained to the point of dishonesty. *Volatility event.* *Unexpected magical surge.* Hermione read the lines and pictured broken glass in a terraced house, parents bleeding from their hands while they tried to understand what their child had done.

She copied the figures into her private log. Halfway through, the self-inking quill dragged dry across the page. Hermione swore softly, shook more ink into the reservoir herself, and finished the entry by hand.

That was what the crisis looked like, most days. Nothing grand. Nothing anyone in authority could stand up and point at in a briefing room. Just enough failure, scattered widely enough, that responsibility could always be passed one desk farther down the corridor.

Down in the Atrium, a maintenance wizard was trying to fill a cleaning bucket from the fountain. On his third attempt the water vanished again before the mop touched the floor. He swore at the bucket, refilled it, and swore harder when the charm stripped the water away a second time. Hermione watched long enough to confirm it wasn't a one-off, then walked out through the visitor entrance into the October night.

London was cold, damp, and entirely itself. Muggle infrastructure had its own forms of failure, but tonight the streetlights worked, the buses ran, and the pavement held steady under her feet. She passed the Tube station and kept walking. The choice had started in January after a Knight Bus ride ended with her sick on the pavement outside a Tesco and the simple thought, humiliatingly useful, that she could just use her legs. The habit had stayed. Forty minutes through Holborn and Camden, past steamed chip-shop windows, a man swearing into his mobile, a woman hauling a sleeping child out of a minicab. None of it relied on magic. Reliability had become more valuable than convenience, which was not a preference she'd expected to acquire.

---

Her flat in Kentish Town sat above a shuttered dry cleaner's. It was one bedroom on a cheap lease, with cream walls she'd never bothered to cover. Books were everywhere: shelves, table, kitchen counter, the armchair by the window that had become storage after the cushion's warming charm started bleeding out by midnight. A blanket still lay folded over one arm, unused. In the bathroom, a Muggle umbrella occupied the tub because a drying charm had once held long enough to become invisible and then failed, leaving the object dry, purposeless, and somehow permanent. No one had been invited over since the move. The place was for sleeping, reading, eating, and leaving again.

By the door, the sticking charm she used as a coat hook had started to peel from the wall. Hermione pressed it back with her thumb until it caught, barely. In the kitchen the kettle charm was unreliable, so water went on over the gas hob instead. The hob was Muggle. The flame did what fire did every time.

Dinner was toast and the last of the cheddar because the refrigerator's expansion charm had collapsed the week before, shoving everything back to actual size and cracking a jar of chutney against the rear shelf. She'd cleaned the glass out of the grooves with a butter knife, bought another jar, put the milk back, kept going. The flat still functioned. So did the divorce, if functioning meant nobody shouted and the paperwork stayed where she'd filed it.

Two months earlier Ron had called to say he was seeing someone from the second shop at Weasleys' Wizard Wheezes. He'd handled it carefully, with the decency he reached for when he knew he was crossing old ground, and she'd said the right things and meant them. Afterwards she'd stood in the kitchen and waited for hurt, jealousy, anything with edges. Nothing came. Just the flat remainder of a marriage that had subtracted itself so slowly there was nothing left to divide.

At the counter she read a journal article on her tablet while she ate. The subject was ambient-field theory, a fresh defense of a conjecture she'd already dismissed twice. The author called magical fluctuation cyclical, self-correcting, ultimately benign; the data stopped in 2001. Thoroughness made her finish the piece anyway. Then she opened the folder of copied notes she'd been carrying between office and flat for weeks: Devon references, one battered Hogwarts index, three pages of names of old families whose records had either burned, been seized, or produced nothing except elegant variations on *not here*. The Malfoy collection sat at the bottom of the last page with no mark beside it. She looked at the name, closed the folder, and washed the plate.

Her left forearm itched. Automatically, she tugged the sleeve lower and pressed two fingertips through the cotton to the place beneath. The charm felt smooth. No seam caught. She did it every morning before work and sometimes again by evening without noticing, in the same sequence as checking the flat wards and patting for her keys.

The Floo coughed.

Green flame rose to her knees and died. Hermione waited. The network tried again with a wheeze and a brief unsteady flare, then dropped. A voice cut through the fire for a second and vanished.

Kneeling at the grate, she cast a diagnostic. The relay was still there. She could feel the north London node humming under the failure, serving four thousand households and barely holding together. Harry's fireplace at Grimmauld Place was trying to reach her.

Apparition was an option. An owl was an option. Instead she wrote, *Floo dropped again. I'm all right. Tomorrow?* and sent the note through the flat's mail charm, which still mostly worked unless it decided to deposit Ministry correspondence in a Muggle postbox three streets over.

Harry would ask whether she'd slept. She'd say yes. He'd make that face where his mouth stayed neutral and his eyes said he'd known her too long for this. Then she'd redirect because the truthful version snagged on stupid details. Yes, she slept sometimes. Yes, she ate. Yes, she kept arriving at work dressed and mostly on time. Once she started explaining why that was not the same as being all right, she would have to keep going.

On the sofa between two journal stacks, she listened to the Floo cough once more. Green fire flickered; Harry's voice made it through this time, just long enough for "Hermione?" before the network failed again.

She left it there.

---

Wednesday passed in refusals and administrative archaeology.

By ten that morning she had three replies waiting on her desk. The Devon collector's niece had gone through the remaining family trunks and found nothing earlier than 1794. The Ravenclaw academic wrote four dense paragraphs to say, apologetically, that his uncatalogued papers turned out to be estate ledgers rather than magical theory. Hogwarts sent a note from the librarian's office confirming that no additional access restrictions had been placed on her research and no additional relevant texts had appeared by miracle overnight.

By lunch Hermione had stopped glancing up at every owl tap. She took her notebook and went three floors down to the Records Annex, where the Ministry stored the paper skeleton of every post-war inventory, seizure, pardon review, and heritage dispute no one had managed to turn into a tidy public history. The room smelled of dust, damp stone, and preservation charms running too hot. One of the catalogue cabinets stood open because its indexing spell had begun shelving cards by date one day, color the next, and alphabet only when threatened.

She braced a knee against the cabinet and pulled drawers by hand instead.

The post-war heritage files filled two and a half cabinets. Families cooperated, obstructed, bribed, lied, appealed, and occasionally discovered the usefulness of suddenly claiming that generations of highly valuable magical material had already been lost to mildew, moths, or youthful carelessness. Hermione read through entry after entry. Black family papers: partial seizure, private settlement, library dispersed. Greengrass: inspected, catalogued, judged irrelevant. Lestrange properties: sealed, structurally unsafe, nothing retrievable without specialist curse-breakers the Ministry had not been willing to fund in 1999 and would be even less willing to fund now. Half the relevant records ended in the dry language institutions used when they wanted history to sound procedural instead of violent.

The cabinet light dimmed while she was reading. She tapped it with her wand. It brightened for five seconds and died again. Hermione took the lamp off a neighboring desk and carried on.

An hour later she found the Malfoy file.

It was thinner than it should have been. Much thinner. The original intake form from May 1998 listed the existence of a substantial private archive, heavily warded, not made available for emergency wartime survey. A later sheet from August 1999 recorded that full Ministry cataloguing had been deferred pending criminal proceedings against the head of household, unresolved property claims, and "resident ward volatility incompatible with standard archival handling." Another note, unsigned, had been attached at an angle as if added in irritation: *Collection remains in situ. No external inventory completed. Review postponed.*

That was all. No catalogue. No follow-up. No later assessment. Hermione stood there holding four useless pages that nonetheless told her exactly what she needed to know. The library had not been absorbed into the post-war cleanup. It had not been opened. It had not even been properly touched.

She copied the reference numbers into her notebook because the duplicating charm on the public desk had fused two sheets together that morning and she did not trust it not to ruin the original. By the time she climbed back to her office, her fingers were blackened with archive dust and there was a smear of it on the side of her hand where she'd pushed hair out of her face.

Croaker's memo arrived Thursday morning on pale lavender parchment, interdepartmental seal, and language so carefully imprecise it might as well have had *routine* stamped over it in red. His margin note was smaller and more direct: *Granger. Relevant to your research. Last uncatalogued major collection. Authorize at your discretion.*

The substance only confirmed what the Records Annex had implied. The Malfoy family library in Wiltshire remained the last major private collection in Britain still uncatalogued. It was known to contain pre-Ministry records, foundational magical theory, and archive material unavailable in any public institution. A qualified researcher was requested to assess the collection for texts relevant to current departmental concerns.

Hermione read it once for content, once because *Malfoy family library* landed in her chest like dropped weight, and once more for the omissions. Croaker did not mention magical decay. He did not mention the stabilization indices she'd been tracking for months. He did not put in writing that the books in that house might be the only surviving record of the system now failing across Britain.

So Croaker was being careful. The memo could sit in a file as routine heritage work. The margin note could not. Croaker was telling her, as discreetly as the institution allowed, that every conventional route had run out.

She flattened the parchment on her desk and pressed both palms to the wood. The Ministry-standard oak felt solid under her hands. It did not move.

Seven years had passed since she'd last set foot on the property. Draco Malfoy she had not spoken to since the trial in the spring of 1999, six years ago, when she'd still been nineteen and angry enough to believe survival ought to buy some authority over how the war was remembered. The Wizengamot had been ready to send him to Azkaban. He had been sixteen when he took the Mark and seventeen at the battle. The prosecution called that choice, as if any child in that house had ever possessed the kind of freedom the word implied.

So she'd testified. She had sat with both hands knotted in her lap, thumbnail biting into the heel of the other hand, and answered as narrowly as she could. The record showed what had happened in the drawing room, and she had no intention of saying the word on her arm aloud for a chamber full of quills. What she gave them instead was the part that mattered for judgment: Draco Malfoy had been a frightened boy in a war, and when the Snatchers dragged them into that room and demanded identification, he had looked at Harry's swollen face and lied. Fear was one thing. Mercy was another. Sitting there, she had not been willing to pretend either of them erased the other.

The verdict was acquittal with conditions and two years of wand restriction. He had stood when it was read, looked at the bench, looked at his mother, looked at the door, and left. From the gallery Hermione had waited anyway, furious with herself for waiting. A glance would have done it. Even a nod would have done it. He had given her nothing.

Six years later she could still feel that stupid pause in the gallery if the memory caught her tired.

The library was still the right lead. For weeks she'd known the gap in her research was historical. The anchoring infrastructure was older than the Ministry and partly built by old families; the Malfoys were one of them, and their archive had stayed private through war, scandal, and every post-war cleanup effort that had pried open the rest. Bathilda Bagshot mentioned the collection in a footnote. Heritage Division whispered about it over lunch. Lucius had shown off selected texts to scholars when it suited him and kept the foundational records locked away. That left Hermione with one remaining option and no clean feelings about it.

She authorized the assignment. Then she drafted the access request to the current head of the Malfoy household, outlining scope, departmental interest, handling protocols, and the compromise she could just barely imagine tolerating: scheduled access in daylight hours, no unnecessary escort, return to London each evening if practical. The first sentence came out too stiff. She struck through *required* and wrote *requested*. She started a line about accommodations and crossed that out entirely. By the time she had a version she could send, the page looked clean enough to pass for calm. It mentioned neither the trial nor the marble floor nor the room's dead cold pressing through her back.

Her best external hand made the letter look like correspondence between strangers. That, at least, was true. Whatever Draco Malfoy had become at twenty-five, she didn't know him. Whatever she had become, all work and exhaustion and a flat full of half-failed charms, he didn't know her either.

By the time she signed, sealed, and pushed back from the desk, her hands were shaking. For a few seconds she only watched them where they rested on the chair arms. The decision had reached her body later than her handwriting. Eventually the shaking eased. The corridor lights outside her office dimmed, steadied, dimmed again. She sat there until her hands were still enough to send the owl.

That should have been the end of the matter for the day, but it wasn't. Once the letter had gone, every ordinary task in front of her became faintly absurd. She still had a stack of stabilization tables to reconcile against the previous quarter. She still had to note the failed corridor lights in the maintenance ledger because pretending not to see them would not make them work. She still had to answer a junior researcher's request for her September field equations. She did all of it. The figures aligned under her hand. The equations packet went out with the right annotations. When she finally looked up, the office had emptied around her and the light had gone the dull color of late afternoon.

Only then did she realize she had missed lunch and most of the afternoon.

On the way out, she stopped by Section G again. The overnight basin had updated itself with new reports. A pediatric Healer in Birmingham had logged three wandless surges in children who had never shown that degree of force before. An apothecary in York had reported preservation charms dropping on regulated ingredients. Hermione wrote the reference numbers into the margin of Croaker's memo because her notebook was in her bag and she did not trust herself to go searching for it without rereading the copy of her own signature still ghosting the back of her mind.

By the time she left the Ministry, the letter to Wiltshire was already beyond recall.

---

Harry's note came Friday evening on a Ministry owl that looked nearly as frayed as the network.

The Floo had been down most of the day. North London's relay had failed again and the public explanation in the morning *Prophet* had used the phrase *scheduled maintenance*. Hermione had read it over tea at her desk and felt heat rise behind her eyes. Lying was infuriating on its own. Being lied to about data she'd gathered herself was worse.

His handwriting remained tight and slightly crooked, the trace of learning to write in a cupboard and never fully losing the angle of it.

*Floo's out again. Ginny says come for dinner Sunday. Don't bring wine, we've still got the six bottles from your birthday because you abandoned us with them. Have you slept at all this week?*

The note made her smile. Harry had known her since she was eleven, bossy, certain, and unbearable in very specific ways. He had stayed through every version since. He no longer asked the kind of question that left room for a graceful lie. He went after the practical failure and waited.

Back came her answer in a hand only slightly neater than his.

*I'm sleeping. I slept eight hours last night. I've taken up watercolours. I have a robust social calendar and many leisure interests. Tell Ginny yes to dinner. And I left early because Robards trapped me in a thirty-minute story about ward calibration and I could feel parts of my brain shutting down in self-defence.*

The quill hovered after that. She could tell him about the Manor now or wait for the Auror grapevine to do it for her within the week. She wrote *I have to go to Wiltshire on Monday* in her head, saw at once how Harry's face would change, and set the quill down without touching paper.

She told him nothing. Sunday would do. In person she could keep it factual for at least the first thirty seconds, which was usually long enough for Harry to hear *handled* and not yet hear the rest.

The second owl arrived after dark.

It hit the outer sill hard enough to make her look up from the kettle. Bigger than the Ministry bird and glossier, with rain slicking the feathers dark as ink, it waited at the window with the kind of patience that suggested it had been taught not to leave until answered. Hermione crossed the room, unlatched the pane, and took the envelope from its leg.

Cream paper. Green wax. The Malfoy crest pressed so sharply into the seal it looked cut instead of stamped.

For a second she only stood there with it in her hand. Then she put the kettle aside, broke the wax, and unfolded the letter.

The hand was not the one she remembered from school assignments thrown down in an irritated slant. This was narrower, more controlled, the lines held almost too tightly as if looseness itself had become a risk.

*Ms. Granger,*

*Your request is acceptable on a provisional basis. No volume from the library leaves the house. Given the scale of the collection and the state of several warded rooms, day access would be inefficient. If the Department intends a proper survey, the researcher will need to remain on site for the preliminary review. Monday morning is workable. Scope and conditions can be settled on arrival.*

*D. Malfoy*

That was all. No apology for the terms. No pleasantry. No acknowledgement of anything except the work and the fact of her name on the page.

Hermione read the letter again. The first request she'd made of herself after authorizing the assignment had been small and cowardly and understandable: go there, do the work, come home at the end of the day, keep London between herself and the house. Draco had just removed that option with six lines and a signature.

He was right. She knew he was right before the flare of anger had finished rising. Wiltshire was too far to commute sensibly without depending on networks already failing under strain. No private archive that old would allow books to move cleanly into Ministry custody, not with family wards laid through the collection and half the country's magic going unreliable around the edges. The house had kept the library private for generations. Of course it would insist on being consulted on its own ground.

She hated that he was right. She hated more that the letter made the practicalities look plain. There was not even room in it for the illusion that this might be a controlled visit.

The kettle screamed on the hob. Hermione snatched it off the flame, poured water without looking, and set the mug down untouched. Steam curled through the kitchen. The letter stayed open under her hand.

If she declined now, the Department would send someone else less qualified and probably less careful, or no one at all. The research would stall where it had already stalled. The corridor lights would keep dimming. Another child somewhere would put a window out with accidental magic because the country's field was misfiring under their feet and no one in authority wanted to say so aloud.

So there was only the next decision.

She sat at the table, pulled a clean sheet toward her, and wrote back.

*Mr. Malfoy,*

*Monday morning will be acceptable. I will require working access to the library, any associated catalogues or family reference systems, and a secure surface for Ministry notes. If there are house conditions relevant to handling the collection, please state them on arrival.*

*H. Granger*

She sanded the ink, sealed it, and only then noticed that she'd signed with an initial instead of her first name, as if parity could be arranged through stationery.

The owl waited while she tied the reply to its leg. When it had gone, the flat looked altered in a way that had nothing to do with magic. She stood in the kitchen with both hands on the table until the new fact settled into something solid. These would not be visits. There would be no trains back to London at dusk. She would sleep there.

The suitcase lived on top of the wardrobe because after the divorce there had been nowhere she particularly wanted to go. Hermione dragged a chair over, climbed up, and brought it down herself. The old extension charm on it had frayed two summers ago and she no longer trusted any repair she'd done under current field conditions, so she dug out the plain Muggle case from under the bed instead: hard-sided, black, inelegant, purchased in January when she'd started replacing household enchantments one appliance at a time with objects that did not depend on the atmosphere of British magic behaving itself.

Packing gave her hands something to do. She started with work because work was sortable. Devon notes. Hogwarts copies. Her current graphs. Three blank notebooks. The Ministry letters. A stack of sharpened pencils because self-inking quills had become temperamental and she was damned if she was going to arrive at Malfoy Manor at the mercy of unreliable nib charms. Then clothes. Wool jumpers. Dark skirts. Trousers practical enough for archive dust. Shirts that would survive being packed badly.

Halfway through, she found herself holding a pale blue blouse she had not worn since June. The fabric was too soft, too easily creased, wrong for ladders and old shelves and long hours bent over a desk. She folded it back into the drawer and chose a stiffer white one instead.

The motion should have felt ordinary. It did not. Each item placed into the case made the trip less hypothetical. Wand. Charger for the tablet. Hairpins. The tin of headache draught she kept in the bathroom cabinet. A Muggle torch. The small jar of ink that did not answer to refilling charms because it had been bought in a stationer's. By midnight the bed was buried in sorted piles and the case was only half shut because she'd packed books first and left herself nowhere for softness.

She stood back and looked at it with a disgust that had no useful object. The case itself was not the point, obviously, which only made the feeling more annoying. The trip kept becoming real in fresh, stupid increments. She had signed the memo, sent the owl, answered the letter, decided which pencils to trust. Authorizing the assignment had been one threshold. Agreeing to sleep under that roof was another.

On the kitchen table, Draco's letter sat beside Harry's note. One told her to come to dinner. The other told her to report to a house she had spent seven years treating as a location she could keep in the past if she was disciplined enough about not approaching it.

She should have written to Harry again. She should have said, *Actually, Sunday will have to be brief. I'm leaving for Wiltshire on Monday and I don't want to explain by owl.* She did not. He would arrive already worried. He would look at the case. He would ask the direct question. Hermione wanted one more day before that happened.

Past one, she carried the suitcase to the door and left it there because putting it back on the bed would have been a child's version of undoing the choice.

Sleep did not come.

She lay in the dark and listened to the pipes knock in the wall, the television through plaster next door, a siren thinning itself out somewhere toward Camden Road. Twice she got up to check that Malfoy's letter still said what she remembered it saying, as though the line about remaining on site might soften if read at two in the morning instead of eleven. It did not.

When lying still stopped even pretending to be rest, she went back to the kitchen and opened the incident copies she'd brought home from work. The newest report had arrived too late to make the day's briefing ledger. A child in Birmingham had panicked during a burst of accidental levitation and pulled every metal object in the room toward himself hard enough to split his mother's lip. The responding Obliviator noted household damage, neighbour exposure risk, and "parental distress significant." Hermione read that last phrase twice. Parental distress significant. Somewhere a Muggle mother was holding a tea towel to her mouth, looking at forks embedded in the wall and trying to comfort a child who thought he'd done something monstrous.

She copied the file reference into her notebook because if she was going to walk into the Manor on Monday she wanted the freshest figures in front of her, not the cleaner, older set she might have preferred to carry. The numbers were starting to outrun discretion. That was the whole point. The crisis did not wait for anyone's nerves to settle.

Near three, she showered because the flat had gone cold enough that standing under hot water felt simpler than continuing not to sleep. Steam fogged the mirror. When she came back into the bedroom in her dressing gown, the suitcase by the door was the first thing she saw.

Her body knew the route to panic well enough to begin without permission. Not a scream, not a collapse. Just the fast mean clarity of the drawing room snapping into place inside her head: marble under her spine, chandelier light in her eyes, Bellatrix's hand hard on her wrist, the knife point finding skin. Hermione pressed both palms to the edge of the dresser and made herself stay where she was until the room in front of her was only the room in front of her.

No one was here. No one was touching her. The walls of the flat stayed fixed. The window held. The suitcase remained a suitcase.

Eventually she could move again.

She dressed for bed a second time, checked the latch, checked it again, and lay down with one hand under the pillow and the other clamped over her left forearm. The charm was still there, slick and even under her palm, and beneath it the old raised letters seemed suddenly too close to the surface. She kept her hand there until her breathing evened out enough to pretend at sleep.

Sleep finally took her sometime after three, and even that felt closer to collapse than rest. At six the alarm went off to a cold room: the warming charm had bled out during the night. She showered, dressed, and snapped the suitcase shut.

Before leaving for work, she slid Croaker's memo and Draco Malfoy's letter into the front pocket of the suitcase by the door. The case looked ugly, practical, undeniable. Every time she crossed the flat, she saw it. Every time she saw it, the same fact waited there: on Monday she was going to Wiltshire, and she was not coming back at the end of the day.


# Chapter 2

The Mark woke him at four again.

Not with the old burn. Not with the fishhook drag behind his navel that had once meant summons, movement, whatever room the Dark Lord had chosen for that night's audience. That signal died with the man who cast it. What remained was slower and less useful: an arrhythmic ache deep in the bones of his forearm, magic trying to force itself through a route that no longer led anywhere, heating the sealed channel until the whole arm felt inhabited by failed intent.

In the dark, Draco lay still and waited for it to pass. The bedroom was too large for one person. Three years earlier he'd moved into his parents' suite, not out of appetite for grandeur but because the alternative was the room he'd slept in at sixteen, and that room still carried the ghost of the boy who had come back from the Dark Lord with bile on his chin and a strip of blackened skin on his arm. Airing it out had not helped. Redecorating had not helped. Eventually he'd shut the door and left it shut.

Around five the ache eased from unendurable to familiar. He got up because there was no reason not to.

At the far end of the ground floor sat the servants' kitchen, the one he actually used because the formal one still implied staff and a table laid for more than one person. Tea first. Then he ate toast at the counter, with no plate and no chair.

From there his body took the same route it always took to the library: east corridor, back stair, portrait gallery, narrow passage, south entrance. The reception rooms would have been faster. Faster meant the west-front room with its chandelier, the conservatory where Bellatrix had once sat humming in the evenings as if she were visiting relatives, his father's study with Ministry reports spread across the desk like a failed barricade. Thought rarely entered into it now. His feet made the turns that kept him out of certain rooms and let the rest of him catch up later.

The library was the only room that justified the house. By the south window waited his chair, shaped to him by repetition, and on the table beside it lay the treatise he'd been worrying through for a week. Seventeenth-century sympathetic magic, dry as old plaster and no more useful than the rest. For eight months he'd looked for anything that might explain why a dead man's brand had begun to wake in his arm. St. Mungo's had refused to touch it. One private curse-breaker had lifted his sleeve, watched the serpent twitch under probe light, and stepped back far enough to make her refusal feel physical. Another had been steadier. She had asked sensible questions, smelled of dittany and old wool, and spent nearly an hour mapping the curse's surface before admitting the visible work sat on top of something older and denser than her training reached. She'd suggested the Department of Mysteries with a straight face. He had laughed until she left.

So he read. Somewhere in the family's collection there might be a reference, or a precedent, or at least a theory that did not end in institutional helplessness. Morning after morning he pulled books, made notes, followed citations into older shelves, and found nothing but dead men's confidence.

All morning the Mark kept up its low pulse. Pressing his thumb hard against the brand sometimes blunted it for a minute or two. Lately even that had started to fail.

Winter altered the Manor before it did anything stranger. By November the east wing whistled at the windows, far rooms went hard with cold, and corridors sometimes seemed to narrow by inches he could never prove. He did not call the house alive, or listening. That way a shutting door could stay timber, stone, old magic, and the possibility that he was tired. This morning, with the Mark beating in his arm and the windows black, the distinction felt thinner than usual.

His mother had not visited in two weeks. That was deliberate. Narcissa Malfoy did nothing by accident. Three years earlier she had taken a rented house in Knightsbridge, tasteful and proportionate, rooms that stayed where they were put. She'd been graceful about it. *The Manor is yours, darling. It needs someone in residence.* She had never said *I can't live there anymore.* She had only paused on the threshold, set two fingers against the doorframe, and left them there a beat too long before stepping out. Twenty-five years in this house had worn at her in ways she did not discuss. Draco had not asked for details. Asking would mean admitting the house could lay hands on people at all.

By noon the treatise had yielded nothing. Lunch was soup, bread, and an apple he forgot on a side table until the flesh began to brown against the wood. After that came the east lawn, walked because his body needed movement and the grounds were the one place large enough to cross without brushing past doors he preferred closed. At half three, the usual circuit broke.

An owl struck the library window hard enough to rattle the panes and dropped Ministry stationery onto the desk.

Department of Mysteries. Departmental seal bitten deep into the wax. Paper thick enough to feel official before he read a word of it. At the library desk, where all mail got opened, he broke the seal and found Granger's name at the foot of the page.

First reading: content. She requested access to the Malfoy library for work related to the ongoing investigation into systemic magical decay. She listed the categories she needed with the precision of someone who had already exhausted everything easier: pre-Union anchoring theory, estate magic, curse-binding records, old family ledgers, architectural magic that predated the Statute. The Ministry would provide a standard stipend for room and board. She anticipated a stay of several weeks at minimum. She required unrestricted access to all holdings, including restricted sections, and permission to keep working notes in the house rather than transport them through a Ministry whose own wards were starting to misfire.

Second reading: the rest of it. She had not entered this house since Bellatrix Lestrange drove her onto the marble under the chandelier and carved a slur into her left forearm while Draco stood there with a wand in his hand and discovered, in real time, how useless wanting to stop something could be. The last time they had spoken was at his trial seven years earlier, when she sat in a courtroom and described that night with measured accuracy. Not melodrama. Not mercy either. Her voice had stayed level all the way through the bit about the knife. No word he might have offered then would have survived the distance between them. Silence had seemed less insulting than an inadequate apology, so silence was what he'd given her.

Beneath the formal request sat the real fact. Hermione Granger would not have written to him unless every other door had failed to open. He was the last one.

At her name the Mark kicked once under his sleeve, sharp enough to make his hand jerk. His thumb went to it before he'd decided to move.

She needed the library. In return, he needed a witness who could look at the brand on his arm and not recoil from what had put it there.

The Unspeakables could probably have done something with it if he let them. Something classificatory. Something invasive. They would have turned his arm into a case file, his body into Department property, his house into a site visit. The Ministry had already owned enough of him after the war. He was not going to invite a second occupation.

He pulled writing paper closer. Ruined one sheet with *Accepted,* which sounded like a clerk. Ruined another with *You are welcome to the library,* which was both untrue and impossible. On the third he kept to terms. Access granted. Rooms available. Arrival at her convenience. One additional condition, set out in a single sentence as flatly as he could make it: if she intended to remain in the house, she would examine the Dark Mark and provide a full diagnostic assessment.

He nearly struck the sentence before sealing the letter. It was opportunistic. It was also the only version of asking he had.

Her owl returned the next morning with a reply no longer than his own. She would arrive Thursday at eleven. She accepted the additional condition only as a matter for later discussion and reserved the right to decline after preliminary inspection.

Two days later, with the date fixed and no further correspondence likely, Draco stopped pretending this could be managed by posture alone.

---

On the morning she was due, Draco cleaned.

Not the whole Manor. That project belonged to someone with staff or delusions. The library got done, the guest wing got done, the kitchen got done, and so did the corridors between them. Dust lifted from shelves under his wand. Stone floors lost their film of disuse. Fresh candles replaced the stubs in the library sconces. He stripped the old sheets from the east-arm suite, laid out clean linen that smelled faintly of lavender from the preservation chest, and opened the windows long enough for November air to scour the shut-room smell into something merely old.

There was a narrow study adjoining the library, east-facing and mostly abandoned except for household ledgers and a dead inkwell on the sill. His grandfather had once used it for accounts. Draco cleared it as well. The desk came out. The larger oak worktable stayed. He stacked the ledgers in a corner, opened the curtains, and tested the lamps twice. If Granger stayed several weeks, she was going to need somewhere to spread out her notes without taking over every flat surface in the library. He would rather surrender one room deliberately than watch the house do the choosing for him.

In the restricted section he unsealed the north-wall wards far enough to inspect them, then reset them to admit a second wand signature once he keyed hers in. Several shelves held books that objected to casual handling. One volume shrieked when disturbed. Another leaked sleep charms from a cracked spine. He moved both to a locked cabinet rather than risk the first afternoon turning into an avoidable disaster.

By half ten the cleaning had tipped into arrangement. Tea tray in the morning room. Extra towels in the guest bath. A spare quill and fresh ink in the study. He recognized, somewhere between decanting biscuits into a tin and checking whether the guest-room lock still worked, that this was the most attention he'd paid the house in over a year. The recognition annoyed him enough that he kept working harder.

By eleven he was in the entrance hall because that was where guests were received, and whatever else this arrangement was, it would be conducted correctly. The hall was one of the few formal spaces he had kept up. Black-and-white marble. A ceiling built to dwarf arrivals. A staircase wide enough for four people abreast. The family had always understood entrances better than what followed them.

From the drive came the crack of Apparition. She had not used the Floo. That could have meant the network was misbehaving again, or it could have meant she had chosen the method that left a front door between them. He suspected the second.

Before she could knock, he opened it.

On the step stood Hermione Granger in a dark coat with a leather bag over one shoulder and a second case shrunk to pocket size in her left hand. Her hair was pulled back and already escaping. The years had changed her and failed to change her. She was smaller than memory had made her, more contained, less mythic than the Ministry clippings and courtroom recollections he'd allowed to accumulate in her place. What remained most familiar was the expression. She had prepared for this. She knew how the first half minute was supposed to go and had come armed for it. Across the courtroom seven years ago he had watched that same preparation carry her through testimony that spared him nothing and still kept him out of Azkaban.

"Malfoy," she said.

"Granger."

No hand offered itself. The absence stood between them as plainly as any contract.

"Come in."

She crossed the threshold under her own power and it still looked like impact. The leather strap creaked under her grip. Her right hand left the bag and went straight to the button at her left cuff, thumb grinding once over the fabric as if checking that something under it remained covered. A pulse jumped at the base of her throat. Then she was past him, shoulders pulled square by force, and the front door was swinging shut behind her.

Formal hush closed around them in the entrance hall. Her gaze moved over the marble floor, the sweep of the stairs, the height of the ceiling, measuring what had altered and what had not. Then, involuntary enough that he might have missed it in anyone else, her eyes cut toward the west side of the house where the corridor turned out of sight. The last time she'd crossed this floor she'd been half dragged that way, Bellatrix's hand twisted in her hair, blood already wet on her sleeve. Draco did not need the trial transcript to remember the sound she had made in that room. He also didn't need to say any of this out loud for it to take up the whole hall.

"The morning room's this way," he said. "We'll discuss terms before I show you anything else."

"Fine."

He took the east corridor on purpose. She followed half a pace behind, quiet enough that the old house had room to speak between their footfalls. Her bag brushed her coat with each step. Once, at the turn, he heard her exhale through her nose the way people did when trying to stop a body from doing something embarrassing in public.

The morning room was the closest thing the house had to neutral ground. South-facing windows. A desk used for household business by three generations of Malfoys. Two chairs placed opposite each other with enough oak between them to count as civility. Tea already set out, whether from hospitality or habit he could not have said.

Granger took the chair opposite without waiting to be placed. She set her full-sized bag on the floor by her right foot and kept her coat on. Draco sat behind the desk and reached for the duplicate parchment she'd already drawn from the case in her hand.

"Ministry authorization," she said. "Access to the library and residential stipend. Croaker signed it yesterday because the accounts office refused to process anything involving this address without a second signature."

"Sensibly paranoid of them."

"Some of it is paranoia. Some of it is experience."

He skimmed the form. Technical wording, departmental seals, the usual language by which government tried to make a dangerous arrangement look administrative. At the bottom sat her signature, sharp and controlled.

"You've been specific," he said, tapping the list of materials. "Anchoring texts. Estate magic. Curse-binding. You're not wandering in here to browse."

"I've already done the browsing elsewhere. Public archives are incomplete, Hogwarts let me at what it had, and most of the private collections that survived the war are either inaccessible or useless on this point."

"And mine is the exception."

"Your family kept records instead of principles."

The line might once have invited a reflexive sneer. These days it only sounded accurate.

"You'll have full access to the library," he said. "All holdings, including the restricted sections, provided I warn you first which books object to handling."

"Object how?"

"Teeth, in one case. Sleep charms in another. General bad temper in several."

"Useful to know."

"The guest wing's in order. You've got your own rooms and a private bath. Use the kitchen whenever you like. There's a study adjoining the library if you need a separate workspace."

That got her attention in a way the tea had not. "Separate as in a door and four walls, or separate as in a table in a corner?"

"A door. Four walls. East-facing windows."

"Good."

He could hear the relief because she had not bothered to hide it. Not comfort. Not gratitude. Pure professional practicality, and underneath that the smaller, uglier fact that a room with a door she could shut mattered to her in this house.

"If you need to move through the rest of the house for research, do it," he said. "I'd only ask for notice before you enter private rooms."

"Which rooms are private?"

"My suite. My mother's rooms when she visits. A few family studies. I'll tell you as we go."

"Who else is in residence?"

"No one."

That was too quick, too bare to stand alone, so he added, "My mother comes out from Knightsbridge on occasion. She doesn't stay often. No staff beyond maintenance spells."

Her gaze held him a second longer than the question seemed to require, taking the measure of the answer and what it implied. He knew what she would have seen if she chose to see it. One cup. One place at table. The house too large and him thinned down inside it.

"And the Ministry?" he asked. "How often am I meant to expect supervision?"

"If you're worried about officials tramping through the corridors, don't be. Croaker wants results, not spectacle. I'll send reports by owl. If I need outside materials, I arrange them from here."

"So they've installed you and gone away."

"They've installed me where the books are."

Flat, factual, almost rude in how little room it left for self-importance. He respected it more than he meant to.

He set the Ministry parchment down. "My additional condition concerns the Mark."

Across from him, she didn't move. The only visible shift was in her hands. They went still on the arms of the chair, not loose anymore. Her left sleeve was buttoned tight at the wrist. Habit, then, and not an accidental one. Most people would have missed why. He did not. The room seemed briefly crowded with memory anyway: her body twisted on the floor, Bellatrix kneeling over her, Draco's own uselessness taking up as much space as the violence did.

Without ceremony, he pushed up his left sleeve and laid his forearm on the desk.

Even now the body's memory came with the exposure. Sixteen. A table edge against his hip. Lucius's hand locked around his elbow hard enough to bruise. The Dark Lord's wand pressed to skin, the spell driving in deeper than ink, and the smell after, not smoke exactly but burnt protein and fever. The Mark had faded after the war, gone to a dead dark grey. In the last month it had started blackening again, the skull and serpent sharpening at the edges as if old instructions were working their way back toward the surface.

Granger leaned forward. She did not touch him.

"Turn your wrist," she said.

He did.

Her gaze tracked the skull first, then the serpent's tail, then the raised skin at the edges, quick and level and unsentimental. He watched her noticing happen in sequence. Recognition. Professional concentration. Something else she killed before it reached her face.

"It's been waking up," he said. "Not the summoning. That's gone. This is different. Pain without a signal. The magic in it is restless. It keeps trying to move and there's nowhere for it to go."

"When did that start?"

"Eight months ago. At first it was mostly at night. Now it's constant enough to make sleep optional."

"Any precipitating event?"

"Not one I noticed."

"Any old objects taken out of storage. Any blood magic. Any curse exposure outside the obvious one. Illness. Fever. Injury."

"No."

"That's an actual answer or a polite one?"

"Actual."

Another second passed while she kept looking. At this distance the scar on his arm was nearly between them like a third participant in the conversation, and an old one.

"From the surface alone, this isn't simple residual curse activity," she said. "There are at least two layers visible, maybe more. The original brand is one structure. Something else is sitting under it or running through it, but I can't — that's a surface read. If you want an actual map of the curse, I'll need proper light, time, and sustained contact."

Not now hung in the room without her needing to say it twice.

"What are you proposing?" she asked.

"You get the library for as long as you need it. In return, you examine the Mark. Diagnostics, records, whatever you need to tell me what it's doing."

"You want me to study a curse two Healers wouldn't."

"I want you to study it because two Healers already couldn't. Or wouldn't. And I'm not — the Ministry is not an option."

"That's not the whole reason."

No, it wasn't. She knew that. He knew she knew it. The room held for a beat on the edge of the thing neither of them was going to say cleanly.

"You know what this is," he said at last, looking at the Mark rather than at her. "I don't need to explain the object to you. Or the provenance."

"No," she said. Her voice stayed even. "You don't."

That was the war-loaded center of it. Not just her department. Not just her competence. Him asking the woman Bellatrix had pinned to the floor in this house to come closer to the symbol burnt into his own arm by the other side of the same war.

She sat back a fraction. Enough to restore distance. Enough to remind them both the desk was doing work.

"I can assess it," she said. "I'm not promising treatment — I'm not even promising a full diagnostic until I know what the layering is. If it's unstable in a way that risks me or anyone else, I stop. If I need outside consultation, including the Department, I tell you before I seek it, not after."

"Agreed."

"The research comes first. The library is why I'm here. The Mark work gets scheduled around it, not the other way round. No improvised diagnostics because we happen to be in the same room."

"Understood."

"Last names."

"Of course."

"Records stay in my hand unless we agree otherwise. You can read them. You don't edit them."

"I'm wounded by the implication."

"You should recover."

There it was, the first movement that sounded remotely like the old version of them and nothing like it at all. He almost answered with something dry and easy. The moment did not permit easy.

"One more condition," she said. "If the house does anything that affects the work or the route between my rooms and the library, you tell me."

That pulled his attention off the Mark. "Such as?"

"Locks changing. Doors sticking. Corridors deciding to be elsewhere. I work with enough unstable systems already. I won't have the building pretending it's not one."

He might once have dismissed that as nerves. He did not now.

"Fine," he said. "If the house does anything odd, I tell you."

She looked as if she knew there were half a dozen loopholes in that sentence, all of them potentially useful to him. Then she let it stand. Either because she didn't want to spend the first hour here negotiating architecture, or because she had already spent enough effort getting through the front door to prioritize every risk correctly.

"The study," she said. "I want to see it before I unpack."

"You can."

"And for the Mark. Tomorrow morning, if there's decent light."

"There is."

"Good."

Neither of them reached for the tea. Steam climbed between them and thinned. At length he poured anyway, because the sound of liquid in porcelain gave his hands something to do. She accepted the cup and wrapped both palms around it without drinking. It took him a moment to understand why she held it that way: not for warmth, though the room was cool, but because it kept both hands occupied and visible.

"One other question," he said.

Her eyes lifted over the rim of the cup.

"How many places said no before you wrote here?"

She looked at him a long enough second that he thought she might refuse the premise outright.

"Enough," she said. "Public collections first. Then the predictable private ones. Then the people who claimed to have records and didn't. Then the people who had records and preferred not to let a Ministry researcher tell them what those records meant. Your library is one of the last intact family collections with any chance of holding pre-Statute anchoring work."

"And because I'm difficult, you assumed the books might be useful."

"Because your family hoarded old magic, Malfoy."

That, too, was fair.

"I'll show you the library," he said. "Then the study. Then the guest wing."

---

In the library doorway she stopped with one hand still curled around the brass. For one clean second she forgot to guard her face.

His usual route brought them there by the east corridor and the back passage rather than the reception rooms. If she noticed the detour she said nothing. Through the south entrance she went, then stopped just enough for the pause to register: shelves rising to the ceiling, mezzanine circling overhead, centuries of collection and theft and scholarship compressed into one room.

"Your catalogue," she said after a moment.

"Mostly nonexistent. My grandfather kept a ledger. It covers about a third of the holdings and stops in 1971. Everything since then is memory, luck, and family shorthand."

"Show me the ledger."

He went to a side cabinet, pulled out a thick green volume, and set it on the nearest table. She was already moving before it landed, fingertips gliding along shelf spines, head tipped to read titles. A folio came off one shelf, got opened, dismissed. Another replaced it. Then three more landed in a neat stack on the nearest table: French, Latin, English. She was not looking around anymore. She was sorting the room into use.

"Restricted section?" she asked, flipping open the catalogue without glancing at him.

"North wall behind the screen. It's warded, but I'll key you in. Most of it is Dark Arts adjacent rather than outright Dark. Theory, history, magical taxonomy. Some of it is genuinely dangerous. Some of it only thinks it is."

"That's your reassurance?"

"My honesty."

She made a small sound that might have been agreement and might have been the beginning of a headache. At the ledger she worked fast, eyes moving between his grandfather's script and the shelves themselves, already building correspondences. Draco watched her recalibrate the room in real time. To him the library was weight, ancestry, duty, the only part of the house he had not come to resent. To her it had become, within thirty seconds, a problem with components.

"You'll need to show me anything on estate anchors even if it isn't catalogued as such," she said. "Families called these things by different names. Foundations. Seat magic. House-binding. Inheritance wards. Half the time they're talking about the same structural work and pretending otherwise."

"There are several sections on domestic wards and line magic."

"Not line magic. Bigger than that."

"You haven't seen the shelves yet."

"I've seen enough of this country to know what people hide under tidy labels."

The dryness of it made him want, very briefly, to smile. He didn't.

He crossed to the north wall and lifted his wand to the blackwood screen. The ward there recognized him with a low click and a stirring of air cold enough to smell metallic.

"Come here," he said.

She joined him, leaving the cup untouched on the reading table. Up close he could see the fatigue under her glamour of competence. Not the scar itself. That remained hidden. But her eyes had the overbright precision of someone who had not slept enough and intended to make that everybody else's problem rather than her own.

"The ward records owners and entrants," he said. "If you try to remove anything from the restricted shelves without telling me, I will know."

"I wasn't planning to smuggle your books."

"Good. This is the sort of house where theft becomes melodrama quickly."

"Charming."

He keyed in her wand signature. The screen split at the center and drew back into the wall with a soft scrape of hidden mechanisms.

She took one look and swore under her breath. Not theatrically. Not for his benefit. Genuine professional shock.

"What?"

"You have two copies of Vablatsky's field amendments."

"Is that unusual?"

"One complete copy is unusual. Two is obscene."

"My ancestors were obscene in several directions."

She was already inside the ward before the line had fully left his mouth, head turning shelf to shelf. He followed because letting anyone stand alone in the restricted section on the first day felt irresponsible even when the anyone in question was Hermione Granger.

"These are arranged by century, not by subject," she said.

"They were arranged by whichever Malfoy acquired them."

"Of course they were."

She drew out a narrow volume bound in cracked blue leather, checked the title page, replaced it, took down another. Her movements remained efficient, but the strain of the entrance hall had shifted form rather than disappearing. He could see it in the way she kept her left arm tucked closer to her body than her right, in the set of her mouth when she found something useful and did not allow herself the luxury of open excitement.

"How much of this have you already gone through for the Mark?" she asked.

"Enough to learn that old families loved writing about permanence and very little about failure."

"That's because failure embarrasses archives."

"It embarrasses families more."

That made her glance at him. A real glance, not a professional scan. For a moment there was something close to recognition in it. Not forgiveness. Certainly not. Just the faintest acknowledgment that he had said a true thing and not hidden himself behind irony while doing it.

"Any family records on alterations to the house itself?" she asked.

"Ledgers, maintenance books, some correspondence. Not organized."

"Fine. I'll organize them."

"That sounded less like a plan than a threat."

"It's both."

Within minutes she had built three stacks on the nearest library table: anchoring theory, domestic magic, and historical curse structures. A fourth pile started when she found references that looked irrelevant but not safely irrelevant. She claimed the western reading table without consultation, then the smaller side table for notes. He let her. Watching her establish territory had a strange steadiness to it. Less invasion than usefulness. Less threat than motion.

"The study's through there," he said, nodding toward the east door. "If you want a separate workspace."

She followed him out of the library proper and into the adjoining room he'd cleared that morning. Compared with the library it was plain: oak table, two lamps, shelves along one wall, windows facing east over a stand of bare trees.

She set her bag on the worktable and finally took off her coat. Underneath she wore a dark green jumper and a buttoned blouse, sleeves down despite the heat trapped in the room.

"This will do," she said.

"High praise."

"It has a door that closes, stable light, and enough table space for notes. That's all I need."

He almost told her there was a locking charm on the inside if she wanted it. The information felt too intimate in his mouth for a first afternoon and too obvious once given. She would find it herself.

"If you leave papers here, the room generally holds them where you put them," he said instead. "More reliably than the rest of the house."

"Generally."

"I said what I meant."

She walked the perimeter once, testing the floorboards with her weight as if the room itself were under examination. At the window she looked out over the grounds, not because the view mattered, he thought, but because it gave her a moment to stand with her back to him and put her face back in order.

"Tomorrow morning," she said. "I'd like to start the Mark diagnostics here. Better light. Better table height. Less chance of a cursed book shouting while I'm trying to concentrate."

"What time?"

"Nine."

"Fine."

"I'll need uninterrupted access for at least an hour."

"You'll have it."

The arrangement had now acquired a table, an hour, a room, and therefore reality.

From there the ground-floor tour covered only what he meant it to cover. The morning room again. The servants' kitchen with its uneven stone floor and grey basin light. Her gaze flicked once to the single mug on the rack, once to the lone plate drying beside it, then to the saucepan he'd left soaking in the sink.

"You weren't exaggerating about no staff," she said.

"Would that have improved the sales pitch?"

"No."

He opened the pantry to show her the shelves. Tea, bread, tins, fruit, whatever the weekly delivery had brought from the village. Nothing elegant. Enough.

"Use what you like," he said. "If you need anything specific for work or for yourself, put it on the list by the door."

"I can manage my own groceries."

"I'm not implying otherwise. The house is remote. The list is practical."

"Right."

She sounded tired rather than chastened, and he disliked himself a little for how quickly his voice had gone formal. He shut the pantry before the conversation could stiffen further.

Through the back corridor he showed her the gardens, then the passage linking kitchen wing to library. The west side stayed unmentioned. So did the shut doors along the way. She did not ask for names. He did not volunteer them. The quiet between those omissions had texture.

Up the main stair, across the landing, into the east arm: the guest corridor panelled in dark wood, sconces brightening as they passed. At the end waited the suite itself. November light came through the windows thin and unforgiving, laying the room bare in a manner he found unexpectedly reassuring.

"Bedroom there," he said. "Sitting room here. Bath through that door. The hot tap behaves better if you threaten it first."

"I'll keep that in mind."

She moved into the room rather than merely looking from the threshold. The first thing she checked was not the bed or the view. It was the door. She turned, tried the latch, found the lock, tested it twice, then let go as if she had not done anything notable at all.

Draco looked away before his noticing became visible.

"The windows stick when it's very cold," he said. "There's an owl perch outside the smaller one. If the Floo starts failing again, Apparition from the drive is easier than from the front steps."

"Do portraits come in here?"

"No."

"Good."

Her attention moved next to the bath, then the wardrobe, then the writing desk by the far wall. Inventory. Exit points. Working surfaces. Not the movements of a guest admiring accommodation. The movements of someone deciding whether a room could be trusted enough to sleep in.

"It's adequate," she said at last.

"There are preservation charms on the bedding. Fresh towels in the bath. If you need anything adjusted, let me know."

"Thank you, Malfoy."

"Granger."

The correction slipped out on old reflex and landed between them oddly, almost lighter than the rest of the exchange because it was so unnecessary. She looked at him, and for a moment he thought she might say something sharper than the day had yet permitted. Instead she set the shrunk case on the writing desk and straightened.

"Tomorrow at nine," she said again.

"I'll be there."

He left her to settle.

Ordinarily the walk back to the library went east corridor, landing, main stair, back passage, portrait gallery. At the foot of the stairs, where the passage should have bent left, a corridor stood open that had been wall that morning.

It wasn't new. It had returned. As a child he had used it often enough to remember his mother's voice following him through it; sometime around adolescence the house had swallowed it, the way it swallowed doors and routes when it no longer cared to keep them. Now it ran clean from the east stair to the library's north entrance, warm sconces lit, no dust on the floor, already smelling of paper and preservation charms.

Two turns were gone. So was the portrait gallery. The route from the east-arm guest rooms to the library had shortened to something indecently direct. The house had not opened a quicker way to the front door or the drive. It had opened one to the library, to the study where tomorrow morning she meant to put his arm on a table and touch the Mark.

Within an hour of Granger crossing the threshold, the Manor had given back a route it had kept from him for years.

The skin at the back of his neck tightened with the old animal warning of being noticed in the dark, of something larger than himself having just swung its head toward him.

One step took him into the passage. The sconces brightened. His footfall came back at him fuller than it did on the usual route, as though the stone were relearning sound.

Three steps from the reading tables, four from his chair, the corridor delivered him to the north library door.

He did not mention it.

The book on the table opened under his hand. Above him, boards in the guest wing answered her movements once, twice, then fell quiet. Behind his chair the north door stayed ajar, with the corridor beyond it still lit as if the house expected traffic.


# Chapter 3

For this, she'd overprepared. Of course she had. Three parchments of diagnostic protocols lay beside two standard Dark-object assessment frameworks, a stack of control questions, and a sequence of probing charms she'd modified after half the night with Baruffio's monograph on voluntary curse-binding spread across the bed. The literature assumed a compliant subject, adequate workspace, controlled light. It did not assume sustained contact with Draco Malfoy, and it definitely did not assume a house that had started paying attention the hour she arrived.

Morning light made the study usable. That was why she'd chosen it: east-facing windows, a table large enough for notes and wandwork, and the library two doors away in case the morning produced something she needed chased at once. If she had to think after touching him, she wanted books within reach and no intervening corridor.

Across from her, he sat with his left sleeve rolled to the elbow and his forearm laid flat between them like evidence entered into a file.

"I'll need to touch your arm," she said. "Sustained contact. Some of the probing charms need a physical anchor."

"I assumed."

"I'm telling you so you're not startled."

"I won't be startled."

His gaze stayed on the far wall. The control in it was so deliberate it made its own noise.

She moved closer. The chair leg scraped once over stone. From this distance the Mark looked nothing like the wartime photographs or Ministry records. In those images it had been vivid, almost aggressive: skull, serpent, ink-black lines that moved as if they had appetite. On his forearm it had faded toward dark grey. Not gone. Nothing about a Dark Lord's brand simply went away. But the black had softened, the edges had blurred, the serpent's motion had slowed to a tired twitch. It looked less like a symbol than an injury that had stopped healing because it had never been permitted to become an ordinary wound.

At her first touch, the useful fact was tension. Shoulders locked, jaw set, breath measured too carefully to pass for natural. Under her hand his forearm held itself rigid while she turned it toward the light, every muscle braced against stillness. The skull and serpent curved with the arm's actual lines, ink laid over tendon and bone like a stain that had learned the shape of him. His skin was cooler than hers. Pulse steady at the wrist, too steady. He was managing it. Of course he was.

"First pass is a general curse-structure analysis," she said, because protocol required explanation and silence was starting to gather weight. "It shouldn't hurt. You might feel warmth or a mild buzzing."

"Those are meant to be reassuring?"

"They're meant to be accurate."

That got the smallest movement at one corner of his mouth. Not quite a smile. More an acknowledgement that she'd sounded exactly like herself.

Amber light settled over his forearm as the first diagnostic charm resolved the curse's outer structure into something legible. Voldemort's magic carried the fingerprint she'd expected: cold, layered, built to bind loyalty through pain. The surface reading was ugly but conventional. A constriction matrix. Old allegiance channels. Embedded punitive feedback. Through the contact point came the low wrongness of the curse itself, a vibration just below perception, like hearing machinery in another room and knowing from the rhythm that something inside it had worn out badly.

She noted the lattice, angle of response, preservation of surface motion after caster death. On the page her quill stayed exact. Under her hand the Mark stayed still enough to pass for dormant if she had not been close enough to feel the faint, stubborn activity inside it.

The second probe reached the middle layers. Those were stranger. Beneath the brand sat the remnants of a communication architecture, a dead network that had once connected every Death Eater to its maker. Voldemort was dead. The infrastructure remained. Channels that had carried signal now carried nothing. Or tried to. The absence was not clean. Magic still moved down those old paths and met blankness, then circled back on itself. No wonder the ache had no pattern. No wonder it woke him at night.

"Anything?" he asked.

"The middle layer's trying to use channels that don't exist anymore."

"That sounds irritating."

"That's one word for it."

She annotated fast and set the page aside. For the third pass she used her own modification, designed to touch the deepest layer: the binding between curse and bearer, where the Mark met Draco's magical core. She had built the charm on a theoretical model and two badly preserved case studies. It should have done almost nothing if the literature had been right about post-caster collapse.

The Mark moved.

Not the sluggish serpentine drift of the surface. The dark magic in the deep layer turned toward her probe like iron drawn to a magnet. The lines on his forearm deepened by a shade. The serpent lifted its head.

"Granger."

"I see it. Hold still."

Her grip tightened by a fraction as she sent the charm deeper. The response intensified. Dark curse-marks resisted examination. They deflected, lashed out, or went inert. This one reached. Magic flowed toward her wandwork instead of away from it, and beneath her hand his arm, rigid from the first touch, went abruptly slack.

His breathing broke pattern. The careful rhythm gave way to something rougher, less managed. Under sustained contact the faded grey of the Mark darkened toward black, and the restless pull in the old channels stopped all at once.

She looked up. "Tell me exactly what changed."

For a second he didn't answer. Then, more quietly than before, "It stopped."

"The pain?"

"All of it."

The words sat between them. She had expected resistance or flare. Not this. Not immediate relief, and not the nakedness in his face after he said it, as if the answer had slipped out before he could make it smaller.

"Don't move," she said, softer now because the moment required it. "I need a control."

His eyes shut once. Opened. "Fine."

She reduced the charm output while keeping her hand where it was. The deep layer stayed quiet. Then, very carefully, she lifted her palm a bare half inch from his skin.

The effect was immediate. The lines of the Mark jerked dark. His fingers curled hard against the table. Breath caught high and sharp.

Hermione put her hand back at once.

Quiet returned just as fast. Not complete. Not cure. But the frantic, useless churn inside the middle channels dropped away, and the muscles in his forearm loosened under her touch with a relief too obvious to mistake for coincidence.

He let out a breath through his nose and stared at the tabletop. "That's not your spell."

"No," she said.

The answer came out flatter than she'd intended. She did not apologize for the control test. Data mattered. But a pulse of shame still went through her at how quickly his body had betrayed the return of pain.

She ran three more passes under sustained contact, varying pressure, charm depth, and wand angle. Each confirmed the same impossible pattern. Outer layer unchanged. Middle architecture still damaged. Deep binding active seven years after the caster's death and measurably responsive to her magic alone. Not to the diagnostic sequence in abstract. To her. Break contact and the Mark reactivated. Restore it and the agitation quieted again, as if some part of the curse had recognized what was touching it and turned to listen.

That word did not go into the notes.

By the fifth reading his shoulders had lost their first rigid line. Not relaxed. She did not think that was a state he let himself fall into easily, least of all with her hands on him. But the effort of holding absolute control had thinned. A pulse beat steadier at his wrist. Once, when she adjusted the angle of his arm to catch the light, his fingers twitched against the heel of her hand and did not immediately pull away.

Her own body was being unhelpful. The clinical frame had not failed. It was still there, intact, necessary, and she held to it with all the discipline she had. But the contact remained contact. Skin. Warmth returned under his skin while the Mark darkened. Tendon shifting under her thumb when he tried not to react. There was no decent way to turn a forearm into abstraction when it belonged to a man breathing like that across a table from her.

When she finally let go, the absence of contact felt too visible. Her quill scratched three lines across the parchment and caught on the fourth because looking at the page was easier than looking at his arm, or the place inside her hand that still remembered it.
