"The curse structure is more layered than the literature accounts for," she said. "I'll need multiple sessions. The response pattern isn't documented."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning the deep layer reacted to my probe. It wasn't hostile. It was responsive."

"You've said that twice."

"Because it's true twice." She looked back at the notes. "And because I don't have a better word yet."

He reached for his sleeve, then stopped with the fabric in his hand. "How long did it stop for?"

She understood the correction a second late. Not how long had the examination taken. How long would the quiet last after her hand was off him.

"I don't know," she said. "We only just ended."

"Useful."

"I'm aware."

He dragged the sleeve down over the Mark and flexed the arm once, testing. His mouth tightened, then eased, then tightened again, as if he were trying to locate a pain that had not yet decided whether to come back. The motion made the answer plain enough.

"It's already returning?" she asked.

"A little." His tone made clear that a little was not nothing. "Not like before. Yet."

That landed harder than she wanted it to. Temporary relief meant two problems where she had thought there might be one. The Mark answered her, and the answer faded.

"I need you to keep track," she said. "When it worsens, when it quiets, what you're doing at the time, whether you're alone."

"You're assigning homework."

"Observation log."

"That's much less insulting."

Again that nearly-smile, there and gone. The dryness was easier for him than sincerity. Easier for her too, probably.

"Same time tomorrow?" she asked. Logistics were safer than interpretation.

"If you need it."

"I do."

He rose carefully, not because she had hurt him but because careful had become habit. At the door he paused long enough to say, without turning, "It hasn't done that for anyone else."

Then he left.

Footsteps carried him into the corridor and vanished before the distance made sense. The house had been doing that with echoes since her arrival. So had her attention.

East light moved across the table. On the page before her, *recognition* appeared in the margin, got crossed out at once, and became *anomalous differential response.* Defensible, certainly. A dodge too.

Dark magic, in her experience, was hostile or inert. Cursed jewelry did not welcome examination. Hexed artifacts did not lean toward a wand. A boggart-infested wardrobe did not quiet under touch. No framework she trusted covered what the Mark had just done. Worse, no framework she trusted covered the control result either. The reaction had not only been magical. It had been conditional. Contact present: quiet. Contact absent: return.

She sorted the morning into what she knew and what she refused, for the moment, to know. Knew: the Mark's old network was damaged but not dead. Knew: some deeper binding remained active. Knew: her touch altered the curse's behavior in a way no protocol accounted for. Refused: the thought that had tried to form when she lifted her hand and watched the pain come back. Refused: the possibility that the study, the house, any of it might matter.

Which was exactly why she went straight to the library. If the answer was somewhere ugly, technical work was how she preferred to meet it.

---

The library did not save her from dwelling on the examination. It merely gave the dwelling something precise to do.

She claimed the southeast corner because it was nearest the long worktable and because choosing a corner was simpler than choosing a system for a collection that did not appear to believe in systems. The first shelf held household ledgers in three hands, then a tract on medieval warding, then a box of unbound French correspondence, then a bestiary whose illustrations pressed faint noses and claws against the page whenever she walked by. Shelf labels contradicted contents. Provenance slips referred to rooms that no longer existed. Several volumes had clearly been stacked behind other volumes years ago and forgotten there. The Malfoys had not built a library so much as kept acquiring until acquisition itself became the organizing principle.

Out came the Department framework: classification by subject, subclassification by language, notation for preservation state, age, and provenance where identifiable. Within an hour the framework had grown three new headings. *Resistance to diagnostic magic* for texts that refused to be sorted and deflected her identification charms with patient stubbornness. *Unknown script* for the shelves of angular notation she had never seen, ink still wet-looking on parchment gone brittle with age. *Misplaced by any sane person* for the volume on blood ward inheritance tucked between household receipts and a child's primer on lunar tides.

She worked quickly at first, because speed let her outrun the memory of his forearm loosening under her hand. Dust greyed her fingers. Notes spread. Books formed rings around her chair: readable texts, unreadable texts, resistant texts, texts that needed preservation before handling, and texts she meant to put back at once but had already started mining for something more useful than their titles suggested.

A copy charm caught the unknown symbols from one brittle sheaf. A preservation veil settled over a cracked binding. A hostile little tract on funerary wards snapped at her knuckles when she tried to shelve it beside a family register and had to be wrapped in cloth before it would consent to remain there. That would have been enough work for a morning. It was not enough to keep her from revisiting the data.

She brought one page of Mark notes into the library with her. She told herself it was ordinary workflow. It was ordinary workflow. The problem was that the library seemed to notice.

The Flamel manuscript arrived in her hands by accident, or what should have been accident. Between two cookbooks sat a first-generation copy of his notation on anchor-point theory, and the shock of it sent her to the floor hard enough that dust rose around her skirts. The cover was warped by old water damage. A cramped marginal hand had annotated half the pages in writing she couldn't yet read. None of that diminished the prize. The anchor diagrams were intact. So were the proofs for ley-line stabilization and the sections Flamel had judged too theoretical to publish in his lifetime.

For several stolen minutes the world narrowed to ink, parchment, and theorem. Manor, Mark, Draco, all of it vanished behind the problem in front of her. Then the problem opened wider.

Flamel's late diagrams distinguished between a dead network and an unloaded one. A magical structure could survive the loss of its maker if the load had migrated elsewhere before failure. Vessel, secondary anchor, or proximate architecture: the phrasing in the margin was half-erased, but the model held. A network built to take signal from one source might continue in damaged form if another structure bore strain or kept the shape of the channels intact.

Hermione read that page three times.

Voldemort was dead. The Mark's middle architecture still existed. Its deep layer still answered. If Flamel's model applied at all, the old network was not simply rotting in place. Something had carried or preserved enough of it to keep it active.

She wrote *secondary anchor?* in the margin before she could stop herself.

Then she stared at the words until she added beneath them: *Could be local. Could be environmental. Test before theorizing.*

That was the moment the shelf above her shoulder moved.

Not much. A dry wooden sound and a drag of weight. She turned in time to see three books ease themselves forward until their spines sat proud of the shelf by an inch each, like fingers raised in a room where no one had asked a question aloud.

Hermione stayed very still.

The first was an early tract on inherited ward structures. The second was a field manual on curse containment copied sometime in the 1820s. The third was a narrow black folio with no title on the spine and a tarnished silver clasp.

She stood, crossed the half step to the shelf, and checked the wood beneath them as if that might produce an ordinary explanation. No hidden rail. No mechanism. No gap large enough for house-elf mischief, if house-elves were even involved in whatever the Manor considered its own business.

She took down the first two volumes. Both made immediate, inconvenient sense beside Flamel. She took down the black folio last.

It was heavier than it looked. The leather had gone smooth with age. No shelf label on the inside cover. No title page. Only a crest stamped blind into the front board and, beneath that, a line of property notation she could not read. Family shorthand, almost certainly. The clasp did not yield to fingers, unlocking charm, or a modest preservation override. When she set it on the table beside her Mark notes, the silver warmed under her thumb and clicked once.

Not open. Merely acknowledged.

She pulled her hand back.

That, too, went into memory before it went into notes.

Work gave her the only decent way to think. She mined Flamel for structural language, cross-referenced the ward tract, and discovered by noon that the collection's real problem was not only scale but intention. Once she started tagging texts that dealt with inhabited structures, responsive wards, or long-lived magical vessels, those texts began finding her faster than her catalogue could justify. A household inventory with nothing relevant in its title opened by itself to a sketch of boundary wards. A volume she had shelved two cases away on legacy enchantments turned up on the table when she came back from the copy stand. The black folio, which she had moved twice into a pile labeled *later, probably impossible,* reappeared on top of Flamel as if it had always belonged there.

The effect was not theatrical. No flying books. No slamming shelves. Just small revisions to location, each one deniable on its own. Taken together, not deniable at all.

She tested it.

First she tucked the Mark notes under a stack of irrelevant household accounts and left the table for ten minutes to examine the unknown-script shelf in the alcove. When she came back, the top account book was where she'd left it. Beneath it, no notes. Those sat beside the black folio at the far end of the table, neatly aligned with a tract on inhabited wards she had not taken down.

Second test: she reshelved the folio herself, memorized the exact gap, and marked the shelf edge in pencil. Forty minutes later the pencil mark remained. The gap remained. The folio waited on her chair.

Third test: she closed her notes entirely and spent a full half hour on the unknown-script material without letting herself glance toward Flamel or the black cover. The half hour produced useful copies and nothing else. When at last she turned, the shelf opposite had angled several inches toward the nearest window, presenting a row of bindings on estate magic that had not been there when she last looked.

By then she no longer had the luxury of calling it coincidence.

If the Manor's anchoring field was participating in the Mark, then the study adjoining the library had not been neutral ground this morning. Every reading she took inside these walls might already be contaminated by the place itself. That did not invalidate the data. It made the data worse. The Mark might not be a sealed problem on Draco's arm at all. It might be an interaction. Mark, bearer, house. Three variables where she had planned for one.

Her stomach gave a sharp, irritated reminder that the body remained part of scholarship whether scholarship liked it or not. She ignored it and kept working.

By midafternoon the table had disappeared under diagrams, transliterations, and competing hypotheses. Flamel gave her structure but not application. The ward tract confirmed that old houses could retain magical shapes long after their makers died. The curse containment manual was useless for anything subtler than a prison hulk. The black folio remained shut and infuriating.

At some point she realized the room had gone quiet around her in the specific way large rooms did when another person entered and did not speak.

Malfoy stood on the opposite side of the table with a cup in one hand and a book under the other arm. Dust streaked one sleeve. His hair was untidier than she'd yet seen it.

"You look terrible," he said.

"I've had a productive day."

"Those are not mutually exclusive."

She took the cup because it was there and because her hands had started to shake from hunger some time ago. Tea. Strong. No sugar. Annoyingly exact.

"The Flamel notation," she said, still half inside the page. "Do you know what you've got there?"

His eyes dropped to the manuscript open under her hand. "It's been there since before I was born. Possibly before my father was."

"It's one of maybe three copies in existence. The Ministry's been looking for one for decades."

"Then it's fortunate you checked between the cookbooks."

This time the joke was clear enough to count. It faded as his gaze settled on the rest of the table. On the black folio. On the stack of estate-magic texts she had definitely not fetched in any rational order.

"Did you put that there?" she asked.

"Which disaster?"

She touched the folio's cover. "This one."

He set his own book down and leaned far enough to see without crowding her. Up close the tiredness in him was less abstract. There was colour high in his cheeks that had not been there in the morning, the sort that usually meant pain was chewing at someone and they were refusing it the dignity of mention.

"No," he said after a moment. "I didn't."

"Has it been in the library long?"

"I don't know. I know the notation." His eyes narrowed at the line under the crest. "Or pieces of it. Very old family shorthand. Pre-reform property script."

"Can you read the title?"

"If it has one, it's not on the outside." He held out a hand. "May I?"

She passed it over.

He turned the folio once, thumb against the clasp, and went still in the way she was starting to recognize as actual attention rather than politeness. "That's odd."

"Because?"

"Because I should be able to open it."

He pressed the clasp. Nothing. Tried a smaller, more precise movement of magic through his fingertips. Still nothing. No click. No warmth. Only old silver under skin.

"Maybe the preservation work is keyed to bloodline," she said.

"I am the bloodline."

"I was attempting tact."

"Then you should stop. It doesn't suit you."

Dry again, but the dryness was stretched thinner now. He handed the folio back. The moment it touched her palm, the clasp gave that same single click.

Neither of them spoke for a beat.

"It did that earlier?" he asked.

"Yes."

"And you didn't lead with that?"

"I was trying to establish whether I was losing my mind in a reproducible way."

"Were you successful?"

"Annoyingly."

His mouth almost moved. Not amusement exactly. Something closer to recognition.

She set the folio down between them. "How long did the quiet last?"

He knew at once which quiet she meant. His gaze shifted from the book to her face and then away again. "A little over an hour."

That was shorter than she'd hoped and longer than she'd feared.

"Then it started again?"

"Yes."

"As before?"

"No." He considered, rejecting whatever easier answer had first presented itself. "Less sharp. More... restless."

Her quill was in her hand before she'd chosen to pick it up. "Any change in location? Did it worsen in a particular room?"

"Granger."

She looked up.

"I've spent eight months in this house trying not to notice where it hurts," he said. His voice stayed level, which made the sentence worse. "You'll have to give me a day to start keeping notes like a good patient."

Heat went across the back of her neck. "Right. Sorry. That wasn't..."

"No, it was exactly what it was." He reached for the cup she'd already emptied and set it a little farther from the papers. "Are you eating at any point, or is this the sort of scholarship where you collapse on a seventeenth-century manuscript and call it method?"

"I've eaten."

"Tea is not food."

"I didn't say tea."

"I don't believe you."

That should have annoyed her. It did, a little. It also landed with the unwelcome texture of care.

She tapped the folio instead. "Does the library usually bring you things?"

His expression changed at that. Not much. Enough. "Not where I can see it."

"That's a remarkable answer."

"It's the accurate one."

He picked up his book. At the door he stopped and glanced back at the spread of texts, the notes, the black cover sitting exactly in the center as if it had placed itself there for emphasis.

"If that book follows you again," he said, "don't tell yourself it's accidental."

"You say that as though I'd be tempted."

"You say that as though you haven't been doing it all day."

Then he was gone, leaving the tea and the dry fact of his absence behind him.

The conversation improved nothing. It only sharpened the problem. The relief from contact had lasted a little over an hour. The house was selecting material relevant to the Mark the instant she started asking the right questions. And Draco, who had grown up here, had given her the sort of answer people gave when they did not want to put a thing into firm language because language made it harder to ignore.

She should have stopped there. Eaten properly. Left the folio for daylight and Malfoy's partial translation. Gone to bed while the corridors still belonged to ordinary architecture.

Instead she kept working, because the pattern was there now and she could not bear to set it down.

Late light drained from the windows. Wandlight took over. She copied Flamel's diagrams, built a comparison grid for the Mark's middle channels against old inhabited-structure models, and finally, after another failed opening sequence on the folio, forced herself to shelve it in a temporary holding gap two cases over from the estate-magic shelf. She marked the location in her notes. If it turned up again, that would be data as well.

Dinner appeared on the end of the long table without servant or summons, warm and proportioned with irritating accuracy. Soup, bread, sliced pears. She ate half of it standing up and noticed only when the bowl was empty that she had not tasted much beyond salt.

By half past nine the library had narrowed to islands of lamplight and paper. Her back ached. Ink had dried on the side of her hand. She found the temporary holding gap where she'd shelved the black folio and saw at once that it was empty.

The note beside the shelf remained in her own hand: *black folio, clasped, old property script, hold here until daylight.*

The folio itself was gone.

She stood looking at the gap longer than the fact deserved if the fact had been ordinary. Then she shut her notebook hard enough to make dust jump from the table.

Bed. Tomorrow. There were limits to how much anomaly one day could productively hold.

---

At half past nine the Manor was quiet in the way places went quiet when they had not actually settled, only withdrawn from immediate display.

What she wanted now was her room, her bed, and the morning's notes spread in sane order under better light. The route should have been simple. Left out of the library, down the corridor with the tall windows and the portrait of the witch in green robes, right at the staircase, up one flight, guest wing. Fifteen minutes. Two turns.

Moonlight silvered the corridor when she turned left. The tall windows were where they belonged. So was the portrait frame. The witch was not. In her place stood a bearded man in late-sixteenth-century robes, dark-haired and still, watching with the calm reserve of a painted figure choosing not to move.

Possible explanation: she'd misremembered. No, not misremembered. Mis-sequenced, perhaps. Tiredness. Distraction. Ten hours of archival work after a morning spent with her hand on Draco Malfoy's arm. Unlikely, still, but safer than the alternative, and she was trying to get to bed, not start an argument with the house.

The staircase appeared where it should. One flight up brought her to a landing with a south-facing window. Her room faced east. No straight ascent should have done that.

Outside the window lay a formal garden she had not seen from any other angle: hedges clipped to geometric severity, a stone bench, tree line beyond. Up here the air was cooler. Underfoot, the stone felt rougher, older, worn in a different pattern than the guest wing. She put a hand to the wall and found the mortar colder too, as if she had climbed not a floor but a century.

Back down the stairs, then. At the bottom waited an entirely different corridor, narrow and vaulted, belonging to another architectural century. No portrait. No windows. Blue flame burned in sconces she'd not seen elsewhere in the Manor, and her steps disappeared into the stone instead of echoing back.

Hostile wasn't the word. Not yet. Pressure came closer: in the air, in the light, in the silence. Not-danger, if she insisted on classifying it. Which was not remotely the same as safe.

Point Me spun her wand north and held. Asked for her room, it pointed left and slightly up. Twenty meters, by estimate. Twenty meters later she opened a door onto a broom cupboard full of mops, buckets, and lemon-cleaner magic old enough to have turned sharp.

That was when fear stopped being abstract.

She shut the door on the cupboard and took two slow breaths. Then she started behaving like herself.

Illumination charms marked the floor at each junction. A chalking spell laid arrows on the stone. A simple tally went into the margin of her notes: left, straight, stair, landing, narrow hall, cupboard. She checked her watch. Eleven minutes since leaving the library. Long enough to be annoyed. Not yet long enough to be frightened into stupidity.

Another corridor offered the tall windows again, but the staircase at its end led down into a hall she'd never seen, vast and cold and unused. A hunt tapestry covered one wall. As she turned away, the nearest painted hound shifted its head by the breadth of a blink.

She went back at once.

The chalk arrow she had left at the last junction waited on the wall, except it now pointed toward her instead of away.

Hermione stopped so abruptly her shoes scuffed stone.

No trick of angle accounted for that. She had drawn the arrow herself. She knew the drag of her wand on the air. She knew where the stroke had gone.

"Right," she said into the corridor. The sound did not travel properly. It seemed to hit the walls and stay there.

She tried a structural revealing charm next. The spell mapped immediate load-bearing lines in pale blue over the stone and should have shown a clean corridor with side branches. Instead the lines layered over one another until three mutually impossible passageways occupied the same space. Old wall through new arch through blocked opening through something that was not exactly a room but had once meant to become one. The spell collapsed under its own contradictions and fizzed out.

Not ordinary wards, then. Or not only wards.

She kept moving. Method survived longer than comfort. A gallery of windows overlooked a courtyard too small to be the central one. A staircase ended at three doors, none familiar. One corridor felt almost known until the sconces changed, the ceiling lifted, and the air picked up the damp vegetal sweetness of a greenhouse after dark. Somewhere water moved in pipes or roots. Somewhere else a door closed far enough away that the sound reached her thinned and wrong.

Seventeen minutes. Nineteen. Twenty-one.

The real problem was not distance. Point Me kept finding it. When she paused to cast again, the needle always settled with infuriating certainty: there. Slightly up. Slightly west. Not far. The distance was true. Or true enough. It was the space between points that would not hold still.

She came back to the blue-flame corridor twice without meaning to. The first time she only recognized the chipped stone at the base of the third sconce. The second time she noticed something else. On the wall beneath the nearest flame, just above waist height, lay a streak of dark polish worn into the stone as if generations of hands had touched there without thinking.

She touched it herself.

The wall was warmer than it had any business being.

Not warm like sunlight on stone. Warm like a body under blankets. Under her palm ran the same low wrongness she had felt through Draco's skin that morning when the deepest layer of the Mark turned toward her probe. Not the same magic. Not identical. But adjacent enough to make the back of her neck prickle.

She took her hand away.

The blue flame to her right brightened.

At the far end of the corridor, where she would have sworn there had been only stone a second earlier, stood a narrow door with no handle.

She did not like the speed of her own understanding. All afternoon the library had revised her worktable a fraction at a time until she noticed. All evening the house had taken one turn, then another, then another, until she admitted she was not walking through neutral space.

Still, understanding wasn't compliance.

She ignored the new door and turned left instead. The corridor narrowed, bent twice, and delivered her to the same blue-flame passage from the opposite direction.

Fine.

She tried the right-hand stair. Up two landings, down one, past a cracked bust and a locked music room, through an antechamber with a ceiling painted as open sky. Blue flame again. Same warm streak on the wall. Same door now standing open by an inch.

The house could repeat itself all night if it liked. So could she. She backed away a third time, jaw set, and followed a long paneled hallway she had not yet seen. At its end waited a dead wall, a blind turn, and then, with a sort of patient inevitability, the blue-flame corridor once more.

By then the refusal itself had become data.

Hermione stood in the middle of the passage and looked straight at the open door. "You want something."

The blue flames held steady. No answer. No movement. Of course not. The Manor had not suddenly become conversational because she was tired enough to try addressing it aloud.

She approached anyway, wand raised.

Beyond the door lay a room barely larger than a confession box. No window. Stone on all four sides. One narrow lectern in the center, black wood polished by age. Resting on it, exactly where no human servant could plausibly have placed it without passing her three times in impossible corridors, sat the black folio from the library.

She knew it at once from the scuffed lower corner and the dulled silver clasp.

For a few seconds she only listened to herself breathe.

Then she stepped in.

Nothing attacked. No ward struck. The air inside the room was close and still, holding dry vellum, cold metal, and the mineral smell of deep stone. The folio sat shut. Patient. Infuriating.

She extended her wand and cast a detection sequence over lectern, floor, walls. Age. Preservation. Containment. No active curse she could isolate cleanly from the rest of the house. The readings tangled with the architecture and went soft at the edges, as though the room objected to being treated as separable from the Manor around it.

Of course it did.

She considered leaving the folio where it was and sleeping in the room itself out of sheer stubbornness. The thought was absurd. Useful only because it clarified the alternatives.

She backed toward the corridor to test one last time. The threshold held. The blue flames beyond it dimmed. The hallway outside did not become her guest wing. It remained the same narrow passage she had been circling for the better part of half an hour.

Not released.

She went to the lectern and put both hands on the folio.

The silver clasp clicked open.

That stopped her more thoroughly than any trap would have. During the day it had yielded nothing. Under Draco's hand, nothing. Under hers now, in the center of a room the house had apparently built out of refusal, it opened without resistance.

The first page showed no title, only a diagram: nested architectural lines overlaid with a second pattern in darker ink, curves and nodes that looked uncomfortably like a magical circulation map. A later hand had annotated the margin in property shorthand dense enough to defeat her at first pass.

She stared at the overlay until the lines stopped behaving like ink and became instruction.

Not proof. Not yet. Not enough. But enough to make her heart kick hard once against her ribs.

She closed the folio and tucked it under her arm.

The response was immediate and almost insultingly plain. The blue flames outside the room guttered down from cobalt to ordinary gold. Air moved through the corridor as if a window had opened somewhere ahead. When she stepped across the threshold and cast Point Me again, the wand snapped toward the right and held so straight it might as well have been pointing down a drawn line.

She followed it.

No wrong stairs. No repeated junctions. The chalk arrow she'd drawn twenty minutes ago appeared once on the proper wall, pointing the proper direction. Her illumination markers remained where she'd left them. One turn, then another, and the guest wing arrived without spectacle: faded blue rug, crooked sconce shade, thin line of yellow light under her door.

She stopped there with the folio still tucked hard against her ribs and let the shape of the thing settle.

The Manor had not tired of frightening her. It had not decided she had suffered enough. It had routed her through itself until she took the book it wanted in her hands. The release was not mercy. It was transaction.

Inside, the room presented itself exactly as she'd left it: notes on the desk, overnight bag on the chair, bed made with house-elf severity, east window intact. Her body only started shaking once the door was shut.

She set the folio on the desk and discovered that her right thumb was streaked grey-black where the clasp had rested against her skin. Not soot. Not ink. Some older metallic residue that did not wipe off on the first pass of her handkerchief. The mark stayed in the whorls of her thumbprint like a tiny bruise made of tarnish.

Observable consequence, then. Useful. She hated that part of herself for noticing.

Once she let herself count the rest, the evidence came too quickly. The portrait. The wrong staircase. The blue-flame corridor. The warm stone under her hand. The shelf shifts earlier. The folio abandoning the place she'd shelved it. Corridors stretching, then folding back, until she accepted the object the house had been steering her toward since afternoon.

She reached for simpler explanations and discarded them almost as fast. Old wards. Responsive architecture. Some inherited defensive system keyed badly to guests. No. Not enough. None of that chose relevant research material. None of that put a specific book in a sealed room and kept a person moving until she picked it up.

It had let her out when it got what it wanted.

The Manor, then. The house. Whatever name she gave it, she was sleeping inside something that paid attention and had preferences about her work.

At the desk, tomorrow's examination notes gained a second heading beneath Dark Mark protocol revisions: MANOR VARIABLES.

Under it she wrote, in ink pressed hard enough to score the page:

No movement through the house alone after dark.

Release tonight followed retrieval of black folio relocated from library.

Possible interaction: Manor conditions may have affected Mark readings. Retest before theorizing.

Thumb contamination from folio clasp; test whether removable in daylight.

Return black folio to library in daylight. Ask Malfoy for property-script translation before further opening.

The folio sat to the left of the page while she wrote, heavy and shut and no longer willing to pretend it was an ordinary book.

Only after the notes were finished did she open it again.

The diagram on the first page had not changed. Nested house-lines, dark circulation pattern, margin note in a hand newer than the rest. On the facing page the old property script continued in narrow, disciplined strokes. Most of it stayed beyond her. The few repeated marks she could sort into pattern looked more like room references than argument, and that was not a distinction she trusted herself to make after midnight.

She closed the folio on that and left it on the desk where she could see it from bed.

Sleep took a long time. When it came, it came shallow, with the sense of walls holding themselves very still around her.


# Chapter 4

The east corridor had a draught problem. Thin cold leaked between the stones near the third window, the one with cracked leading older than Draco's father. By late autumn he dressed for that passage without thinking, collar up, warming charm ready, body already braced for the bite.

Granger had been in the Manor for four days, and the draught was gone.

On Tuesday morning, halfway to the main staircase, Draco realized what was missing: no cold on the left side of his neck, no thin pull of air under his collar. Warmth held in the wall instead, deep and stored, summer caught in the stone.

It was November.

His palm went to the wall before he had decided to touch it. Something under the stone answered him, pulse-like and faintly alive where there should have been none. After a long second he took his hand away and finished the walk without brushing the wall again.

The library doors were already open. Both panels stood wide. Usually he had to shoulder one aside and rap the old pattern on the other if the latch had settled badly; that morning the oak waited for him like a welcome he had never once been given.

At the long table, Granger had three texts spread in a neat half-circle, the black folio from the night before shut beside her elbow, quill in her left hand, wand in her right. Without looking up, she said, "The Alderic folio was on the third shelf yesterday. Red binding. Preservation charms on the spine. It's moved."

"Moved where?"

The tip of her quill indicated the table beside her. "There. I came in and it was waiting."

Across the room, a black rectangle gaped on the shelf where the book had sat for years. Dust still marked its outline. Eighteen inches from her hand, the folio leaned against a stack of astronomical journals.

"The house does that," Draco said. "It rearranges."

"For me." Only then did Hermione glance up. Interest sat on her face more clearly than alarm. "Three other books I meant to pull today are here as well. I wrote the list last night. I didn't mention it to anyone."

Nothing useful came to mind. Agreement would sound absurd. Denial would sound worse. Throughout his childhood the Manor had ferried Narcissa's reading glasses from room to room without comment, but the house had known his mother for twenty-five years. Hermione Granger had been sleeping here since Friday.

Tea gave him something to do with his hands. In the service alcove off the library, the kettle took up its ordinary muttering while he listened past it. Preservation charms ticked in the shelves. Somewhere overhead a gallery board creaked. Under both sounds sat a lower hum that had not existed last week and now seemed to live in his sternum whenever Hermione was nearby.

By the time he returned with two cups, she was at the genealogical shelves with one arm stretched overhead, fingertips grazing a volume she could not quite reach. The wall altered so subtly that it took him a second to register it. Stone, shelf, and distance shifted just enough. The top row lowered into her reach. Hermione took the book down as if nothing in the room had moved.

His tea landed beside her untouched cup hard enough to send a line over the saucer. He said nothing.

Hermione set the book on the table and finally looked at him properly. "You've got that expression again."

"What expression."

"The one that suggests the building has insulted you."

"That would imply novelty."

A small line appeared between her brows. "If you know what it's doing, you might say so. I can work with strange. I don't work especially well with everyone else pretending not to see it."

Draco looked from her to the lowered shelf and back again. "I'm still deciding whether 'strange' is the useful word."

"Hm." She took her cup at last. "When you find one, let me know."

She went back to the page as if the room had not just re-ordered itself to suit her reach. Draco took his own tea and left before the temptation to stand there being offended by woodwork turned childish even in his own head.

---

Morning work in his father's study went nowhere. The Mark had been bad overnight and worse at dawn, a hot wired ache threading the old brand under his skin. By noon the pain had settled into pulses that caught whenever his wrist turned or his fingers closed too fast. Each catch brought the same small nausea with it. Riddle House rose behind his eyes in pieces too intact to be called memory gone soft: polish and old fire in the air, Lucius's hand digging into his shoulder, the copper taste from where he had bitten his mouth to keep from making a sound too soon.

Paperwork did not compete well with that. He read the same estate letter three times, answered none of it, and finally shoved the page aside hard enough to smear the ink.

The study offered no relief. Too much of Lucius remained in the room: the chair, the desk, the arrangement of shelves maintained for years out of inertia and cowardice and the practical difficulty of deciding where else anything should go. Even the air felt curated by someone else's habits.

A measured knock broke across the quiet. Three even raps announced Granger.

"Come in."

In the doorway she held an old manual open against one palm, two ribbon markers hanging from the bottom edge. "I think this is a diagnostic text on curse marks. It's outdated, but the methodology isn't useless. Your great-great-grandmother wrote it."

"Cassiopeia."

"Yes." Hermione came in only far enough to close the door behind her with her heel. "I don't think it's only a diagnostic text."

Draco looked at the manual, then at her face. "Meaning?"

"Meaning half the marginalia is property notation. Corridor names. Room temperatures. Structural shorthand, I think. She was comparing curse flares to changes in the house."

He let out a short breath that was not quite a laugh. "That sounds like her."

"You say that as if it explains anything."

"It explains that she disliked being told one problem was separate from another." His mouth twitched without humour. "Malfoys do tend to treat categories as a suggestion."

Hermione absorbed that, eyes flicking back to the page. "This was waiting on the library table this morning with two east-wing repair ledgers and a wall survey."

"The house brought you estate records."

"It brought me titles I wrote down before bed." She lifted the book a fraction. "I also wrote down three controls, because I was starting to feel ridiculous. Those stayed where they were."

"Controls."

"Yes."

"You set the library an experiment."

"I set myself one." Her tone remained dry, but the quickness under it was interest, not irritation. "The texts about your Mark and the east corridor surfaced. A botanical index, a book on French inheritance law, and a household inventory did not. If this place is going to be selective, I'd prefer to know the principle."

The principle sat under his skin already, hot and unwelcome. He said, "It may not have one."

Hermione's glance lifted in a clean, brief challenge. "Everything with a pattern has one."

She was usually careful not to sound triumphant when the world presented her with a puzzle. Today she managed something close. The effect on his temper was mixed.

"If you can spare ten minutes," she said, "I'd like to see whether the pattern holds when you're there. If more family shorthand turns up, I want someone who can read it without guessing."

It would have been easy to refuse. He could have said the study required him. He could have said the Mark was bad. He could have said he was not prepared to spend the day assisting her in an argument with the furniture. All three would have been true.

Instead he stood, because the alternative was staying in Lucius's chair with his own hand throbbing on the desk like a threat he had invited to sit down.

The library table had changed while he was away. Hermione's orderly half-circle of texts was still in place, but now three loose sheets lay weighted under a paperknife, lines of her narrow handwriting split into two columns. One column carried check marks. The other did not.

She slid the pages toward him. "Last night's list."

The checked titles were exacting and ugly in their relevance: curse persistence, inherited sigils, east-wing repair summaries, internal wall surveys, Cassiopeia Black Malfoy's private diagnostic notes. The unchecked titles looked chosen to be undeniably irrelevant: herbals, eighteenth-century French legal commentary, a volume on comet charts, household silver inventories.

"You really did do controls."

Hermione gave him a flat look. "Yes."

He took one of the unchecked titles and crossed to the indicated shelf. The book sat exactly where the catalogue said it should, wedged behind a dust line so clean that moving it felt like breaking evidence. He held it up between two fingers.

"Still there."

"Go on." Hermione was already pulling one of the surfaced ledgers toward herself. "Now look at this."

The repair ledger smelled of old vellum and cedar oil. The page she had marked showed maintenance on the east corridor from decades before he was born: repeated patching along the outer wall, references to a stubborn winter leak, a notation in sharper darker ink that had been added years later in family shorthand he had not seen since schoolroom exercises with Lucius.

He translated automatically. "'Buffer line unstable. Reinforce before frost.'"

"Buffer line," Hermione repeated. "That's the same phrase Cassiopeia uses in the margin here."

She turned the manual so he could see. Tiny notes ran up the edge of the page, precise and impatient. Wall temperatures. Flare intervals. Room locations. A small cluster of symbols he recognized from estate records sat beside a paragraph on curse wakefulness.

"She was comparing a body to a corridor," Hermione said.

"Or comparing a corridor to a body."

Her mouth moved as if she had started to answer and thought better of the first version. "Either way, it isn't hospitality."

The sentence sat between them longer than it should have. Draco set the ledger down more carefully than the motion deserved.

"What do you need?" he asked.

Hermione blinked once, surprised perhaps by the directness of it. Then she was moving again, fast and precise. "The actual structural surveys for the east passage. Not the summary ledgers. If the wall is holding heat now, something in it changed. I need to know what sat there before, and whether Cassiopeia altered it for a reason that had anything to do with what she was treating."

"Those records weren't kept in the open stacks."

"Can you find them?"

"Possibly."

He led her to the genealogical shelves because old Malfoy logic had always preferred burying practical matter under ancestral vanity. Estate records tended to hide behind marriage contracts, dowries, and land disputes, all the things a family liked telling itself were nobler than repairs. Hermione followed with her notebook open and her wand tucked behind one ear in a gesture so utilitarian it should not have been as distracting as it was.

The expected compartment was empty. So was the one two cases down. Draco crouched to check the lower panel. Nothing.

"They may be in the archive room Lucius used for seizure inventories," he said.

"You sound as if you would rather set it on fire than go and see."

"I'd rather let it rot on principle."

Hermione leaned against the shelf, not looking at him while she wrote. "Fine. Then before we go and indulge your principles, I want the east passage structural survey, anything Cassiopeia attached to it, and any notes that connect the Mark to room conditions."

For one unimpressed second, nothing happened.

Then a plain mechanical click came from below his knee.

He froze. Hermione did not move either. Another click followed, deeper in the wood. A narrow seam, invisible a moment before, opened in the carved skirting beneath the genealogical shelves. A shallow drawer pushed itself outward by three inches and stopped.

Neither of them spoke.

Draco reached first. The drawer edge bit his knuckles before Hermione had even shifted. The drawer ran further than the visible shelf suggested. Inside lay three rolled surveys bound in faded green tape, a calfskin notebook stamped only with C.B.M. in blind gold, and a packet of parchment forms ruled with the outline of an arm from wrist to shoulder.

Hermione let out a slow breath through her nose. "Well."

Each survey roll carried an old tag in family shorthand. Draco read them off one by one. "'East passage outer wall.' 'Winter buffer adjustment.' 'North draft line.'"

"The corridor," Hermione said.

He lifted the notebook. The leather was dry and warm, as if it had been sitting in a pocket near skin. When he opened it, Cassiopeia's hand looked back at him immediately: strong, narrow, and ruthless about space. The first page contained no introduction, no title, only a date, a room name, and columns laid out with the severe faith that pain became governable if one recorded it properly enough.

Hour. Flare interval. Room occupied. Wall condition. Patient response. Modification.

The next pages did not improve in mood. Cassiopeia never once named the patient. She reduced them to initials and symptoms, as if privacy or cruelty required the same economy. One entry noted a night without sleep and three successive flares in the east study. Another marked relocation to the lower library and improvement held for two hours. A third had only one line in the margin: house over-attentive today; reduced corridor pressure before noon.

Hermione leaned over his shoulder to read, fast enough that her breath moved the paper. "She tracked response to rooms before she tracked potions."

"That sounds like something my family would call efficient."

"Your family should have tried not being appalling for variety."

The line was brisk enough to count as work rather than insult. Draco let it pass because he had no defense worth the effort.

"Who was she treating?" Hermione asked.

"No idea."

"There must have been a name."

"Not if there was shame attached." He turned another page. "Malfoys record scandal either in obscene detail or not at all. It depends which version flatters them more."

Hermione made a quiet dissatisfied sound. "I hate dead people who assume later readers can read their minds."

"You hate everyone who leaves poor notes."

"Yes."

She said it with such offended certainty that he almost smiled. The expression failed halfway but did not humiliate him by being seen.

Hermione reached for the ruled packet and spread the top form flat on the table. "If the Mark is bad now, we can test whether anything in her notes matches what you're feeling. Ten minutes. Fifteen."

No clean refusal presented itself. Pain made honesty easier and pride more foolish.

"All right," he said.

Hermione cleared a space at the long table with brisk competence. The ruled forms fit the page size precisely, as if the drawer had been stocked for the use of that table and no other. She set the notebook open to Cassiopeia's columns, uncapped her ink, and looked at him with professional attention that would have been easier to bear if he did not know how physical professionalism could feel in her hands.

"Sit," she said.

He sat.

"Tell me when the flare peaked last."

"About an hour ago."

"Before or after I knocked?"

"Before."

Her quill moved. "Location."

"Forearm. Up the inside. Sometimes shoulder."

"Heat or pressure?"

"Both."

Hermione glanced up. "That's not terribly useful."

"It's what it is."

"Fine. Worse with movement?"

"Sometimes. Closing the hand. Turning the wrist."

"Show me."

He did both before he could stop himself, testing against pain as stupidly as a child testing a bruise. The ache answered at once. He drew breath through his teeth.

Hermione's eyes flicked to his hand. "That one hurt."

"Yes."

She set the quill down and put two fingers against the inside of his wrist, just below the place where pulse ran strongest. Clinical. Steady. He ought to have been used to it after the first examination. He was not used to it. He was only better at looking as if he were.

The change came at once. Not relief exactly. Relief suggested generosity. This was more exact than that. The pain widened, lost its sharp braided edge, became something he could think alongside instead of around.

Hermione felt it happen, or perhaps only felt the pulse settle under her fingers. "Again," she said.

He turned the wrist a fraction. The ache answered, but duller.

"Lower," she murmured, already writing. "Not gone."

"No."

"Worse in the study?"

He looked at her.

"I am allowed pattern recognition, Malfoy."

"Yes. Worse there."

She wrote that down too. "Sleep?"

"Poorly."

"How poor."

"Three hours, perhaps four in pieces."

"Nausea."

"Some."

"Spell use this morning."

"Minimal."

"Because it hurt or because you had nothing worth casting at?"

"Both."

That earned him the briefest glance, almost approval and not quite. Hermione shifted her grip. Her thumb came down just above the old brand and the pain stepped back again like an animal acknowledging rank.

She felt that too. This time she did not apologise. "There."

"Yes."

"Specific enough to map?"

"Yes."

"Good."

The word came clipped, not unkind. She leaned over the chart and marked small shaded bands up the outline of the arm. When she spoke again, the energy had turned from bedside precision to problem-solving speed.

"Cassiopeia's notes track room conditions across flare intervals. Look." She slid the notebook toward him without letting go of his wrist. "This entry. The flare worsened in a west room with poor wall retention, eased in the library, and changed again with direct contact. She doesn't say contact with whom. I hate that she doesn't say."

"Malfoys assume later readers can infer the obvious."

"The obvious to them is usually buried under three layers of pure-blood nonsense."

"Yes."

She blinked at the easy agreement, then went back to the page. "And here. Same hand, different week. She notes that the house began surfacing relevant records before symptoms stabilized. She doesn't describe that as unusual."

"Relevant to what?"

"To the condition being treated." Hermione met his eyes squarely. "It isn't reacting to me because I'm a guest. It's moving work toward whatever it thinks the work is."

The phrasing hit somewhere tender and unhelpful. Draco pulled his hand back too fast, out of reflex more than decision. Pain answered the movement with a quick bright hook under the skin.

Hermione noticed that too, because of course she did. "Sorry. I wasn't trying to make a speech out of it."

"You didn't."

"I did a bit."

"Only a bit."

Some small tension in her mouth eased. She gathered the papers into a neater pile, then stopped and let one hand rest flat on Cassiopeia's notebook as if anchoring herself back into the intellectual frame.

"Look at the selection pattern," she said. "It isn't offering random comforts. It brought the repair ledgers, the survey, these charts. It didn't bring anything ornamental. It didn't bring anything merely convenient. It keeps serving the same line of inquiry."

Draco looked down at the page again. Hour. Flare interval. Room occupied. Wall condition. Patient response. Modification. The columns made his skin crawl because they were too tidy and because they were useful.

"It is assembling evidence," Hermione said.

"For what?"

She gave a one-shouldered movement that admitted uncertainty without softening it. "For itself. For the Mark. For whatever intersection it thinks matters. I don't know yet." She shut the notebook more gently than he would have. "But I don't think the house is trying to make me comfortable. I think it's building a workspace."

Draco's fingers tightened on the table edge. He made them let go.

Hermione read enough of that from his face to add, not gently, "I know. I don't like that answer either."

Silence held for a moment. Not empty silence. Working silence, full of page edges and old leather and the sound of them both refusing easier interpretations.

At last she said, "Did your mother know what Cassiopeia was doing?"

"If she does, she hasn't said."

"That isn't the same as not knowing."

"No."

Hermione tapped the closed notebook once with the back of her fingernail. "Then ask her."

The order in it was light enough to pass for practicality. The fact that he heard it as an order anyway did not improve his temper.

"My mother does not become more informative when summoned."

"Perhaps not." Hermione slid the body chart to one side and began copying the margin notation at speed. "But if this house is altering walls and handing us diagnostic papers, I'd prefer not to be the last person in it to realise why."

Her tone made the statement about method rather than fear. The fear was there anyway, thin and well hidden. Draco saw it because he knew what room in the house she had not yet spoken of. He said, more abruptly than he intended, "You're not."

Hermione's quill slowed. "Not what?"

"Last to realise." He did not know how to say the rest without saying too much. The Manor is answering you. The Manor is choosing around you. The Manor notices your work because it notices you inside it, and I cannot tell whether that is safer or worse. What came out instead was, "I noticed before you did."

She studied him for a beat, perhaps deciding whether to press. Then she chose mercy or tact or simple concentration and returned to the notes. "All right."

He stood because sitting still had become impossible.

"Where are you going?" she asked.

"To find lunch."

"You don't have to."

"I know."

The service stairs to the old kitchen were colder than the library and blessedly ordinary. Draco cut bread, carved cheese, and found himself angrier with each useful movement, which at least made the activity productive. By the time he brought the tray back, Hermione had filled two pages of notes and forgotten entirely that bodies required feeding unless directly reminded.

He set the tray down within her line of sight.

She looked up as if surfacing. "Oh."

"An eloquent response."

"I was not aware I was giving one." She pulled the plate toward herself with absent gratitude and immediately kept reading while she took the first bite. "Thank you."

It was an ordinary phrase. It still landed oddly. He sat opposite with his own plate and the east-passage survey unrolled between them.

The structural drawing was less a plan than an argument with previous repairs. Old lines had been redrawn over newer ones, wall-thickness notes corrected in three different hands, one narrow channel through the east passage marked as buffer in Cassiopeia's sharper script.

Hermione swallowed and pointed with the crust of bread. "There."

"Try not to annotate the survey with mustard."

She ignored that. "This line. It sits exactly where you said the draught used to be."

"It has been there all my life."

"Not now."

"No."

She frowned at the page. "If this was a buffer line and the house has sealed it, that isn't cosmetic. That's structural adjustment."

"Helpful distinction."

"I mean it isn't fluff. It isn't trying to stop me being chilly." Hermione took another bite because if she did not, she would plainly forget again. "It's changing the building around the work."

Draco said nothing.

Hermione glanced up. "What."

"Nothing."

"That was visibly not nothing."

He considered lying. It felt wasteful in the face of her expression. "I had almost the same thought five minutes ago, and I resent you for getting there independently."

To his annoyance, she laughed. It was quick and low and gone at once. "That sounds difficult for you."

"It is a trial."

"You should be brave about it."

The exchange was stupid enough to feel dangerously close to ease. Draco returned his attention to the survey.

Hermione's voice, when she spoke again, had gone quieter. "Predictability would have been enough."

The line came out so matter-of-factly that it took a second to hear the pressure inside it. She did not look up from the page. Her left sleeve had ridden back half an inch when she reached for the notebook, and the glamour on her forearm held cleanly where it always held. He stared at the fabric, not the arm, and saw anyway a marble floor, a knife, Bellatrix's bright avid mouth.

He made himself look back to the survey before the memory finished arranging itself.

"Will you ask her?" Hermione said after a moment.

"Yes."

The answer surprised both of them. It showed in the brief lift of her brows and in the private irritation that followed in him, because now he actually had to do it.

Hermione only nodded and went back to copying Cassiopeia's notes into a clearer hand. "Good."

By the time the plates were empty, Draco had sent word to Narcissa and regretted it twice. The library table held the notebook, the east-passage survey, and three blank body charts squared to the table edge.

---

Narcissa arrived at half three by Floo, stepping out of the parlour hearth with the contained irritation of a woman who expected a failing network to work properly out of respect for her inconvenience. She gave the room one glance, brushed soot from her sleeve, and looked at him with cool appraisal.

"Your message was unhelpful."

"It was brief."

"That is what made it unhelpful."

Before he could answer, her hand went to the mantel. She closed her eyes for one beat, not theatrically, simply listening through her palm. When she opened them again, something in her face had flattened into attention.

"The fire is running too high," she said.

"I hadn't noticed."

"No." Her fingers remained on the stone another second. "You would not."

Tea was implied. While the kettle heated, Draco told her as much as he could without sounding deranged: books surfaced from closed stacks, Cassiopeia's manual appearing on Hermione's table, a hidden drawer under the genealogical shelves, old body charts that matched the Mark too neatly to be coincidence.

At the notebook, Narcissa went very still.

"You found Cassiopeia's papers."

"The house found them."

Her gaze sharpened, not on him but through him, as though the distinction mattered in a way he had not yet earned. "Have you read them?"

"Some."

"Has Ms. Granger?"

"Enough to know Cassiopeia was tracking the Mark against room changes."

Narcissa took her cup from him and set it down untasted. "You should have started there."

"I started where you might answer."

A less disciplined woman might have smiled. Narcissa only said, "Show me the rest."

He took her first to the library. The hidden drawer beneath the genealogical shelves stood closed again, its seam gone so completely that he would have doubted it had ever moved if the notebook and survey had not been sitting on the table in plain view. Narcissa did not waste time touching the wood. Her attention went straight to the papers.

For several long moments she read Cassiopeia's hand without comment. The silence acquired weight. Draco watched his mother's face the way one watched the sky over difficult weather, not expecting honesty but hoping for legible warning.

At last she closed the notebook.

"I had hoped those were lost," she said.

That was more than he had expected and less than he wanted. "Why?"

Narcissa laid the notebook down precisely on the table edge. "Because recovery is not the same thing as usefulness."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the one you have."

He could have pushed then. The familiar failure of that impulse, repeated over twenty-five years, was that it almost never improved her generosity. So he let the question sit and watched the place where she had set her hand on the leather. Her fingers had tightened only once: at a page he had not yet translated.

"Did the pain change while you were working with her?" Narcissa asked.

He looked up sharply. "That is a very particular question."

"And one you can answer."

He thought of Hermione's fingers at his wrist, the sharp braid of pain loosening under her touch, the lesser improvement that had come simply from being in the library with her while she worked. "Yes," he said.

"By contact or proximity?"

He was quiet long enough for the answer to become its own confession. "Both, I think."

Whatever Narcissa had expected, the confirmation did not surprise her. That was the worst part.

"Show me the corridors," she said.

The route from the library to the east wing felt wrong in a way Draco could not have named until he watched Narcissa move through it. She counted turns with her body, not her eyes, slight pauses registering absences he could not feel until she made them visible. Outside the old morning room she stopped and looked back toward the library.

"It has redrawn the working routes," she said.

"The what."

"The routes that matter to the work being done." She resumed walking before he could do more than absorb the phrasing. "Guest wing to library. Your study to library. This room to both. It is wasting less distance."

"You say that as if the house has a filing system."

"It has habits." Her voice stayed even. "The passage from the morning room to the library is gone. I used it last winter."

They turned toward the library again. The new door appeared just past the bend.

Dark wood stood where blank stone had been when they had passed ten minutes earlier. Brass handled the late light with a subdued old shine. The grain ran deep into the wall itself, not fitted there but grown. Narcissa stopped so abruptly that Draco nearly walked into her.

Recognition crossed her face before composure sealed over it.

"Mother."

"Leave it."

The words came level, but colour had gone from her fingertips where they pressed the stone.

"That door wasn't there a minute ago."

"No."

"You know it."

"Not that door." Her gaze stayed fixed on the handle. "The habit."

He waited. Narcissa did not like being stared into speech. Sometimes, very rarely, that was enough to make her continue just to end the waiting on her own terms.

"After my wedding," she said, "sealed rooms began presenting themselves. Not often. Not dramatically. A music room off a corridor that did not lead there. A winter sitting room with windows onto grounds the wall did not face. Your grandmother called it welcome. I was young enough to find that flattering."

"And later?"

"Later your father decided the house should make itself intelligible." Her mouth sharpened around the word should. "He had hinges measured. Locks catalogued. He turned a notebook on it the way he turned one on everything he meant to own. The rooms stopped presenting themselves for weeks at a time."

Draco kept his eyes on the brass handle. "So it dislikes force."

"Everything dislikes force. The house is only better positioned than most things to object."

That was near enough to dry humour to feel almost intimate. It vanished at once.

"Listen carefully," Narcissa said. "When this house is pressed, it does not yield. It withdraws something else."

"What does it withdraw?"

Her silence was immediate and deliberate. Then, because the door remained there and because some old calculation had altered behind her eyes, she chose a narrower truth.

"Convenience first. Access after that. Entire rooms, if it is in the mood." She did not take her hand off the wall. "Once, when Lucius tried to force an archive room that would not open for him, the west stair gave him a plaster wall for six days. He called it a warding fault. Your grandmother did not correct him."

The image was ridiculous enough that Draco nearly smiled. It died before completion. "Was it like this for him, then?"

Narcissa looked at him at last. "Your father mistook access for victory."

It was an answer and not one. He heard the missing territory around it as clearly as if she had named it.

"And you didn't."

"I learned faster."

"From what."

"From living here." Her voice had gone thinner. "The rooms I mentioned. They came back once Lucius stopped measuring them. I only needed that to happen once."

He said, more softly, "What is it doing with Granger?"

Narcissa's gaze returned to the door. "If I knew that fully, I would already be telling you to send her away or to keep her here. I am doing neither." She drew one careful breath. "I think it means to involve her in work it considers unfinished. That is not the same thing as kindness."

Cassiopeia's notebook flashed back through his mind. Hour. Flare interval. Room occupied. Wall condition.

"The Mark," he said.

"Possibly." Narcissa's hand came away from the wall. "Possibly both. Cassiopeia did not separate them in her notes."

Before he could ask the next question, she said, "Do not put your hand on that handle."

The instruction came too quickly, which made disobedience feel already anticipated. "You knew I would."

"I know you were raised by a man who mistook force for competence." The sentence stayed cool, but the effort of coolness showed in it. "Do not prove him right out of habit."

He wanted to argue on principle. The desire lasted only until he looked at her hand. Her fingers had not quite stopped trembling.

Narcissa resumed walking. This time he followed without pretending it was not obedience.

Near the guest wing corridor she slowed again.

"Is she comfortable here?" Narcissa asked.

"She's working."

"That is not what I asked."

The answer arrived in pieces he did not want. Hermione at the long table as if the room had admitted her before he had. Hermione eating bread over Cassiopeia's notebook because she would not have stopped otherwise. Hermione saying predictability would have been enough in a tone so flat it had cut cleaner than complaint. Under those newer impressions sat the older one he kept circling and never quite touching: the drawing room floor, marble and blood, Bellatrix's knife flashing wet under chandelier light, Hermione on her side with hair stuck to her cheek and the carved word still fresh enough not to be a scar because it was still an open wound. He had stood there with a wand in his hand and done nothing that altered anything.

"As comfortable as anyone could be," he said, and then, because leaving the sentence there felt like cowardice even by his standards, "given."

Narcissa heard the unfinished part without help. Her face did not change, but her eyes did, some inward movement of grief or guilt or merely recognition that passed too fast to name.

"Yes," she said quietly. "Given."

They walked the rest of the corridor without speaking. At the parlour door Narcissa stopped once more and turned to him with the full weight of her attention finally set where he could feel it.

"The Manor does not act out of spite," she said. "That should not tempt you to trust its intentions."

"I don't."

"See that you continue not to."

He fetched her cloak from the stand by the hearth. She fastened it with her mother's silver clasp and looked at him not as son or child or disappointment but as something closer to an equal than she usually permitted, which was almost worse.

"If another door appears," she said, "send for me. Do not test it. Do not mention it to Ms. Granger unless it opens to her first."

Then green fire took her.

The parlour seemed flatter without her in it. Less informed. Draco stood staring at the grate until the last of the Floo flare went out, then swore once under his breath at nothing useful and went to the corridor anyway.

The new door remained where it had been. Varnish and brass met his hand. A faint dry scent of cedar lingered around the jamb. Narcissa's warning sat in his head and did not improve his judgment.

After a moment's stillness his hand closed on the handle.

Resistance came at once, not the catch of a lock but the unmistakable pressure of another hand closing over the handle from the other side.

Draco let go so fast his knuckles knocked the brass. The corridor stayed still. No footstep, no latch, nothing. Under his shoes, stone answered stone in a run of movement so low it might have been imagined if imagination had ever been that inventive in his favour.

The library proved he had not imagined it.

Both oak doors were shut. Since Hermione's second day the room had not closed on her while she worked, not even once. Now the handles refused him completely. There was no give, no rattle, nothing. Through the wood came the scratch of her quill, one scrape of chair legs, and beneath both sounds the low hum threaded through the house like breath.

He left his hand on the handle and did not knock.

Inside, a page turned.

He could picture the exact motion without wanting to: Hermione bent over Cassiopeia's notebook, quill in her left hand, wand somewhere near her elbow, the east-passage survey spread under one palm.

At last he stepped back.

The Manor kept the room from him with quiet certainty. Somewhere beyond the oak, Granger shifted in her chair and went on with the work. Draco looked at the pale crescent the brass had pressed into his palm until it faded.


# Chapter 5

The work had narrowed to a bottleneck with one Latin verb at its center. If they got the verb wrong, every note she'd made about the decay so far leaned the wrong way: toward attrition instead of damage, toward a tired system winding down instead of a damaged one bleeding out through its seams.

On her eleventh morning at the Manor, grey light hit the east-facing window the house had angled toward dawn. Tea waited on the side table in the usual silver pot. After the usual morning sequence, Hermione dressed, gathered yesterday's notes, and took the short walk to the library. Today the Manor kept it to twenty-three paces.

Settling in was still not the word for any of it. Her job was to use the archive, examine the Mark, and go back to London when both were done. The fact that the house had learned where she liked the light did not alter the arrangement. It only made the arrangement harder to keep impersonal.

Most mornings Malfoy was there first. The childish irritation that had caused in the first week had worn down into a steadier fact: he was where the work was. This morning he stood at the east table with one family journal open, another stacked on top of it, sleeves rolled to the forearm on both sides as if he'd already been at it an hour.

"You were right about the 1712 tract," he said without preamble when she came through the door. "It's the same term in the maintenance rolls. Not similar. The same."

Hermione set her notebook down. "Which term?"

He turned the book toward her and tapped a line written in a cramped, mean seventeenth-century hand.

*Deperdere.*

The margin note beside it was worse. Malfoy family shorthand layered over Latin, half code and half private arrogance. Hermione bent over the page. Under the notation sat a short heading she recognized from the previous afternoon's work: *Ad Tethyn Laboris.*

"No," she said after a moment. "That can't be right."

"Excellent. We're in agreement already."

She ignored that. "If this is attached to the Tethys Working, it can't mean ordinary loss. That procedure only appears in emergency maintenance contexts."

"Yes."

"Then why did you tell me yesterday it might still be standard dissipation?"

"Because yesterday you were treating every family usage as self-mythologizing nonsense and I wanted you to slow down enough to read the line."

Her head came up. "I was not doing that."

One eyebrow lifted a fraction. "You were doing it elegantly."

She opened her mouth, shut it, and reached for the parallel text she'd left flagged overnight. By the time she'd found the place, he had already moved a second volume beside the first.

The argument started there and refused to stop.

*Deperdere*, in standard Latin, could serve for loss, ruin, wasting, destruction. In the Malfoy records the word had narrowed. Not poetically. Functionally. One set of texts used it for ambient decline, another for pressure escaping damaged infrastructure, another for what happened when a working that should have held in one place started leaking into the surrounding field.

"It's not a synonym for *amittere*," Malfoy said, one fingertip resting on the line. "My grandfather's journals make the distinction cleanly. *Amittere* is misplacement, forfeiture, natural expenditure. *Deperdere* is what they wrote when a structure failed and magic went where it wasn't meant to."

Her own source, an anonymous 1790s theoretical treatise in standard Latin, resisted him and supported him at once. "The distinction only holds if the original builders imagined the field as a bounded system."

"They did."

"Not universally." She found the place she wanted and pushed the book across. "Here. *Fundamentum magiae est fluvialis, non lacustris.* The foundation of magic is riverine, not lacustrine. Flow, not reservoir. If the field is movement rather than storage, then structural damage doesn't create leakage from a sealed vessel. It changes the course of an existing current."

He read it, jaw set in concentration rather than resistance. Hermione had started recognizing the difference. Resistance sat higher in him. Concentration narrowed him.

"You're making the same point differently," he said at last. "If a riverbank breaks, the water still goes where it shouldn't. That's the family usage. Not loss in the abstract. Structural hemorrhage."

The word landed harder than she wanted because it was precise, not dramatic.

"Hemorrhage implies active pressure," she said.

"Yes."

"Which means the recent failures aren't the result of gradual depletion."

"Yes."

She looked back down at the page. If he was right, then the Floo failures and concealment slippages and household charm collapses she'd been tracking were not symptoms of a nation slowly running out of power. They were breaches. War damage in old infrastructure kept under strain until the strain found a way out. That model was worse. It was also cleaner.

The quiet that followed lasted just long enough to become useful.

Hermione crossed to the adjacent stack and pulled down the 1712 text again. Malfoy moved aside without looking as she leaned over the table, which meant he had started anticipating her body in space. That fact had become hard to ignore lately. Not alarming exactly. Not harmless either.

"If the Tethys Working is an emergency rerouting procedure," she said, flipping pages until the brittle paper threatened to protest, "then this line might not describe the damage. It might describe the purpose of the working. Not stop the loss. Disperse the pressure before a damaged anchor shears."

"Read it."

She did, first in Latin and then in English because thinking aloud made the syntax visible. "'When the knot refuses binding and the channel takes heat beyond its measure, the field must be...' bloody hell."

"Yes."

"Not restored. Divided."

His mouth bent, not into a smile exactly, but into the shape he made when she arrived where he'd been waiting. "Divided irretrievably," he said. "Deliberately."

Hermione read the line again. Then a third time, slower. The ugly logic of it clarified under repetition. The Tethys Working had not been written to heal damaged infrastructure. It was triage. Bleed off excess pressure. Preserve the surrounding system. Sacrifice local integrity to save the larger network.

"If that's right," she said, "the people who built this expected anchor damage to cascade."

"Of course they did. They weren't idiots."

"No. But they were optimists in other places. Half the repair language assumes competent stewardship over centuries."

"There was your mistake."

She almost said *your family wrote half this* and didn't. It wasn't the point, and he had already done his share of not turning every disagreement into inherited guilt.

Instead she said, "If this model holds, the Ministry's current numbers are worse than useless. They're averaging national instability as if it were fluctuation. If breaches are regional and structural, the average hides the failures that matter."

"Can you prove that from what we have?"

"Maybe not prove. Support hard enough to make Croaker take it seriously."

"That wasn't the question."

It irritated her that he had learned where to press. It irritated her more that he was right to press there.

"Not yet," she said. "But if the maintenance rolls and the theoretical texts converge, then yes, I think I can."

He nodded once and reached for a fresh sheet of parchment. "Then we stop arguing over whether the term matters and start mapping where it appears."

"We've only been arguing because it matters."

"You've noticed."

The next two hours stripped the room of anything except work.

They built a columned list across three sheets: family usage, formal usage, context, implied mechanism, probable modern symptom. Hermione translated and categorized. Malfoy read the shorthand she still could not look at without wanting to set fire to several generations of dead men. The work between them had found a rhythm that was not effortless and not easy. It was better than either of those. It was exact. She would pull a term apart until only one defensible meaning remained. He would catch the place where an apparent contradiction was really a house-specific variation or a family assumption disguised as universal law. Three times she told him he was overgeneralizing from Malfoy sources. Twice he told her she was flattening usage that had technical consequences. Both times they were both partly right.

At one point she reached past him for the maintenance roll and he shifted the parchment toward her before her fingers got there.

"You anticipated that," she said without thinking.

"You were leaning."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the only one you're getting."

The room held the line between them: not quite banter, not quite avoidance. Her pulse did an irritating little step anyway.

By noon the table had vanished under evidence. Books, maintenance rolls, family notebooks, her translation sheets, his smaller cleaner annotations. Dust moved through the high light in slow gold bands. The library smelled of paper, mineral preservative, and the tea he kept forgetting to drink while it was still hot.

On a fourth pass through the 1643 journal, Hermione stopped with her finger braced against the margin.

"Here," she said. "This isn't just family shorthand. It's a distinction between field attrition and anchor rupture."

Malfoy came around the table instead of making her pass the journal across. He bent beside her shoulder, close enough that she caught cold air and starch and the faint bitter trace of black tea. Too close to pretend not to notice. Not close enough to justify reacting.

His hand landed just above the line, pale against the yellowed page. "Read the sentence before."

She did. Then the one after.

There. Not ambiguous this time. Not even written well. The writer had been documenting a damaged site and, halfway through the entry, had stopped generalizing and started naming parts.

"Fine," she said. "You're right about the word."

"I know."
