She looked at him properly then, at whatever the night had done to his face. "After lunch. The usual setup."

"Fine."

Some small check passed behind her eyes. Once the coat came off, she crossed to the separate table she had claimed earlier in the week and unpacked her satchel with precise, indifferent efficiency. Quill. Ink. Notes. By the time he looked back down at his text, the conversation had already been dismissed.

---

After lunch they used the worktable by the tall windows because the light there was best, even now, even with the winter afternoon already thinning at the edges. The instruments went down in their familiar order: wand, diagnostic crystals tuned to the Mark, the probe resonator she had built herself out of Ministry salvage and a piece of old Malfoy silver, a clean sheaf of parchment for notes. Hermione unrolled the protective cloth around the crystals and checked each one against the light before setting it down. One had a hairline crack through the base from the last series. She turned it between finger and thumb, frowned, and set it aside for the newer one.

"How many flares since Monday evening?"

He took the folded catalogue sheet from his pocket and passed it across. The margin was inked over with times and one or two useless annotations written when the pain had been bad enough to make his hand clumsy.

Her eyes skimmed. "You wrote these down as they happened."

"That was the idea."

"This one says *hand went numb for maybe a minute*."

"That was also the idea."

A faint movement at one corner of her mouth suggested contempt for his phrasing rather than amusement. He took what he could get.

"When did it start radiating past the forearm?"

"Yesterday afternoon."

"Shoulder too?"

"After midnight."

"Headache? Nausea? Any hearing distortion?"

He looked at her. "You think it's getting ambitious."

"I think dead command channels do strange things when they're trying to reconstitute themselves." She laid the sheet beside the quill. "And you look as if you haven't slept."

"I slept some."

"Don't bother lying while I'm taking notes."

Her sleeves were already half rolled. The right stopped neatly below the elbow. The left she managed differently, leaving the cuff lower and arranging one extra fold with more care than the task required. He had noticed the asymmetry before without understanding it. Now he only registered that he was noticing it again.

"Sleeve," she said.

His own left sleeve went back. The Mark looked vicious today, skull and snake cut sharp under inflamed skin. The skin from wrist to inner elbow had taken on the glossy, stretched look it got before a bad flare. She would see the change at once. She did. Her mouth set. She did not comment.

Cold fingers closed around his wrist. Three days without contact had made even that baseline touch a jolt. He kept his face still and failed at the shoulders.

"Your hands are cold," he said.

"The library's cold."

"It is."

Not an argument. Only data. Her thumb settled over his pulse, feeling for something more useful than his answer. Outside the windows the grounds had gone iron-grey under low cloud, hedge lines black with frost. The daylight already looked tired.

"Standard series first," she said. "Then I want to run the deeper mapping again. I've been through last week's data and the secondary decay pattern isn't stable enough to call noise."

"What is it, then."

"I don't know yet."

That was as much uncertainty as Hermione Granger ever put in her voice on purpose. It sharpened the room.

Blue light netted over the Mark. The first diagnostic always felt thin, almost polite, a surface pass over the curse's visible architecture. The crystals took the charge and returned it as layered colour: cobalt for active curse structure, white-fire at the joints where old compulsion channels still tried to remember themselves, a murkier red along the edges when the skin was inflamed enough to distort the readings. She moved the wand slowly, reading off numbers under her breath while the self-scribing quill kept pace on the parchment.

"Stabilization index down another four points," she murmured. "Surface reactivity high. Channel irritation obvious. That's expected."

"Expected is comforting."

"No, it isn't." She didn't look up. "It only means I hate surprises more than I hate bad news."

The curse surged once against the charm and then went obediently still under her hand. Relief came so fast his fingers uncurled on the tabletop before he could stop them. He had not realised how hard he had been bracing until the absence of bracing became possible.

Her eyes flicked from the parchment to his hand. "The pain's been worse."

"Manageable."

"Your definition of manageable is not medically serious."

"How tragic for medicine."

"Malfoy."

There it was, the version of his name that was mostly warning and partly fatigue. He let the deflection go.

"It started flaring harder after dinner yesterday," he said. "And again after midnight."

She nodded once, already inside the second sequence. This one required the resonator. She set the silver frame over the inside of his forearm, adjusted the crystals into the notches around it, and spoke the activation charm at a lower pitch than usual. The device answered with a live hum against the wood of the table. Pale structure lifted out of the Mark in translucent layers, rotating above his skin like something architectural rather than bodily, a small obscene model of damage.

"Don't move."

"I wasn't planning to."

"I'm serious. The deeper pass is unstable today."

"That's reassuring."

"It isn't meant to be."

One hand held his wrist steady. The other guided the wand through the rotating planes, marking intersections, following old magic down into older damage. The setup pulled her closer than the first series had. Her left forearm rested along the table near his hand while she angled the wand with her right. Lavender preservation soap clung faintly to her skin under the mineral dust of the library. The scent had got into the Manor with her and then into the linens, the towels, the air after she left a room. Now, after three days of distance, the ordinary fact of it felt almost invasive.

"The lower channels are inflamed all the way to the elbow," she said, more to the parchment than to him. "You've got sympathetic response along the old coercion lattice. Hold still."

"I am holding still."

"Your pulse is arguing."

He looked down at the place where her thumb pinned it. "You should have a better patient."

"I have the patient I've got."

The quill scratched faster. Sweat gathered at her temples as she pushed deeper than usual, mapping not only Voldemort's brand but whatever lay under it now: the eight months of reactivation, the weeks of resonance, the house's interference or assistance or whatever name one gave to a sentient building behaving like weather around two people. She said numbers. She said *again*. She said *that wasn't there on Monday*. Each phrase fell flat and technical on the air, but the hand on his arm betrayed her. The pressure kept changing by degrees.

"Was it worse in the guest wing corridor?" she asked suddenly.

He went still for reasons unrelated to the warning she'd just given him. "What."

She lifted the catalogue sheet with her wand hand and tapped the line he had written in the margin near the worst flare. *past guest wing. bad one.*

"I assume this means what it looks like it means."

His mouth almost did the old family thing and turned the truth into something elegant and uninformative. He was too tired for elegance.

"I was walking," he said.

"At half one in the morning."

"Obviously not as a hobby."

Her thumb shifted once on his wrist. Not quite approval. Not quite censure.

"You shouldn't let it build to that point before telling me."

"You didn't seem interested in my company this week."

The instant the words were out, he wanted them back. Not because they were false. Because they were too close to the bone to survive the room intact.

Her wand paused midair. The resonator whined as the charm lost its smooth path and then found it again.

"I'm interested in the Mark not tearing half the channels in your arm open," she said. Each word clean. "Whatever else this week was."

There were a dozen different bad ways to answer that. He took the least bad and said nothing.

The work made a scaffold they could sit inside. Within it they were examiner and subject again, researcher and problem, two capable people occupied by a technical difficulty. Her hand on his arm, his pulse under her thumb, the relief moving through the Mark when she touched it: all of that could pass as apparatus if he kept his thinking narrow enough.

For a few minutes he managed it.

Then she shifted the resonator lower. The motion pulled her left sleeve back a fraction. He saw the cuff catch, not on fabric, but on resistance. A shimmer ran over the skin above her forearm, slight as heat over stone. Hermione swore under her breath and corrected the angle, but the strain had already started to show. The glamour did not fail all at once. It thinned.

At first he thought it was only the light on the crystals. Then the even brown tone of her skin went soft around one section and refused to settle. The surface blurred, re-formed, blurred again. Beneath it something paler pressed up with ugly patience.

"Hermione."

She didn't answer. She was staring at the resonator, forcing the spell through one more pass as if she could outwork the problem before it became visible.

The shimmer travelled. What lay underneath resolved by degrees, like lettering under rubbed ash.

The letters had been carved, not cut. Bellatrix had taken her time. The scar tissue lay raised and silver-white against Hermione's skin, edges ragged where the cursed blade had torn instead of slicing cleanly. The word had been made to last.

MUDBLOOD.

Five letters and five more, spanning the inside of her left forearm. The opening strokes bit deepest. The M still carried the hardest pressure, the blade driven down into skin pinned flat against marble. The U dragged a fraction at the lower curve where Hermione had jerked. The final D ran shallower. By then Bellatrix had been laughing, the arm slick, the knife slipping. He knew that sequence because he had watched it happen from twelve feet away and failed to move.

The drawing room came back with the clarity of punishment. The chandelier throwing too much light onto the marble. Bellatrix's skirt dragging through blood. Hermione on the floor with her hair half out of its tie, one hand trapped awkwardly beneath her because Bellatrix had wanted her still and got it. Lucius at the edge of the room, white and vicious and unwilling to interfere with his own sister-in-law's entertainment. The knife flashing when it turned. The smell, metallic and hot, fighting with polish and old smoke. Hermione making a sound through her teeth because screaming would have given Bellatrix pleasure and she had known it even then. Draco against the wall with every survival instinct in his body pointed inward, pretending stillness was not participation.

Before thought caught up, his hand left the table and landed over the scar.

Warm. Raised. Real.

Hermione flinched hard enough to break every spell she was holding. The mapping collapsed into sparks. The resonator screamed once and went dark. One crystal rolled in a bright blue spin across the tabletop and struck the wooden lip. The quill scored one wild line through the parchment and stopped. Her whole body had drawn inward around the exposure, and her forearm went rigid under his palm.

"Granger."

Her gaze stayed fixed on his hand.

"Granger, I-"

"Don't." The word came flat and over-controlled. "Don't say you're sorry."

His fingers started to lift. She caught his wrist before he could move them far. Her grip was hard enough to bruise.

"I wasn't," he said. It was not clear whether that was true.

"No." Her breathing had shortened, then corrected. "No, you were."

The urge to say *I didn't know* rose uselessly to his mouth. He had already failed that line once. Knowledge was not the point. The letters were under his hand whether he had expected them or not.

He expected her to wrench away. Instead she set the wand down with deliberate care, caught the cuff of her left sleeve in her right hand, and dragged it higher. The fabric snagged against the scar tissue for a second before clearing it. She pulled until the last letters stood fully exposed from wrist nearly to elbow. Then she turned the arm completely upward on the table and left it there between them, pulse hammering under the thinnest part of the skin.

"Don't do it halfway," she said.

There was anger in it. Not loud. Harder than loud. He understood then that seeing it by accident and pretending he hadn't would be its own kind of pity. A quick retreat. A moral reflex disguised as courtesy.

Only then did he move his thumb. Slowly, across the first groove of the M.

The scar tissue had its own map under his skin, dense and ridged, healed only in the technical sense that the wound had stopped bleeding. Some parts were smooth with bad magic's overcorrection. Others held tiny catches where the blade had torn going in or out. He traced the U. The D. Under his thumb the letters felt like a record no spell could revise.

Her breathing changed. It did not quicken. It turned deliberate, one measured inhale after another as if she had decided that control could still be had in this room if she chose the units carefully enough.

He read by touch as the light went down. B. L. O. O. D.

At the final letter he stopped, not because the word was finished but because his hand had started to shake. He had not noticed it until the tremor transferred into her skin.

"You can stop," she said.

He looked up. Her face gave him nothing easy. No permission. No accusation. The same expression she wore over a difficult text, stripped of every social buffer.

"I don't want to," he said.

The truth of it made the room smaller.

Something flashed across her face then and disappeared before he could name it. She did not take her arm back.

Back at the beginning, he traced the M again. This time she made a small sound into her closed mouth, half swallowed before it became anything he could safely interpret. Not pain. Or not only pain. The sound went straight through him and lodged somewhere structural.

"I see it," he said. His voice came out scraped raw. "Every time I close my eyes, that room is there. The floor. The chandelier. Your hair on the marble. The way she kept talking to you like the knife was part of the conversation." He stopped because the memory had turned too exact to move through smoothly. "I see it."

Hermione stared at the table between them. "I know."

"I didn't know it was still there. On you. I thought..." The assumption revealed itself only while he was making it. Seven years of believing a Healer or a spell or Hermione's own brutal competence must have erased it. Must have filed it under war damage already processed. "I thought somebody had fixed it."

That got her eyes back on his face.

"Fixed it."

He heard the failure as soon as it left his mouth. Not because the word was wrong in medicine. Because it implied there had been a version of her afterward that needed restoring to acceptability.

"I mean-"

"I know what you mean."

"No, you don't. I mean I thought there'd been a treatment. A curse reversal. Something."

"Where would it go?"

The question dropped straight through him. It wasn't sharpened for effect. She sounded honestly baffled.

His thumb stalled on the L. The scar kept all its ridges under his skin, the first cuts driven deeper than the last. Hermione had her arm turned upward between them because there was nowhere else for it to have gone.

"Dark magic doesn't heal clean," she said. "You know that."

"I know." His thumb rested on the L. "I just wasn't thinking about yours."

"No." Her gaze dropped to his hand on the scar. "People don't."

He thought of the extra fold she had made in the left sleeve before the examination. He thought of how often she kept that arm angled away from the light, and of his own willingness to take the glamour for the whole answer. She had been hiding it in front of him for days, and he had let her.

"You cover it every day," he said.

"Every day."

"With glamour."

"And sleeves. And not wearing half the things I used to own. And pretending I don't notice when it's July and I'm the only person in the room buttoned to the wrist." She said it evenly. The evenness made the cost plain. "It took me longer than I'd like to admit to work out that the glamour only gets me so far under heavy magic. So I built the rest of my life around the sleeve."

He followed the raised edge of the B with one fingertip. "Because when people see it, they stare."

"They stare first," she said. "Then they correct themselves and try not to, which is worse. Or they ask whether it still hurts, which is idiotic because of course it still hurts sometimes, it's cursed tissue. Or they tell me I was brave." Her mouth twisted. "As if I had a choice worth naming."

The library held the sound of her breathing and the soft settling ticks from the spent resonator. Outside, some wind moved against the windows without enough force to rattle them.

"When I first went back to the Ministry after the war, I covered it badly," she said. The words came as if she had not intended them and now could not justify stopping. "A clerk in Records saw it in the lift. I'd spoken to him every day for a week by then. He'd argued with me about filing procedure and borrowed my pencil and called me difficult. After that he started asking whether I needed someone to walk me home. Every morning. He never argued with me again." She looked at him steadily. "That was the week I learned pity can make a room unusable faster than fear."

He had spent years thinking hatred was the worst way a room could tilt around a person. Hatred at least admitted personhood. Pity reduced.

"You didn't want that from me."

"I didn't want it from anyone." She pulled a breath in through her nose, let it out carefully. "I can work with almost anything except being turned into evidence. Once people know, the scar arrives before I do. It does the talking. I don't."

His hand, still on the word, felt suddenly both intrusive and insufficient. Not because she had asked him to stop. Because she had not.

"What am I doing right now, then."

Her eyes went to his face, assessing as if the answer might change while she watched it. "You're still here."

It was such a small distinction that he nearly missed the scale of it.

"You think that'll last."

"I think you don't know yet." A beat. "Most people don't."

He could not argue. There was a part of him that wanted to separate himself from *most people* on principle. That part sounded adolescent even in silence. Instead he let his fingers rest against the letters and told the truth he had been dragging around the room since the moment the glamour failed.

"I watched," he said. The admission came out heavy and badly shaped. "I stood against the wall and watched her do it. I was sixteen. I was terrified. My father was in the room. None of that is enough. I know it isn't."

"No." Her voice held no mercy and no cruelty either. Only fact. "It isn't."

He nodded once. The answer hurt less than the possibility of absolution would have.

"And don't do the other thing either," she said.

He looked up.

"Don't turn this into your confession because it's easier to hold your guilt than what happened to me." Her thumb tapped once, almost absent-mindedly, against the inside of his wrist where her hand still lay from the examination. "I'm not carrying that for you."

The rebuke landed clean because it was deserved. He had been raised around people who made confession into performance and remorse into a method of keeping the subject themselves. He knew the trick well enough to feel sick at having approached it by instinct.

"I'm not asking you to."

"Good."

The word lay between them a moment. Fire shifted low in the grate. One of the diagnostic crystals, forgotten on its side, slowly bled the last of its stored light into the table.

He looked back down at the scar. "I'm not going to apologize."

Her eyes narrowed a fraction, not because the line was wrong, but because it risked being one more version of male self-importance if he handled it badly.

"That sounds very like something a Malfoy would say."

He deserved that too. "Probably. Let me finish."

She waited.

"If I say sorry, then you have to answer it." His thumb pressed into the groove of the L. "Even if you don't want to, the room changes. You're stuck telling me it wasn't my fault or it was a long time ago or I was sixteen or some other thing that makes this smaller so we can both stand up and pretend we've done the civilized part." The sentence frayed and he let it fray. "And I can't make this—" He stopped. Shook his head once. His thumb stayed on the L.

The hammering in her pulse eased by degrees under his hand. Across the table her other hand still circled his wrist where it had held him for the examination. He only registered it fully when her thumb shifted once against the skin below the Mark, a live warm stroke that might have been accidental if everything else in the room had not been so deliberate.

"Nobody does that," she said. Her voice had dropped out of its usual register. "Everyone always—" She stopped. "Nobody just stays in it."

No register he possessed offered a useful reply. He left the air between them unfilled, and for once the omission did not feel like cowardice.

She moved first again. Her hand turned on his arm and settled flat over the skull and snake. The touch had nothing to do with diagnosis at first. Warm palm. Warm fingers. His Mark quieted under it with humiliating speed.

She felt it happen. Her brows drew together, not in surprise, but in concentration.

"It's hot all the way through," she said, almost to herself. "Much worse than Monday."

"You can stop."

"So can you, apparently. You haven't been telling me half of it."

"I'm telling you now."

"Because I found the margin notes and because you look dreadful."

"Professional victory to you."

"Don't be difficult."

"I wasn't aware I had another texture."

That almost got the corner of her mouth again. Almost. She shifted her hand higher, feeling along the swollen edge of the curse with her fingertips rather than the flat of her palm. The motion was clinical until it wasn't. No spell. No crystals. Just the knowledge in her hands and what his skin did in answer.

"It settled the second I touched it," she said.

"It usually does."

"Not like this."

He made the mistake of meeting her eyes. Whatever she read in his face, she chose not to name.

"The deeper pass isn't finished," she said after a moment. "The readings broke when the glamour did, but the flare pattern is bad enough that I need at least a stabilization sweep before I leave it alone."

"Can you do that."

"Yes." She glanced at her exposed forearm, then back at him. "Unless you're about to tell me the sight of me is too upsetting for the science."

His grip on the scar tightened by a fraction before he made himself ease it. "Don't be obscene."

Her eyes held his for one suspended second. "Good."

She withdrew her hand from the Mark only long enough to retrieve the wand. The loss of contact made the curse stir at once, a small ugly flex under the skin. Both of them felt it. Hermione swore softly, put the wand down again, and set her left hand back over the Mark before the flare could build.

"Not like that," she said. "It's keyed too tightly right now."

The statement carried more than the immediate problem. He heard the rest of it and had no better response than stillness.

"What do you need."

"The flare log. The clean parchment. And don't move unless I tell you."

He passed the catalogue sheet with his free hand while she kept the other pinned under her palm. She drew the unruined pages toward herself, uncapped the ink, and began copying the times from his margin notes in quick precise strokes, all without removing her hand from his arm. The position brought them absurdly close over the table, shoulders nearly touching, her hair fallen forward enough that he could see the loosened curl at the nape of her neck.

"This one," she said, tapping the line from half one. "You wrote *guest wing* and then crossed it out."

"I noticed."

"You crossed it out so badly I had to angle the page toward the light to read it."

He said nothing.

"I am not going to ask what you thought you were doing."

"Thought would be generous."

That got him a brief look. Not soft. Not hard either. Simply present.

"All right," she said. "Then I'm not going to answer it."

The changed mercy of that nearly undid him.

She worked through the notes and the broken data side by side, reconstructing what the self-scribing quill had caught before the spell collapse. Numbers. Channel density. Reaction intervals. The language returned them to safer ground without fully rescuing them from what had happened. Every few lines she paused, eyes half closed, feeling with her hand what the instrument had not finished telling her.

"The secondary decay isn't decay," she said finally.

"What is it."

"I said I don't know yet."

"But you think something."

Her thumb moved once over the inner curve below the skull. "I think the curse is trying to use a pathway it didn't have when it was first cast. That's why the flare profile looks wrong. It's not summoning. It's not simple reactivation." She stopped herself with visible effort. "I need more data before I call it anything."

He had spent enough time beside her work to recognise that tone. Not caution. Withholding. Not necessarily from him. Sometimes Hermione's discipline took the form of refusing language before language could simplify a thing.

"Does it end with my arm still attached."

"I expect so."

"An inspiring bedside manner."

"I'm not here bedside."

No. She was here with her hand on his Mark and Bellatrix's word open to the room between them, which was something far worse and far better than bedside.

When the clean page had more numbers than blank space, she set the quill aside and finally looked down at the contact holding his arm quiet. Her forearm lay fully bared beside his. Scar and Mark close enough that one more inch would have put silver letters against blackened snake. The juxtaposition was so violent it almost looked contrived. It wasn't. It was only history refusing to distribute itself fairly.

He heard the shape of the thought before he said it and knew it would land badly.

"This isn't the same."

Hermione's head lifted sharply. "No."

"I didn't mean-"

"I know what you meant." Her voice had roughened at the edges, exhaustion or strain or the simple fact of sitting here unhidden. "But don't let the sight of them next to each other tempt you into symmetry. Your arm is what they did to you. Mine is what your side did to me in your house. Those things can talk to each other. They are not twins."

The correction did not humiliate him. It steadied the room.

"All right," he said.

That seemed to cost her more than the speech had. Some of the tension went out of her shoulders. "All right."

They sat with that for a while. Fire dropped lower. The colour had gone out of the windows completely, leaving only the reflection of the room in the glass: table, instruments, two bent figures, one bare forearm and another. Somewhere in the Manor a clock sounded six, muffled by distance and too much house between.

Hermione blinked down at the page as if the sound had reminded her there was still a sequence to the afternoon. "I need the tea."

It took him a second to understand the sentence. Not because it was unclear. Because she had asked.

"I'll get it."

Her hand remained on his arm until the last possible second. When she lifted it, the Mark stirred once in complaint and then subsided, as if whatever protest it had planned had been delayed by the certainty of return. He did not comment on that. Neither did she.

The corridor to the servants' kitchen was thirty paces again.

He noticed it, stopped, and kept walking.

The kitchen fires were hotter than the library's, iron kettles steaming and copper pans hanging dull in the light. An elf appeared before he could call and looked up at him with the wary competence of a creature who had learned the household's tempers generations ago.

"Tea for the library," he said. "Strong. And send up something edible."

The elf's ears twitched. "For one place or two, sir?"

The question struck him so hard it might as well have been aimed. "Two."

Bread arrived first, then a plate of cold chicken, pickled onions, hard cheese, and the heel of a loaf still warm in the middle. By the time he carried the tray back himself, the answer had not grown easier in his mouth. Two.

Hermione had not moved far. She was at her own table now with the pages spread around her in a semicircle, making a clean transcript from the ruined notes. The left sleeve stayed shoved above the elbow. She looked up when he came in, glanced to the tray, and then to his face with an unreadable expression that might once have become refusal.

"It's only tea," he said, too quickly. "And bread. You haven't eaten since-"

He stopped. There was no safe measurement to finish that with.

"Since luncheon," she said for him, though they both knew the luncheon tray had gone back mostly untouched. Her eyes dropped to the bread, then to his hands. "All right."

He set the tray on the table between the workspaces, not too close to either of them. An old instinct made him reach for the second cup first. He checked it halfway to the saucer and let himself finish the motion rather than pretending not to have noticed. Hermione watched the correction happen and said nothing.

The first swallow put colour back into her face so incrementally he might have imagined it if he had not been looking for any evidence he had not made things worse by bringing the tray at all.

She held the cup in her left hand.

There was no performance in it. No testing glance to see whether he would flinch now that the scar had become part of an ordinary gesture. She was simply thirsty, tired, and unwilling to waste the effort of hiding from him again tonight. The letters curved white around the handle.

He broke bread because doing nothing with his hands had become impossible. She took half when he offered it. That was all. No ceremony. The simplicity of it hurt more than a declaration would have.

For several minutes the only sounds were cup against saucer, quill against parchment, and the low stubborn hum still trapped inside the damaged resonator. The quiet had changed register. Earlier in the week it had been refusal. Now it was work the room had permission to hold.

Hermione finished the first page of clean notes and slid it aside. "Read me the flare times again."

He did. She wrote them into the margin beside the deeper measurements. When his voice roughened at the second half-one entry, she did not look up. The mercy of that was beginning to feel less like avoidance and more like skill.

"This line," she said, tapping the jagged place where the quill had gone wild at the spell break. "I think this is the channel response, not artifact."

He came around to her side of the table before he had considered whether that was now allowed. She made space without moving her chair back. Their shoulders nearly touched over the parchment.

"Here," he said. "That isn't a three. The tail continues underneath the blot. It's eight."

"It could be either."

"It's eight. You do the same shape when you're rushing."

She turned her head just enough to look at him. "You've started identifying my numerals."

"We've been working together for weeks."

"That wasn't an accusation."

It had sounded like one because he still heard accusation in any sentence that revealed he had been watching her closely. He looked back down at the page.

"It's eight," he said again, more quietly.

Her quill hovered over the line. "Fine. Eight."

The concession should not have mattered. It did.

They worked through the rest of the broken series that way: her reading the instrument's shorthand, him recalling where the line had been interrupted, both of them pretending the arrangement was ordinary enough to survive. At one point she reached past him for the catalogued folio they had been pretending to consult all morning and her forearm brushed the inside of his wrist. The Mark answered with a short, almost grateful pulse. They both felt it. Neither commented.

By the time the last page was in order, the food had thinned to crumbs and the tea had gone from hot to merely warm. Hermione set her quill down and flexed her cramped fingers. Ink had smudged along the side of her hand.

"The inflammation's lower now," she said without looking at him. "The surface heat came down once the contact stabilized."

"Because of you."

"Because of the examination."

He let the correction stand even though they both knew it was only partly true.

Her gaze shifted to the scar, to the letters made ordinary by proximity and impossible by content. She rubbed a thumb once along the inside of her wrist, not over the word exactly, but close enough to count.

"I should recast it," she said.

"Should you."

His two words came out level, but she heard the rest anyway. Recasting it now would return them to the week she had just spent building: one where the skin existed and the room pretended it did not. Perhaps that would be easier tomorrow. It seemed impossible tonight.

She leaned back in the chair and shut her eyes for one brief second. When she opened them again, they were clearer.

"Not tonight," she said.

The answer did something to his breathing he did not trust enough to examine closely.

She gathered the clean pages into order. He watched the care with which she aligned the corners, the competence returning as structure around whatever the afternoon had exposed. It would have been easy to mistake that competence for retreat. He knew better now, or knew enough not to make that mistake casually.

"This week," he said before he could decide whether it was wise, "was it because of this."

Hermione's hands stopped on the pages. Not frozen. Arrested.

"Partly," she said after a moment.

The single word sat there with all the restraint of an entire closed door.

He could have asked the rest. He could have said *and the other part?* He could have tried to prise information out of the space that answer left behind. Instead he looked at the bare forearm still visible between them and at the exhaustion she had not bothered disguising.

"All right."

Her eyes came to him then with something very like surprise.

That was not the end of what she had not told him. He knew it. Some asymmetry still stood in the room with them, technical or personal or both. But whatever had happened this afternoon, he had no intention of forcing it at wandpoint by calling a question honesty. Not tonight.

For the first time in days, the silence that followed did not feel punitive.

Hermione gathered the instruments back into their cloth in slower order than she had set them out. The cracked crystal went into a separate wrap. The resonator, still sulking from the break in the spell, required both hands and a muttered adjustment charm before it would collapse into travel size. Draco took the parchment stack when she handed it over without asking why. He held the pages while she tied the ribbon around them. A small task. Domestic almost. The sort of thing that would once have passed unnoticed. Now his awareness of each transfer of weight felt indecently sharp.

When the last instrument was away, she did not roll the left sleeve back down.

That fact altered the room more than any declaration could have. Not permanently. Not, perhaps, beyond tonight. But enough. Enough that the sight of the scar stopped arriving as revelation and started arriving as presence. Still terrible. Still specific. No longer newly hidden.

He realised after a while that the Mark had gone completely quiet.

Not numb. Not absent. Resting.

Hermione saw him notice.

"Does it hurt now."

"No."

"At all."

He tested the answer honestly. The arm still ached in the deep bruised way any cursed tissue ached after being roused and handled. But the live viciousness had gone out of it. The commandless reach. The blind agitation.

"No," he said again. "Not like before."

She nodded and made a note of that on the cleanest page, as if the relief deserved the dignity of record.

The hour had pushed fully into evening. The fire had dropped to coals. Most of the candles in the library were guttering, wax spilled into pale loops down the iron branches. He should have rung for more light. He did not. The half-dark felt proportionate to the day.

Hermione drew the final page toward her and began the formal transcription the Ministry would eventually see if she ever decided this set of notes was fit for any eyes but her own. Her hand had steadied. The script on the page was the competent, elegant version of her work, not the slanting field notes she allowed herself in the middle of a live problem. She wrote with her left forearm bare above the parchment, the silver letters catching and disappearing as the candlelight moved.

He went back to the other table because remaining at her shoulder while she made the clean copy would have turned intimacy into surveillance. From there he could still hear the scratch of the quill. He opened the book he had failed to read all morning and found, to his surprise, that the words now stayed where they were put.

A little later an elf came silently to clear the tray. Draco almost spoke, then thought better of it. Let the household see what it saw. Let it take the measure of the untouched second cup no longer waiting untouched.

Hermione did not look up until the elf had gone.

"The archive should open tomorrow," she said, eyes still on the page.

He should have asked how she could know that. He should have made some dry remark about the house's opinions or the unreliability of magical architecture with emotional ambitions. Instead he heard only the plain fact under the sentence: *tomorrow.* Not departure. Not another day of shut doors and professional frost. Tomorrow.

"All right," he said.

Her quill moved again. "We'll test it before nine. If it doesn't, I want the surface measurements from the south wall and the threshold stone."

"You assume I'm awake before nine."

"I assume misery has made you punctual."

Against every reasonable instinct, that almost made him laugh. The sound came out small and rough and real enough that her mouth changed in answer, not quite a smile, but the memory of one making a first attempt back into the room.

Then she bent over the parchment again and kept writing.

The left sleeve stayed where she had shoved it. Each pass of the quill carried the silver letters through the dying firelight, and across the room his Mark stayed quiet.


# Chapter 13

The pub was a Muggle place near Charing Cross Road, a narrow room with uncomfortable chairs and middling beer and no associations. She and Ron had started using it after the divorce because neither of them had ever kissed or argued or decided anything in this building, which was exactly why it worked. There was no memory to navigate around. It was just a room.

She'd Apparated into the alley behind the used bookshop three doors down and walked the rest, her coat pulled close against the December cold. The Floo would have been easier, but the Floo was unreliable now. There had been three failures in the last week alone, connections dropping mid-transit, and she didn't fancy rematerializing in someone's kitchen in Birmingham because the network had lost her routing. So she had Apparated, with the familiar squeeze and the ringing in her ears afterward, and then London came back at her all at once: damp pavement under her boots, diesel from a bus taking the corner too fast, wet cardboard in the bookshop bin, a cyclist swearing at a van. After weeks of controlled quiet, the city felt almost aggressively public. It wasn't bad. It was only more than she was used to.

Ron was already there. These days he was always early, a habit he'd acquired only after the separation. At the sight of her, he stood, which was new too, some politeness he'd adopted lately that she hadn't asked for and didn't correct because correcting it would mean discussing it, and discussing it would mean admitting the shape of their interactions had changed.

"Hey." He kissed her cheek. His face was cold. "You look — you look good."

It came out slightly wrong, the compliment carrying too much weight because he'd had to decide to say it, and the decision was audible. It wasn't worth rescuing.

"Got you a cider. Is that still right?"

"That's perfect."

They sat. The pub was half-empty at two in the afternoon: a few regulars nursing pints, a woman reading a paperback in the corner, a football match on the mounted television with the sound off. Ron glanced at it. Since the divorce he'd become genuinely interested in football, one of the small expansions his life had made without her in it. He'd explained the offside rule to her twice and she still didn't understand it, which he found funny in a way that wasn't mean. The fact that he could find it funny instead of irritating was one of the ways she knew they'd done the right thing.

"How's work?" she asked.

"Good. Weird. George has got me managing the books for the second shop and I'm completely rubbish at it, but it turns out that when I'm rubbish at the books, George does them himself, and then they're actually done right. So it's a system."

Her laugh brought out a grin, pleased with itself for getting it and then slightly embarrassed by the pleasure. A second later his ears went pink and he looked down at his beer, and the familiarity of it pulled at the old, muted place where their marriage used to live. It wasn't loss. It was fondness for the thing they'd been that had become the thing they were now, which was different and less and also, in ways she still couldn't fully articulate, more honest.

"You?"

"Still at the Manor. The collection keeps turning up material no one's catalogued properly. It's useful." The professional frame arrived automatically, the one that cost her nothing. "More useful than anything else we've got."

Ron drank his beer. Across from her he looked broader than she remembered, which happened every time she saw him after a gap. Her mental image of him was still nineteen and lanky; the man in front of her was twenty-five, solid, and slightly sunburned from the running Harry had mentioned. He'd taken it up in the summer and kept going through autumn. Now he ran along the canal near his flat three mornings a week, and the idea of Ron voluntarily running, Ron, who at school would have considered a brisk walk to the Great Hall excessive exertion, was one of the small surprises of their post-marriage life that she found genuinely, uncomplexly good.

"So you're there full-time," he said. It wasn't a question.

"Mostly. I come back for meetings."

"And Malfoy's there."

"It's his house, Ron."

"Yeah, I know. I know how it sounds." He stopped, rubbed the back of his neck, then started again with the visible effort of someone rerouting a sentence away from where it was headed. "I mean the house. How's the house? Like, is it... fine? As a place to be?"

There were several ways she could answer that. The house was extraordinary, terrifying, beautiful, sentient, responsive, and currently adjusting its internal geography to accommodate her preferences in ways she hadn't asked for. The house had shown her the room where she'd been tortured and then, weeks later, started warming her bathwater to the exact temperature she preferred. The house knew things about her that she hadn't told it.

"It's unusual," she said. "But manageable."

Ron gave her the look reserved for obvious evasions, the one that said he'd heard the gap and was still deciding whether to push on it. He'd gotten better at that since the divorce. During the marriage he'd either pushed or he hadn't, with almost no calibration. Now there was a middle range, a pause where he weighed it.

"Hermione."

"What?"

"You said manageable. You didn't say fine."

She took a swallow of cider. The pub was warm with the blunt warmth of a radiator on a December afternoon. From the corner came the dry sound of a page turning, and from the regulars' end of the bar came the muted commentary of the match. Beneath all of it, like a radio signal from very far away, ran the low hum of the Mark resonance. It was faint in London and thinned further by distance, but it was there. Draco's pain, that restless ache the Mark carried now, magic searching for a channel that no longer existed, travelled through miles of December air and settled in her forearm like an echo.

Pressure helped, so she pressed her wrist against the edge of the table.

"I don't have a neat answer for you," she said. "The house is complicated. The research is real and it matters. And I'm handling it."

A strip of label was already coming loose beneath Ron's thumb, a nervous habit she'd forgotten about and now remembered with the sharpness of an old object found at the back of a drawer. The recognition was almost physical. All through their engagement he'd done that in restaurants, peeling labels while they talked about the wedding or didn't talk about the things they should have been talking about instead.

"George and Angelina are getting serious," he said after a while.

"Really?"

"Yeah. She's been at Sunday dinners three weeks running. Mum's broken out the good china. You know what that means."

"Molly Weasley pulling out the good china is practically a betrothal announcement."

"Don't say that to George, he'll bolt." Ron smiled. The smile was genuine and easy and had nothing to do with Hermione, which was the kind of smile she liked best from him now, the ones that were about his life, not about their shared past. "Anyway. She's good for him. She's got this way of just not taking his shit, you know? Like, he'll do the George thing where he deflects with a joke and she just... waits. Doesn't laugh. Waits until he says the real thing."

"Good."

"Yeah."

He drank, set the bottle down, and looked at her over the rim of it. "She's also made me start buying actual food."

"Ron."

"I know. Deeply embarrassing. There's coriander in my fridge right now."

"What are you doing with coriander?"

"Letting it go bad, mostly." A quick, crooked grin. "Anyway. I've got a routine now, is the thing. It's boring. It's good."

Her fingers tightened once around the glass.

"I do eat," she said.

"I didn't say you didn't."

"You meant it."

"I meant—" He rubbed at the ragged strip of label with his thumb. "Yeah. I meant it a bit."

The woman in the corner turned another page. Someone at the bar laughed. Hermione sat in the middle of those ordinary human sounds and felt the resonance pulse, faint but continuous, a thread that connected her to a house in Wiltshire and the man inside it. Ron, peeling his label across from her, had no idea that she could feel another person's pain in her own arm. He didn't know that a house might be engineering half the comfort it offered, or that the reason she couldn't say fine was stranger than anything he was probably imagining.

"Listen," Ron said. He put the beer bottle down. The label was half off, a ragged strip of wet paper. "I know it's not my — I know I don't get to say this. But I'm going to say it once and then shut up."

"Okay."

"You do this thing." He wasn't looking at her. "Where you find a problem and it becomes — look, you did it with Buckbeak's appeal. Third year. You did it with the Horcruxes. You did it with us, our marriage, when it was ending, you had a whole — you researched the divorce like a project, Hermione, and it was the most organized breakup in history and also the saddest thing I've ever been part of."

"Ron—"

"I'm not — that's not a dig. I don't even know if I'm saying it right." He pressed his thumb into the shredded label. "Just that with us I kept thinking I'd say something, and then I didn't, and by the time I did it was—" He stopped. "You know."

She didn't say anything. The cider had gone warm in her glass.

"That house," he said. "That family. The research and the decay and whatever else is happening that you're not telling me. I don't mean Malfoy — or, I don't know, maybe I do, I don't actually care about that bit. I just—" He stopped. Started again, quieter. "I just don't want to ring you in three months and find out you haven't left the building. All right?"

She turned her glass on the table. Once. Back again.

"You're not wrong," she said.

He blinked. "That's new. You admitting I'm not wrong."

"Don't get used to it."

He smiled. The rueful, self-knowing one she'd always liked best on him. "Wouldn't dream of it."

By the time they were putting on coats, he was telling her about the flat. He'd finally hung the shelves in the bedroom, badly, and they'd already fallen down once. This weekend he was going to try again with a level because Harry had pointed out that eyeballing it wasn't working. In return she gave him a translation problem she'd been stuck on, kept vague and professional. As ever, he listened without tracking every technical detail and still caught the shape of it, the frustration and the fascination, nodding in the right places.

"Oh," he said, as they headed for the door. "The other thing. Mum went to St. Mungo's for her knee, the old jinx, the Moody one, and they turned her away. Said they're rationing healing magic. Prioritizing critical cases."

"I know about the rationing."

"I figured you would." He wound his scarf around his neck, a Chudley Cannons scarf, orange and faded, that she'd given him three Christmases ago when they were still married. He still wore it. That left her with no idea what to do. "The Floo dropped again this morning. Two hours. And a kid in Diagon Alley, six maybe, set a rubbish bin on fire. Accidental magic, but the kind that shouldn't happen that young, not that strongly."

"I know."

"Right." He looked at her. "If the work matters, the stuff you're doing at the Manor, then I suppose it matters. I just want you to come back from it. That's all."

At the door she hugged him. He was warm and solid and smelled like beer, cold air, damp wool, and the soap he'd used since school. His return hug was careful, his arms finding her the old way and then stopping short, leaving space between her ribs and his as if he were measuring it by feel.

"Take care of yourself," he said.

"You too."

He left. She watched him walk up Charing Cross Road toward the Leaky, hunching into his coat, and felt the ache of watching someone you loved walk away from you in the most ordinary way, not leaving, just going, back to his flat and his badly hung shelves and his Sunday dinners and the life he was building that didn't need her in it. It wasn't loss. Loss had already had its turn. This was the healed place afterward, skin closed over, only a faint pull when an old bruise got pressed.

On the pavement she felt the December cold on her face and the Mark resonance in her arm. When she pressed her wrist against the brick wall beside her, the wall was only a wall, cold and rough and utterly indifferent to her touch.

---

Harry's office at the Department of Magical Law Enforcement was the same corner room they'd given him two promotions ago. He'd done nothing with it except accumulate files and add a photograph of Ginny and James that sat on the desk facing outward. James was nine months old in the picture, chewing on a toy broomstick with the focused commitment of a child who'd located the centre of his universe.

"You look tired," Harry said.

"Hello to you too."

He stood and hugged her, a real hug with both arms, the kind of contact that required no negotiation because the friendship had survived a war and everything after. It could take the weight. He smelled like cedar soap. He'd used the same brand since fourth year.

"Tea?"

"Please."

Harry made tea badly. It was too strong, there wasn't enough milk, and the cup had been repaired so many times the handle sat slightly crooked. Hermione drank it anyway because Harry's tea was one of the constants of her life, and she wasn't prepared to lose constants right now.

"Ron said you saw him," Harry said as he settled behind his desk.

"An hour ago."

"How was it?"

"Fine. Good, mostly. He told me about Molly and St. Mungo's."

Years of absorbing bad news professionally had reorganized the muscles around Harry's eyes. He didn't look haggard. He looked set, alert in a low-frequency way that never quite switched off.

"It's worse than the rationing," he said. "The Floo's been intermittent for three weeks. We've had four accidental magic incidents in the last ten days, all kids under seven, all discharging way beyond what their age should produce. One of them blew the windows out of a flat in Camden. Muggle flat. We had to send a team for the glass, the neighbours, the parents, all of it. Two Aurors spent half the afternoon explaining to a terrified mother that her kid wasn't possessed."

Hermione set her cup down. "Faster than we projected. Once it starts breaking through in children, it isn't staying in the Floo or hospital wards anymore. The Manor records are the only place we've found that says how the old anchors were weighted. Without that, we're patching blind."

"I got the Mysteries briefing. It said that with more diagrams."

She almost smiled, but not quite.

"How close are you?" he asked.

"Close enough to know we're reading the right material. Not close enough to promise a fix."

There was a knock. The door opened on a young Auror with wind-burned cheeks and a stack of incident forms hugged to her chest.

"Sorry," she said. "They need your signature on the Camden follow-up. Muggle Liaison wants a second sweep in the morning."

Harry took the top parchment, scanned it, and swore under his breath.

"What now?" Hermione asked when the door shut again.

"A neighbour found robe fabric in the window frame and thinks it's from a theatre costume. Which would be funny if the kid who blew the window out hadn't done it because the magic came up too hard and too fast and no one could stop it." He signed the form with quick, irritated strokes and shoved it aside. "That's what it looks like here now. Paperwork stacked over actual fires."

Through the office wall she could hear someone arguing with a filing cabinet. The cabinet was making a grinding, petulant sound, the kind of noise an enchanted object made when its underlying magic was misbehaving. The wizard outside swore. A drawer slammed hard enough to rattle Harry's framed photograph.

"And that's before the part where people start looking for someone to blame," Harry said.

"You mean the Ministry."

"I mean everyone." He opened the top folder on his desk and turned it toward her. Paper-clipped together were a Prophet cutting with her name in it, a request for clarification from the Minister's correspondence office, and a brief note from Department of Mysteries administration asking whether her Malfoy travel authorizations should remain unrestricted while the story was circulating. "Croaker had to answer for you this morning."

She looked down at the clipping. Skeeter's paragraph was only a few lines. It didn't need more. *Extended residence*. *Historic estate*. *Questionable private access during a national emergency.* Her name was there once. Malfoy's twice.

"That's absurd," she said.

"It is, and it still happened." Harry tapped the second parchment. "The correspondence office wants to know why a Department of Mysteries researcher is effectively living at Malfoy Manor while St. Mungo's is turning people away. Administration wants to know if your travel needs reviewing because once a name hits the paper everyone suddenly remembers procedures."

"So what did Croaker say?"

"That if they pulled you now, they could explain it themselves to the parents in Camden and the healers at St. Mungo's. It bought you time. That's all."

Hermione stared at the papers. A Prophet mention was cheap trouble. Internal review meant forms, signatures, someone deciding how many nights a week she was allowed at the Manor. It meant hours shaved off the archive and handed to people who cared more about appearances than whether St. Mungo's kept turning patients away.

"People are watching," Harry said. "Not because they care about — look. The country's frightened. And you're in the paper next to Malfoy."

She pushed the folder back toward him. "The research is real. The archive is the only source."

"I believe you." He leaned back in his chair. "But Croaker had to answer for you this morning, and a month ago none of this was on anyone's desk. That's—" He gestured at the folder. "That's what's different."

She knew that. The trouble was that hearing it in Harry's office, with incident forms still wet from Camden on his desk, made it sound less like theory and more like a clock starting.

"Ginny read the piece too," Harry said.

Hermione closed her eyes briefly. "Of course she did."

"She said to tell you that if you're going to keep sleeping at Malfoy Manor, you'd better decide what story you're prepared to let people tell about it, because if you don't, Rita bloody Skeeter will do it for you."

"That does sound like Ginny."

"Mm." His mouth twitched, then flattened again. "She also said—" He ran a hand through his hair. "You know what she said."

Hermione looked at the desk instead of at him.

"Harry—"

"I'm not asking." He was quiet for a moment. "I don't even know what I'd—" He stopped. "Just don't let it get ahead of you."

The tea tasted terrible. Outside the office, the filing cabinet had apparently lost its fight; a second drawer banged open and a wizard muttered, "For fuck's sake," with the exhausted venom of a man arguing with furniture in the middle of a public emergency.

"How bad is it going to get?" Harry asked, quieter now.

"Bad." Her answer came too fast. She forced herself to slow down. "Hogwarts, Azkaban, this building. Same old work under all of them. If they pull me out of the Manor before I get the weighting tables, we're improvising with wards we can't afford to improvise with. That's why I'm there. Every line I get out of that archive matters."

"And Malfoy?"

It was too direct, even for Harry, and because it was Harry he got to ask it.

"Malfoy reads the family notation," she said. "Without him I lose half the material."

Harry looked at her for a beat too long. He knew that wasn't the whole answer. He also knew he wasn't going to get the rest by pushing.

"Right," he said.

He let it go. She recognized the move from Ron earlier, the same deliberate step back from a door he could force and wouldn't.

They stood. His second hug was briefer, an arm around her shoulders and a squeeze.

"If you need anything," he said. "You know."

"I know."

When she left, the folder with her name in it was still on the edge of his desk.

The visitors' entrance took her out through the red telephone box and deposited her on a grey London street in December. It was four o'clock. The light was already failing. In mid-December the sun was gone or going by then, and the sky had flattened into a steel colour that would be black within the hour. On the pavement around her London kept moving, commuters and shoppers and a man selling roasted chestnuts from a cart on the corner, smoke rising into the cold air and dispersing.

She'd meant to go back to the flat. There were clean jumpers in the wardrobe, notes she'd left spread over the kitchen table, a sink full of mugs she hadn't dealt with before returning to Wiltshire three days ago. One quiet evening in Bloomsbury had seemed, that morning, like a reasonable plan. She'd wash her hair, eat toast standing at the counter, and sleep in a bed no one else had ever touched.

Instead she kept walking.

She passed the bookshop where she'd bought her first non-magical novel after the war and the cafe where she and Ron had sat on a Tuesday afternoon and agreed, over lukewarm coffee, that the marriage was done, not in those words and not dramatically, but in the accumulation of small honest sentences that added up to the same conclusion. At the Tube entrance a busker was playing a song she nearly recognized, his guitar case open on the pavement, and a woman dropped a coin in while he nodded without breaking the melody.

On Shaftesbury Avenue she passed a shop whose display window should have held a concealed advertisement for Madam Malkin's, one of those charmed billboards that Muggles saw as a shoe advert, and the magic was flickering. The image stuttered between dress robes and brogues, two frames overlaid. A passing Muggle glanced at it and kept walking, registering the glitch as bad wiring. Hermione stopped dead on the pavement.

The image jerked again: dress robes, brogues, dress robes. Hermione's hand went into her coat pocket and closed around her wand. She could have propped the concealment for an hour, maybe two, but not from a public pavement without drawing exactly the kind of attention the failing charm was meant to avoid.

A bus breathed to a stop at the kerb. Cold air pushed exhaust and roasted chestnuts down the street. Two schoolgirls in green blazers shoved past her, arguing about homework. The city went on around the failing magic with the blank, practical indifference of a place too large to stop for one person's realization.

She could still go to Bloomsbury. She could let herself into the flat, put the kettle on, spend one night away from the Manor. The thought lasted less than a minute. Croaker was already burning time on her behalf, and the work waiting in Wiltshire had just followed her into a shop window.

She turned away from Bloomsbury.

From the alley behind the telephone box she Apparated, and the world compressed to a needle-point and expanded again until she was standing on the frosted gravel of the Manor drive with the last grey light dying over Wiltshire and her ears ringing and her breath coming out in clouds.

---

The Floo failed twice before it finally deposited her at the Manor gates instead of the front hall. The walk up the drive took four minutes. Once, early on, she'd timed it, when the Manor had still been a professional assignment and she'd still been pretending measurement could make this manageable. She didn't bother anymore. The cold bit at her face. Her boots crunched on frozen gravel. The bare trees along the drive stood like structural drawings of themselves, all line and no softness, black against the darkening sky.

With each step the Mark resonance strengthened. The faint signal she'd been carrying all day in London filled in and gained resolution, the way a radio station clarifies as you drive toward its tower. His pain and wakefulness came through with it, plus a quality she couldn't name but had learned to recognize, agitation edging toward expectation. The resonance put him two floors up, though she was still outside and the information shouldn't have narrowed that sharply at this distance. Either the bond was learning her or she was learning it.

Halfway up the drive she made herself a small, stupid promise: she'd go to her room first. She'd wash the London out of her hair, change her jumper, eat, and write Croaker before the guilt of not doing it turned into avoidance. Only then would she go to the library. Only then would she deal with Draco if he was there. The order mattered.

The front door opened before she reached it.

It didn't wait for a hand on the latch. Heavy oak swung inward, smooth and unhurried, and the entrance hall beyond was lit with that golden light the Manor made when it seemed almost pleased.

Then she stepped inside.

Warmth met her at once. It wasn't the blunt heat of a radiator or a fire. It seemed to know the cold spots in her coat, the numbness in her fingers, the ribbon of December cold that had slipped down the gap at her collar while she'd stood on a London pavement thinking about things she didn't want to think about. It found all of it and answered with an attention that felt less like a heating charm and more like being noticed by something enormous and careful.

The air smelled of old paper, preservation spells, and the faint mineral tang of the Manor's stone. Beneath that was another quality, not a smell but a settling, a sense of the house easing around her return as if the building itself had been holding tension that her presence released.

For a moment she stood in the entrance hall with her coat still on and her hand still wrapped around the strap of her satchel.

Ron's voice, rough with restraint: I just don't want to ring you in three months and find out you haven't left the building.

Harry's voice: just don't let it get ahead of you.

Ginny's voice, carried by Harry and clean as glass: decide what story you're prepared to let people tell.

She was supposed to be able to see the main staircase from here.

Tonight the corridor to the library was short. One left turn opened straight onto the hall outside the library doors, gold light laid down across the runner as neat as an invitation. The staircase was still there somewhere to the right, past a stretch of shadow and a doorway that had not been there that morning. The house wasn't blocking her. It was making one option easy.

The back of her neck prickled.

On the wall beside her, the hook the Manor had made for her waited at exactly the right height. It was plain iron, not decorative, and it had been there since the third night, as if it had always belonged there. She'd never asked for it. Tonight, with the corridor shortened and the library already lit, taking her coat off on that hook felt absurdly closer to consent than hanging wool on iron had any right to feel.

She kept it on another fifteen seconds, long enough for the cold to leach out of the fabric and the warmth underneath to start feeling good.

Then she took it off anyway.

The library door was already open. Inside, the room glowed warm and gold, the lamps lit, the shelves steady. Her desk sat where she'd left it, notes stacked in order, a cup of tea beside them that she hadn't made and that was, she knew without touching it, exactly the temperature she preferred. Across from her chair, Draco's stood pulled slightly back from the long table, as if someone had just risen from it or had been expected to.

She stopped on the threshold.

She'd told herself on the drive that her room came first. She'd deal with her hair, find food, write the letter, and only then work.

The library had cut all of that out of the evening and left only the part it wanted.

Her stomach went hollow with it, not fear exactly, not even surprise. It was recognition. The house had learned the shape of the shortest path through her resistance and laid it under her feet like carpet.

When she crossed the room the chair at her desk held the last of the day's warmth. She did not sit, not immediately. She set her satchel down instead and put two fingers against the untouched teacup. The tea was at the perfect temperature. Of course it was. The fact of it made irritation rise so fast it almost steadied her.

"No," she said aloud, very quietly, to the room or the house or herself.

The word went nowhere useful.

The Mark resonance was stronger here. It wasn't sharper with pain this time. It was closer. It was present. He was awake above her, restless in a way she could feel without understanding. When her fingertips touched the edge of the desk, the ache under Draco's Mark eased by a fraction, enough that she felt the release in her own arm before she let herself think what it meant.

A soft click sounded across the table. A second cup had appeared beside Draco's place, steam lifting from it in a straight pale line. He wasn't there. The house was still making room for him.

That, more than the first cup, made her want to leave. The second cup turned hospitality into staging. It made her feel watched in a newly practical way, as if the Manor had moved from preference to arrangement while she was out in London pretending the problem was still theoretical.

She looked back toward the hall. The staircase remained out of sight. It wasn't gone. It was merely inconvenient.

If she walked out now, found the long way round, and went upstairs, the house would not stop her. She knew that. It would let her have her room, her washbasin, her dry jumper, perhaps even the letter to Croaker. It would only have shown her, first, how much easier it could make the other choice.

Her hand stayed on the back of the chair.

She thought of the folder on Harry's desk, the flickering concealment charm on Shaftesbury Avenue, and Ron talking about coriander dying in his fridge because at least the fridge proved he was going home every night. The house had opened the door before she'd reached it. It had shortened the corridor. It had laid out the work and the tea and the seat across from hers as if her evening were already settled.

The ease of it disturbed her so badly that for one clear second she wanted her flat with physical force: the bad radiators, the stack of unpaid post on the sideboard, the kitchen tap that dripped if you didn't wrench it hard left. She wanted a place indifferent enough to let her come and go without comment.

She sat down.

She did not sit because she was convinced. She did not sit because the warning voices had gone quiet. She sat because the notes were here, and the archive was here, and Draco was somewhere above her carrying that low, constant pulse under his skin, and the house had made every step away from the room feel like extra work she no longer trusted herself to choose.

She did not touch the tea.

For nearly a minute she only looked at the second cup across the table, steam rising steady into the warm air. Then she pulled her notes closer, still in the clothes she'd worn through London, and set her quill to the line they hadn't solved that morning.

The library door stayed open behind her. She left it that way, and she did not go upstairs.


# Chapter 14

The texts were in a script he had never seen his ancestors use.

Draco sat with the folio open across his knees because the desk had stopped pretending to be large enough an hour ago. It was bound in a hide he couldn't name, the pages thick as card and faintly warm where his thumbs rested. The notation ran in cramped vertical columns: runic shorthand braided with drafting marks, measurements dropped straight into the language as if the writer had never believed words and stone were separate things. He could pick out fragments: family names, compass bearings, *Fundament* repeated often enough to matter. Read as a whole, the page refused him.

He was not alone with it.

Across from him, Granger was bent over a second manuscript in the gated archive. The room had opened that morning with suspicious ease. The threshold had not paused them or demanded that they prove anything first. After breakfast the inner door had simply been there, already ajar, as if someone had risen early to prepare for guests. If the house meant welcome, fine. If it meant to make togetherness feel ordinary, that fit too.

She had noticed it too. Her gaze had touched the doorframe, paused there, moved on. She did that more now, storing away whatever the Manor offered instead of naming it aloud. Four days earlier, after the Floo failed twice and finally sent her back from London, he'd told her the archive had not opened once in her absence. That was true, and not the thing he'd meant. Since then they had fallen back into the work with a dangerous ease. Where the room had once snapped shut when one of them crossed the threshold, it now held itself open until both of them left, lighting the bowls before either wand came out and keeping both chairs tucked up to the table as if they belonged there. The house had stopped shoving. It waited them out.

"This column here," Granger said. A quill sat behind her ear. The other half of a biscuit rested with criminal calm on the spine of a fourteenth-century manuscript. Her hair, which had started the day tied back, was now mostly being governed by a pencil. "The notation shifts. It's not the same hand as the first three pages."

Closer made sense. The table was wide, the script tiny, and her finger had already found the line in question. Draco moved his chair until their sleeves brushed.

"Different register," he said. "The earlier pages are formal. This is rougher."

"Rougher how?"

The strokes were thicker. The verticals ran uneven. Abbreviations had been shoved into the margins with no concern for elegance.

"Field notes. Written on site, I'd say. While the work was being done."

Her attention sharpened. "Original construction?"

"Fourteen-thirties. Braccus Malfoy oversaw the anchoring work here, but he didn't do it himself. He designed it. Other families handled the field construction. Lineal artisans, my father called them. Families older than ours in England, older in this kind of magic too." Lucius's voice came back with unpleasant clarity, one more inheritance Draco had not asked to keep. "The script must be theirs, or partly theirs."

"So neither of us can read it alone."

"No."

A small nod, more to herself than to him. "Your family knowledge gives us the names and geography. I can work the structural theory. If this is a trade language, that may be enough."

It was not the first time she'd said *enough* and meant *us*.

Work lowered her guard by inches. Concentration put color in her face. It also made her careless in oddly specific ways. The biscuit balanced on the manuscript. The pencil was losing its war with her hair. She read under her breath and never seemed to notice. He had been watching her for weeks. Today he stopped bothering to pretend otherwise.

The Mark ached low in his left arm with that newer, stranger responsiveness that had followed her sustained examinations. Near her, the ache pulled instead of bit, and he caught himself leaving the marked arm braced on the table's edge instead of folding it away. He had stopped pretending the Mark and his own body were separate systems, and the house kept arranging the work so that arm stayed nearest her.

"Look at this," she said, drawing the folio toward the candle. "These aren't repair instructions. They're readings."

Her finger traced a recurring cluster: the root rune, a simplified property sigil, then two parallel strokes tied together by a short bar.

"Inspection?" he asked.

"Assessment. The year mark changes here, here, and here. So do the figures in the margin." She tapped the paired strokes. "This cluster only shows up beside the stable entries."

A cramped note sat under the third mark, written in the mixed hand they were starting to recognize: family shorthand overlaid on trade notation.

Draco sounded it out. "*Concordia incolarum.* Or near enough."

"Concordance of inhabitants," she said. "Household accord, maybe. That's direct textual evidence, not me speculating. And this hooked sign beside it is a resonance mark. I've seen the same form in older anchoring theory."

"Meaning the readings improve when the people in the house are aligned."

"Meaning the people who kept these records thought inhabitant concordance affected anchor stability." Her quill hovered above the page without touching it. "What I'm inferring is the next part. The folio doesn't tell us whether that concordance means blood, marriage, oath, or any two people the house can turn into a usable relation. It doesn't prove the Manor is doing this to us on purpose. But it does show that the house was designed to route stabilizing force through its inhabitants."

"Connection meaning the bond."

"Connection meaning some magical relation between house and resident. I'm being careful on purpose."

He looked at the paired strokes again. "You're also not saying no."

She didn't answer immediately. She flipped back a page instead, though he could see from the way her eyes moved that she was no longer reading. "This much is solid," she said at last. "The Manor wasn't only built on the ley-lines. It was built into them. The house is one of the anchors."

That settled between them with real weight. Britain's magic was failing. The Floo staggered. Children's accidental surges kept worsening. St. Mungo's rationed. The damage was here too, in the stones around them.

"And the house knows it's failing," he said.

"I think it reacts to strain," she said. "Whether that's knowing in any human sense, I don't know."

He pointed again to the paired strokes, and to the note beneath them, and to the whole nauseatingly neat logic of the page. "And that?"

She set the quill down with more care than the gesture should have required. "That says accord matters. Not romance, not necessarily. Not even affection in how you'd usually mean it. Just a usable alignment between inhabitants."

"Usable by whom."

"The house, presumably."

No joke came this time. The room was too warm for one and the line on the page too ugly. He thought of shortened corridors, doors opening before they touched the handle, tea appearing at the exact temperature she preferred, and the way his Mark stopped needling him when she sat close enough to brush his sleeve.

"So when the lattice weakens," he said, "the house leans harder on whatever local stabilizing mechanism it's got left."

"Yes." Her voice had gone flatter, faster. "Or it rewards the pattern that produces the least local strain. Which amounts to the same problem."

"Meaning all of this." He gestured at the archive, the warmth, the ready light, their chairs already too close together. "Not neutral."

"No." She looked finally at him. "If the house gives us access, comfort, maybe even pain relief because we're easier to route when we're in step, that isn't hospitality. That's leverage. I don't know whether it's intentional leverage. I don't know whether the original builders cared about the distinction. But the distinction matters to me."

It mattered to him too. Mostly because she had said *pain relief* without looking at his arm.

"You're talking about coercion."

"I'm talking about a building that may reward compliance with easier conditions and punish the opposite." She dragged a hand through the air between them, impatient with her own phrasing. "No. That's not precise enough. I'm talking about not knowing when we're choosing something and when the house is arranging the choice."

For a beat neither of them moved.

Then she pushed her chair back.

The scrape of its legs across stone sounded too loud in the room. Her sleeve came away from his. The air changed so quickly he almost thought he'd imagined it, except the folio under his hand cooled at once and the ache in his arm sharpened into something meaner.

"Granger."

"Don't move." She was already standing. Her face had gone intent, stripped of everything but procedure. "If we're going to be disgusted, we may as well be accurate about it."

She took three steps toward the inner threshold.

On the second, the candle nearest the door guttered sideways.

On the third, the brass lattice at the archive entrance gave a small metallic click and began, almost politely, to draw inward.
