Draco's hand clamped flat on the page. The line they had just translated didn't vanish, exactly, but it lost its coherence. The two scripts stopped meeting in his head. The Mark bit hard enough to drag a breath out of him.

She turned at once. "What changed?"

He could have lied. He had the reflex for it, still. "The door," he said.

"And?"

The pain had already locked his fingers into the edge of the page, and she already knew too much for false modesty to do either of them any good. "My arm."

Her jaw set once under the skin. "How much?"

"Enough."

She took one more deliberate step backward anyway, watching him instead of the threshold. The brass lattice moved another inch. Somewhere deeper in the shelves a preservation charm gave a strained little whine.

"Come back," he said, more sharply than he meant to.

She did. The door stilled before it shut. The candle righted itself. Warmth returned to the page under his palm, and his fingers uncurled from the parchment as the vicious edge came off the ache in his arm so fast it almost felt obscene.

Neither of them spoke for a moment.

Then Granger pulled a clean sheet of parchment toward her, dipped her quill, and wrote in a hand so neat it was nearly violent: `threshold response / proximity / contact / Mark`.

"Write it down," she said.

"You are."

"Then say it."

He looked at the new column headings, at the page that now felt complicit in his lap, and said, "Distance of roughly six feet. Door began to close. Candle distortion. Mark flare."

Her quill moved. "At the moment the sleeves stopped touching?"

"Before the door. After the chair moved."

"Fine." She wrote that too, then added something smaller underneath that he couldn't make out because she'd angled the page away from him. "We track every environmental change from here on. Door behavior. Room temperature if it shifts enough to matter. Unsolicited objects. Route changes if we leave and come back. Anything your Mark does."

"Anything my Mark does."

She looked up again. "Yes."

He ought to have objected to the clinical tone. It would have been easier than admitting how relieved he was that she had given the ugliness a method instead of recoiling from it. "And what exactly does that get us?"

"A chance not to be stupid." She sat again, though not where she had been. She set her chair beside his with a measured handspan between them, laid her notes flat in the strip of wood between their elbows instead of passing them hand to hand, and adjusted the chair another inch when the house, apparently displeased by moderation, gave a soft settling click in the wall behind the shelves. "If we're going to keep working in here, we do it with the variables named."

"Do you want to stop?"

That got no answer at first. She stared at the words *proximity* and *contact* as if she might revise them into something less naked.

"No," she said finally. "I want to know what stops happening if we refuse the terms. That's different."

"And if the terms are simply us being in the same room."

"Then we document that too." Her fingers tightened once around the quill. "But I'm not going to go on calling it innocent because it comes with decent lighting."

The line was almost funny. Neither of them smiled.

He looked at her new chair placement, at the precise gap she'd left between them, at the notes she now deposited between them as if touch itself had to go through procedure first. "You think it can tell the difference between agreement and surrender."

"I think it can detect reduced local strain." Her voice went clipped with concentration, the way it always did when emotion threatened and she chose precision instead. "Whether it understands the human distinction is a different question."

"That isn't better."

"No."

He watched the threshold for another second, half expecting it to twitch again. It stayed still now that she'd sat back down.

"So we're load-bearing," he said.

This time the sound that escaped her was not a laugh. It was shorter, rougher, something between disbelief and disgust. "Apparently."

"The bleakest available formulation."

"Usually your specialty."

"Not on purpose."

She wasn't looking at him now. She was redrawing the column headings so the ink dried clean. "Let's not let the house mistake sarcasm for concordance either."

"Can it do that?"

"I don't know. I'd rather not train it."

That, at least, was funny enough to register, but only just. The room had changed shape around them. Not physically. The shelves still rose to the same dim height, the candles still burned in the same glass bowls, the old folios still smelled of hide and mineral dust. But the comfort had turned conditional. Every convenience now carried a hook.

They went back to the page more carefully than before.

The difference announced itself at once. They no longer drifted into collaboration. They built it by hand. Her notes stayed on her side until she chose to slide them over. He read aloud only after checking she was following. When the script forced them closer, they moved closer deliberately and Granger marked the moment in the margin of the log with a quick, furious stroke of ink.

For the next hour the work split into two parallel tasks. On one sheet they built the language. On another they recorded the room.

The language came first in fragments: a property sigil he recognized from land grants, a shortened trade mark she identified as a load notation, a year-form he knew from family memorial tablets that turned out, under her eye, to be a maintenance cycle rather than a date. Their key began as a mess of arrows and crossed-out guesses. Then a structure emerged. The mixed hand was not sloppiness. It was mediation. The family script carried names, claims, locations, rights of access. The artisan notation carried procedure, force, stress tolerances, the practical language of how not to let a thing collapse while standing inside it.

"It's not a register," she said, leaning over the central sheet without quite crossing the distance she'd set. "It's an interface."

"For people who didn't trust each other enough to share a proper language?"

"Or trusted each other exactly enough to make one for the job and never write it down anywhere else." Her eyes narrowed over a repeated cluster halfway down the page. "Look. That same mark turns up beside three different family names, but the number form only changes when the household count changes."

"Household count?"

She tapped the margin with the end of the quill. "I think so. Or inhabitant load. Something body-based. Not bloodline. Occupancy."

He read the notation again and didn't like how neatly it fit with the rest. "The house needs people in it."

"The anchor system inside the house may need resident presence to stabilize. That's not the same claim."

"You keep saying that as if cleaner wording will make it nicer."

"It won't." She didn't even pause. "I'm doing it because I don't want to accidentally prove more than we have."

That too fit. Not just the caution. The ferocity underneath it.

She reached past him for the folio, stopped short of contact, and angled the page toward the light. "This line here. If I've got the root right, then this says the west range went unstable in a winter when the inhabited count dropped."

"Dropped how?"

"No idea yet. Deaths, travel, staff moved elsewhere, family away. The notation doesn't moralize. It just records load loss."

He had grown up in rooms where staff appeared and vanished without anyone admitting the mechanics. He had never before considered what the house itself counted when it counted them.

"Read the gloss below it," she said.

It took him two tries. The words were half in family shorthand, half in the trade argot that still snagged his eye at inconvenient angles. "Threshold lag corrected by redistribution to service corridor."

Her gaze sharpened. "Exactly. It compensates. If there aren't enough inhabitants in one part of the house, it reroutes strain somewhere else."

"People as ballast," he said.

"People as part of the mechanism."

"Again, not better."

"Again, no."

She wrote *occupancy-linked redistribution?* on the translation sheet and, after a small visible hesitation, copied *west range instability after resident loss* into the log beside their notes on the threshold reaction. Draco watched the second notation appear and felt the morning harden into something less deniable. This was no longer the vague suspicion that the Manor liked them close. This was architecture.

A book slid halfway out of the shelf to his left.

Neither of them moved.

The volume waited there, protruding from the row at a helpful angle.

Granger turned her head slowly and looked at it as if it were a spider that had chosen literacy as a hunting strategy.

"Write that down," she said.

"You really are committed to making the room feel unwelcome."

"The room has done enough to deserve it."

He rose, because it felt better to stand than to accept the book while sitting obediently in the seat the house preferred for him. The volume was a late-seventeenth-century survey of repaired estate enchantments. He recognized it from the main library, where it had lived two rooms and several privileges away from anything as practical as use.

"It surfaced a secondary reference when we stalled," Granger said as he brought it back. She bent over the log again. "Unrequested."

"Helpful, though."

"That's the issue."

He set the book down between them instead of handing it to her. "You're allowed to be helped by something and dislike the terms."

"I'm aware."

She opened it anyway.

He disliked how much relief the sight gave him, not because the book mattered but because she had stayed.

The surfaced text confirmed two of their guesses and complicated a third. By noon they had enough of the notation to distinguish assessment entries from repair entries and baseline readings from crisis notations. Baseline entries ran cleaner, with full measurements and fewer marginal corrections. Crisis entries crowded themselves sideways, like people forced to share a passage built for one. Stable seasons produced one set of marks. Strain produced another. And threaded through both, with dismal regularity, were the paired strokes for concordance.

They showed up beside marriage entries and inheritance shifts, beside nothing so legible as love, but all over anything that altered who lived in the house and how they related to one another inside it.

"There," Granger said, and shoved a page toward him hard enough that the candle flame jumped. "Read that without editorializing."

He did.

The gloss was maddeningly compact, but the sense of it came through. "Resident accord sufficient despite disputed claim."

She sat back.

"That's bad," he said.

"Yes."

"Because the claim dispute didn't matter so long as the residents behaved in a way the house could use."

"Because the system can apparently tolerate legal or political instability if the relational strain inside the house stays under whatever threshold it considers workable." She pressed her fingertips into the bridge of her nose. "God. That's foul."

It was more than foul. It was intimate in the ugliest possible way, not because it cared about love but because it didn't have to.

"Does it make a difference," he said slowly, "whether the accord is genuine?"

She dropped her hand. "What do you mean?"

"If two people are in step because they agree, or because one of them yields, or because they're frightened, or because the house has learned what arrangement buys the least resistance. Does the system know the difference."

Her silence landed harder than any immediate answer would have.

"I don't know," she said at last. "And I don't think the people who built it were designing for consent."

That could have been a joke about his family if she'd chosen to sharpen it that way. She didn't. She said it like a scientist identifying a contaminant in the sample.

"Neither were most of the families involved, I expect," he said.

"No."

He looked down at the line again. *Resident accord sufficient despite disputed claim.* It could have referred to a boundary inheritance, an oath conflict, a marriage negotiation, a custody matter, a servant dispute. The text didn't care. The house had taken the fact of reduced strain and recorded success.

"If we're the local mechanism," he said, "can we afford to refuse it?"

That got her eyes back on his face immediately.

"Of course we can."

"That's not what I asked."

The room went quiet enough that he could hear a charm ticking faintly in the far shelf.

"No," she said, after too long. "Maybe not without consequences beyond us. But that's different from saying it gets to decide for us."

"Is it?"

"Yes." The answer was instant now, almost angry. "It has to be. Otherwise we're letting a building set the ethical terms because it's old and useful."

"And very well insulated."

That did get a brief, unwilling change in her face. Not a smile. More like the beginning of one she had judged inappropriate and cancelled halfway through.

"Write that down too," she said.

"The insulation?"

"No, Malfoy. The actual point."

He obeyed because she was right. On the log, in the margin beside *threshold response / proximity / contact / Mark*, he wrote in a smaller hand than the rest: *cost of refusal indeterminate; utility not consent*.

She read it over his shoulder and didn't correct the wording.

The first tray appeared at twelve twenty-three.

There was no sound before it. One moment the cleared corner of the desk held nothing but dust and the shadow of stacked reference texts. The next it held a silver tray with tea, two cups, a plate of sliced pear, and a folded linen cloth still warm from whatever kitchen had been instructed without being asked.

Neither of them reached for it.

"Useful," Draco said after a moment.

"I know it's useful."

"I was agreeing with you, not the tray."

She bent over the log and wrote *unrequested refreshment* so hard the nib caught. "The timing is appalling."

"Were you hungry?"

"Yes."

"Then unfortunately the appalling tray has made a fair point."

She gave him a look sharp enough to strip varnish, then picked up the teapot and poured without another word. He waited until she'd taken the first sip before taking his own, less out of suspicion than out of a rank desire not to be first in any new form of submission. The tea was exactly strong enough. Of course it was.

Granger closed her eyes for one swallow, then opened them and said, "I hate that."

"That it's good?"

"That it knows."

He looked at the tray, at the pear slices arranged in a fan precise enough to be either courtesy or surveillance. "It has known for some time."

"I know." She set the cup down. "That's what this morning confirmed."

They ate anyway.

The domesticity of it made the problem worse. They drank tea over a page that had just told them inhabitant concordance was a functional variable. They took fruit from a silver plate while the house watched them consume the precise amount of care required to keep working. Nothing in the scene looked violent. That did not make it benign.

When the tray vanished half an hour later, taking the peels and empty cups with it, Granger did not look up from the log. She only added *cleared without request* and underlined it once.

By early afternoon their translation key had started to hold.

They identified seven recurring categories: property designation, anchoring quadrant, seasonal condition, household load, concordance state, intervention type, and outcome. The language sharpened as they worked. He began to hear where the family shorthand had been laid over the artisan terms rather than translated. She started anticipating which family abbreviations hid actual measurements instead of names. Several times their hands moved toward the same line at once and checked short before contact. Each time the hesitation sat in the air longer than it should have.

At one point she frowned over a repeated glyph and said, "This can't be direction. It doesn't track the geometry."

"It does if it's local rather than compass-based."

"No, because then the ordinal markers wouldn't—"

"You're assuming a stable frame of reference."

"In a structural assessment, yes, I am."

"That seems optimistic for medieval builders."

She glanced at him, offended into full wakefulness. "Not medieval. Fourteenth century. And no, it isn't optimistic. It's basic competence."

"From your side of the blood war, perhaps."

"What does that even mean?"

"It means the family glosses use directional language opportunistically. Look at the variant here."

"That's not a direction marker."

"It is in this hand."

"No, it's seasonal flow. The stroke opens downward, see? If it were directional it would close on the return."

They stared at the page together.

Then, from somewhere behind the shelves, came a soft internal thump. Not loud. Not dramatic. Just enough of a sound to make both of them look up at the same instant.

Granger's expression changed first. She snatched the log toward her and wrote *room response during disagreement?* with quick, furious strokes.

"You heard it," she said.

"Yes."

"Good. I didn't want that to be only me."

He looked from the note to the page they'd been arguing over. "Could be settling."

"Could be." She blew on the wet ink. "Could also be a house that prefers resolved conditions."

"Perhaps it simply admires your competence."

"Don't flatter it."

He went back to the glyph. The page swam for half a second, not enough to count as magic, maybe only enough to count as irritation. Then he saw what she'd seen and grimaced. "Fine. Seasonal flow."

"Thank you."

The sound did not repeat.

They both looked at the shelves anyway.

"I am not," she said, still staring into the room, "going to fake agreement for a building."

"That had been my next suggestion."

"I know."

"And?"

"And if you let me win a point I haven't actually won, the translation key becomes useless." She tapped the margin note she'd just made. "If the house reacts to concordance, I want to know whether it reacts to truth or just to the shape of reduced friction. I'm not contaminating the data because the room likes us compliant."

He should have been able to make a joke out of that. There was one waiting, something about her being catastrophically unromantic at precisely the wrong moment. It died before he said it. Nothing in her face suggested room for deflection.

"Right," he said.

"Right."

She nudged the disputed page toward him again. "And write down that the response stopped after resolution."

"You really don't believe in half measures."

"Not where sentient architecture is concerned, no."

He wrote it down.

The work that followed went slower and better.

It went slower because every convenience had to be assessed before it was used. It went better because naming the danger had made their collaboration sharper rather than blunter. If the house wanted concordance, it was getting a version built out of actual scholarship and mutual irritation, not dreamy compliance. They checked each other harder. They challenged shortcuts. They used the surfaced books, but only after cross-referencing them against older sources she pulled herself from the shelves and volumes he identified from family sigils burned faintly into the leather. When the room warmed, it went in the log. When a shelf lit before either of them reached for a wand, it went in the log. When his Mark eased after ten straight minutes of working shoulder to shoulder over a knot of shorthand too cramped to parse at a distance, that went in the log too, though he resented dictating it and she resented writing it.

By four o'clock the page of observations had become as dense as the translation key.

Some patterns emerged anyway.

Distance alone was not the whole variable. Once, while they remained three feet apart, the room steadied noticeably when she solved a structural ambiguity he had been dragging in the wrong direction and he acknowledged it without argument. Another time their chairs were nearly touching, but when a line about property succession sent them into an ugly twelve-minute dispute over whether a compressed family mark indicated claim or custody, the candles in the west niche burned low and blue until they reached a reading they both accepted. Granger added *response may track reduced interpretive strain, not merely proximity* and underlined *may* twice.

"So it doesn't just want bodies in a room," he said.

"No." She rubbed ink off the side of her hand with the heel of her palm. "It may want coherence. Or what's functionally close enough to coherence."

"Still charming."

"Yes."

He watched her rewrite a translation from scratch because she no longer trusted the earlier version, and understood suddenly why the work felt better with the ugliness named than it had when the room was trying to coax them into comfort. Hidden leverage wanted passivity. Seen leverage had to contend with Granger, which was bad luck for any system stupid enough to think comfort alone would do the job.

The first real breakthrough came over a page neither of them had been reading correctly.

He caught the family hand first. A genealogical marker buried in the margin, almost casual. "Draco Malfoy, sixteenth of his name," he murmured. "Cousin line, not mine. They split after Braccus. Normandy and Wiltshire."

"Site supervisor?" she asked.

"I think so. Look. His notes sit between the artisans' marks and the family script. He's translating both directions."

She shifted closer before catching herself, then came the rest of the way anyway with an expression that made it clear the movement had been chosen, not surrendered. Her shoulder stopped a breath from his. Together they studied the line.

"If he was mediating," she said, "then this whole document isn't a pure family register. And it isn't a pure trade register either. It's a working hybrid."

"A creole."

"Yes." Her eyes stayed on the page, though her voice had gone softer. "A project language. Temporary, practical, and probably uncodified anywhere else."

"So the only way to rebuild it is to do what they did."

"Put both knowledge sets in the same room and see what sticks."

There it was again, the thing the house wanted and the thing he wanted and the fact that those were not, disastrously, mutually exclusive.

He said, because he needed the sentence to land somewhere less dangerous, "Convenient for the Manor."

"Useful for the work," she corrected automatically, then stopped. "No. That's lazy. It's useful for both, probably."

"Which one are we serving."

"Today?" She went quiet for a second, then answered without looking at him. "Both. I don't know in what ratio."

That was honest enough to make his pulse change.

They stayed bent over the line until it yielded the rest of its sense. The cousin-line supervisor had been recording mediation between resident authority and field correction teams. Family claim established access. Artisan procedure established stability. Neither set of knowledge was sufficient without the other. The note itself was dry. The implication wasn't.

"There never was a version of this work that happened alone," Draco said.

"Apparently not."

The room, as if pleased by the conclusion, let a second candelabrum kindle itself in the east niche.

Granger closed her eyes.

"Write that down," she said.

He did.

By twilight the table looked like the scene of a very orderly siege. Three manuscripts lay open at competing angles. Their central key had become a sheet and a half. The log of house responses had spread to a second page. Draco's handwriting, which usually ran clean and economical, had shrunk tighter and tighter beside hers until the difference between their notes looked less like separate authorship and more like a conversation being forced to live on parchment because speech could no longer keep up.

Fresh candles waited on a shelf just inside the threshold. This time the house had not placed them directly on the table, perhaps having understood from the log that naked helpfulness was now being entered into evidence. Draco fetched them himself. By the time he turned back from lighting them, she'd shifted the desk arrangement: his folio beside hers, reference texts arced between the chairs, space cleared in the middle for a shared index of the symbol families they'd identified.

She had done it absentmindedly, probably. It still knocked him off balance.

"What?" she asked when she caught him looking.

"You reorganized me."

"I made room for the other manuscript."

"Very noble of you."

Something on her face loosened for a second, dry and unwilling and much closer to amusement than the rest of her ever admitted in public. Then it was gone. "You were spreading."

"A terrible accusation."

"And true."

He sat again. The chairs were closer now because there was no longer room for them not to be. She glanced once at the log, at the latest notations on distance and response, and then deliberately did not move either chair.

That decision changed the air more than a confession would have.

Evening came down hard outside the windows. Inside, the archive narrowed to candlelight, open texts, chalky air, and the friction of two minds refusing to let each other be lazy. They argued for nineteen minutes over whether a compressed glyph marked seasonal variance or directional load transfer, only to realize the answer was both, dependent on the accompanying property sign. They found a sequence of baseline entries clean enough to prove the southern anchoring records would matter more than the northern ones when they came back tomorrow. They traced one ugly annotation through three different hands before discovering it referred not to repair failure but to a resident oath that had lapsed and been renewed. They did not discuss the implications of that one beyond the strictly necessary, perhaps because the implications were standing between them at the table already.

At some point the house produced a wool throw over the back of the chair Granger wasn't using.

She stared at it for a full five seconds.

"Absolutely not," she said to the empty room.

The throw remained where it was.

Draco looked from the neatly folded fabric to her face. "Do you want me to write down that the house has developed a sense of affront."

"I want you to write down that it is escalating."

"Those are not mutually exclusive."

"Malfoy."

He wrote *unsolicited comfort object* in the margin while she glared at the throw as if daring it to advance. Five minutes later, without a word, she dragged it across her lap with the tip of a quill and kept reading.

He said nothing. So did she.

The intimacy of that silence was different from the one that had lived between them earlier in the book. It was not the brittle quiet of two people guarding territory. It was the quiet of work shared so long and so closely that speech had become selective. He could tell from the way her shoulders angled when she was about to object. She could tell, apparently, from the movement of his hand before he reached for a reference. Twice he went for a book and stopped because she had already slid it toward the center. Once she started to ask for a measurement table and cut herself off because he was already there.

The work was making a language out of them. The house liked that far too much.

So did he.

That ought to have been the disqualifying detail. Instead it sat in him with the kind of clarity that made denial look adolescent.

Later, while she reread a passage they'd finally cracked, the pencil gave up and her curls fell loose around her face. One foot was tucked under her. A hole showed at the toe of her sock. She did not notice him noticing. She almost never noticed the things about herself that made him feel most helpless.

The name for it arrived cleanly: love.

He was not falling. It was not the start of anything. It was a fact, and once he let himself see it, it became the plainest one in the room.

And the morning's discovery did not make it smaller. That was the problem. If anything, the uglier logic of the house exposed the feeling more brutally by proving what it wasn't. The Manor might exploit proximity. It might reward concordance. It might ease his pain when she sat near enough and punish distance with a precision that felt obscene. None of that explained the hole in her sock. None of it explained the way he wanted her notes left in unstable piles on his table, or the exact sound of her voice when she said *no, that's lazy* to a bad inference, or the stupid amount of tenderness called up by seeing her ignore her own exhaustion because a line of fourteenth-century notation had offended her.

He had spent years building a life narrow enough to survive in. He ate alone. He kept corridors empty. He reduced friendships to the few people who could bear his silences. Need had always been the dangerous part. Need was leverage. Need made a person visible.

Now he wanted ridiculous, ordinary things. He wanted tea brought without ceremony and cursed on sight for being too well timed. He wanted her coat on the hook outside the library because she'd stopped pretending she wasn't staying late. He wanted her shoulder against his when the script turned ugly. He wanted the argument that lasted nineteen minutes because neither of them would let the other get away with being half right. He wanted to touch her outside the sanctioned brutality of the Mark examinations. He wanted a hand at her back as she passed, his fingers in her hair, contact that belonged to a life rather than to damage.

And because the morning had happened, he could not tell himself that wanting those things was ethically simple. The house had turned even innocence into a variable. Which meant every choice now had to survive being chosen anyway.

"Malfoy."

He looked up.

The folio sat open in her lap, forgotten for the moment. Attention had replaced concentration on her face, alert and careful both. She had caught him staring.

"You're doing it again," she said.

"I know."

That should have been the point where he looked away and made a joke durable enough to hide behind. It should have been the point where she used the work to step cleanly around whatever this was becoming. Instead neither of them moved.

The air between them went very thin.

Then she said, with the kind of precision that meant she had noticed more than she wanted to admit, "You've got that look you get right before you stop listening to anything I say."

"That seems unfair."

"Is it?"

"Possibly not."

Her gaze flicked once to the log lying open beside them. *proximity / contact / Mark*. Then back to his face.

He said, because anything else felt more dangerous, "You've got ink on your chin."

Her hand went to the wrong side of her face.

Some part of him understood exactly what the room would make of the next movement. That part suggested very firmly that he remain still. Another part, older or newer or simply more tired of cowardice, ignored it.

Before he could reconsider, he reached out and brushed the smudge away with his thumb.

Her skin was warm.

She stayed perfectly still.

Somewhere in the wall behind the eastern shelves, a hidden latch eased with a sound so soft he might have imagined it if her eyes had not changed at the exact same moment. She had heard it too.

Neither of them mentioned it.

"There," he said, quieter than he meant to.

"Thanks." Her voice was careful now, but not retreating. Her eyes did not leave his.

He should have taken his hand back at once. He did, eventually. It felt like taking it out of a flame by stages.

For another beat she looked at him as if deciding whether the next sentence ought to be personal or procedural. Procedure won, but only just.

"Can you check the property markers on the northeast section?" she asked. "I think this entry matches the inspection sequence, but the branch sigil is wrong."

"Yes."

Work resumed.

The change was slight and undeniable. Her chair stayed where it was. When their shoulders touched again, no one shifted away. The log remained open at the edge of the table with its ugly columns and damp ink and all the evidence they'd accumulated against the room. None of it stopped them.

If anything, that was the most compromising thing in it.

The next hour produced what they had actually come here for: not answers, but a way forward. They isolated the notation cluster that marked baseline readings prior to major strain. They identified the southern anchoring sequence as the cleanest surviving run because later damage had hit the northern quadrants harder. They built enough of a working lexicon to tell the difference between failure in the structure and failure in the resident field around it. That distinction alone would save them days.

"Tomorrow," Granger said, tapping the heading they'd finally broken open, "we start here. Southern records first. If the baseline holds, we can compare the postwar variance to the current decay maps."

"And keep the log."

"Obviously."

"You sounded offended that I'd ask."

"I am."

He let that settle, watching her stack the loose pages in order of use rather than chronology, which was how he had always known she was more tired than she admitted. Her fingers had gone ink-stiff. A dark smear crossed the side of her hand. There was another one, faint, at her jaw where he'd missed a spot after all. He wanted absurdly to fix that too.

"Granger."

Her notes paused in her hands.

This was the point where he ought to have said something measured and safe about the research. The key they'd built. The efficiency of the day. The value of tomorrow's work. Instead he heard himself say the truer thing.

"This is the best work I've ever done."

The admission came out rougher than he had planned. Perhaps because he had not planned it at all.

"I wanted you to know that," he added.

For a moment she only looked at him. The room did not dare make a sound.

Then, very quietly, "Yeah. Me too."

He should have let her go with that. He should have let the sentence stand on its own and not reached for more. But the day had already established that restraint and safety were not the same thing, and cowardice had bought him very little for years.

"Leave the southern folio here," he said. "I'll have dinner sent down tomorrow and we'll keep going."

That got a fuller look. Not shocked. Not even wary, exactly. Measured.

He added, because the distinction mattered now, "On purpose this time."

Something in her face shifted at that. Not softness. Recognition, maybe.

"All right," she said.

She gathered her notes, but not all of them. The log and the central key stayed on the table between their chairs, pages weighted with an inkstone as if both of them understood without saying it that tonight's work could not yet be packed away into innocence.

At the threshold she stopped and looked back once, not at him at first but at the room: the candles, the open manuscripts, the chair she'd left too close to his. Then she looked at him.

"If it starts asking for more than we're prepared to give," she said, "we stop."

She offered no qualification and no theory language to soften it.

"Yes," he said.

She nodded once and went.

After the door shut behind her, the archive stayed open a few seconds too long, holding the threshold wide and the candles steady as if it expected him to call her back. That patience no longer felt innocent.

He did not move at first. He listened to her steps recede into the outer library and then into whatever route the house had chosen to offer her tonight. He listened until even the echo of them was gone.

Then he looked down at the page they'd been working on and tried, stupidly, to read the last line alone.

The first two words yielded: a family designation and an outcome mark. The third went dead under his eye. It was not blank. It had not changed. It had simply closed in a way that felt almost bodily, like a door pushed to but not latched. He tried again with the key they'd built together, managed another fragment, and lost the thread on the next line entirely.

The room seemed to notice.

The warmth at his back thinned just enough to raise the hairs along his neck. The brass lattice at the threshold gave the faintest preparatory tick.

His Mark answered before the door did with one sharp pull, deep enough to make his fingers curl against the page.

He sat very still and hated how much the pain felt like information.

So that, too, went into the evidence, this time in the body rather than on parchment. One of them could remain in the room alone for a moment now, perhaps, but it could not hold its center that way. Not yet. The work wanted both knowledge sets. The house wanted whatever alignment let it keep using them. And he, apparently, wanted her enough that those two facts had become impossible to sort cleanly from each other.

He could not tell whether that last part was the room's triumph or his own.

On the table, the log lay open to the final exchange they'd written together. *cost of refusal indeterminate; utility not consent.* Below it, in Granger's hand, from later in the evening: *continue only by deliberate choice*.

He read that twice.

Then he reached over, turned the log face down, and snuffed the candles one by one.

The archive door had begun to draw inward by the third flame. By the fifth it was almost closed. He left both folios spread on the table and did not move her chair back across from his.


# Chapter 15

By midnight they still hadn't forced the Levett manuscript to yield.

They'd been at it since dinner, or since what had passed for dinner: a tray sent down at seven and abandoned in stages over the next four hours until the tea went tannic and the bread hardened at the edges. Hermione had eaten most of the cheese without noticing. Malfoy had eaten whatever his hand found first, bread, then the sliced apple she'd forgotten she'd cut, then the heel of a loaf gone dry near the fire. At some point the house-elves had replaced the teapot without interrupting them. At some later point he had nudged her plate back within reach after she let it drift under a stack of parchment. Neither of them had thanked the other for any of it. The careful politeness of the first weeks had thinned enough that these small acts now passed between them unannounced, as if naming them would make them fragile again.

The text itself looked like the work of a man who despised posterity. It was a fourteenth-century construction manual for anchoring infrastructure, written by a Malfoy called Levett only because one branch of the family had briefly used maternal names for younger sons. Marginal annotations ran in three languages and at least two hands. The primary text used runic modifiers neither of them had seen independently, but cross-reference between her theoretical framework and his family knowledge of the symbols had made the whole thing almost legible.

For the past forty minutes, one passage had refused to yield.

"It's not 'binding,'" she said, frowning at the glyph cluster in the center of the page. "The radical is wrong. It's more like threading. Weaving through?"

"Plaiting." He sat close enough that their shoulders nearly met every time they leaned over the text, which was often. Cedar soap clung to him, and beneath it lived the mineral tang of the deep archive, preservation charms layered so thick in the air that everything tasted faintly of chalk. All evening his handwriting had kept shrinking in the margins of their working translation, tighter and denser with each hour. "The secondary radical modifies it. It's a textile metaphor. My great-great-however-many-times grandmother was apparently a weaver before she married into the name."

"How do you know that?"

"There's a tapestry. Third floor. She wove it herself, and my mother made sure I knew, because..." The sentence stopped there. Nearly midnight, and whatever had almost followed was more than he meant to hand over. "Because apparently every dead Malfoy must remain educational."

That made her smile. "Public service through embroidery."

"Textiles," he said, as if accuracy could protect him from the joke. "Apparently."

So plaiting was the right word.

Under that reading, the anchoring magic described threads drawn through substrate, through land and ambient field, then braided into a structure that held. It was not bound or hammered into place. It was woven. At once the decay looked different: not masonry failing but fabric coming apart, thread after thread slipping loose.

"That works," she said. "Look. If you read it as plaiting, then this section follows. The whole anchoring system is textile structure. The ley-line connections aren't channels, they're threads. And war damage would be..."

"Cuts. Tears in the fabric."

"Which means the Ministry's been trying to mend a tapestry by building scaffolding around it. No wonder nothing's held."

He pulled a second text toward him, a later manuscript in Latin with French glosses. The page he wanted had been marked with a strip of parchment in his own hand two days ago. Without thinking about it, she watched him scan the Latin. His reading speed there beat hers, one of the few places pureblood education had outpaced her own. Sometime in the third week, she'd stopped resenting that and started relying on it. "This passage references a repair method," he said. "Rethreading. Actually running new thread through the existing warp instead of patching over damage."

"You'd need access to the original structure. The loom, essentially."

"Which would be the anchoring points themselves."

"Which are..."

"Under the house. Among other places."

Across the desk their eyes met. The implication settled with the same weight so many things had been carrying between them lately. What they found in this library was going to matter beyond the two of them and beyond this room.

"We should map them."

"Not tonight." He pushed his chair back a fraction and stretched until his neck cracked.

"If I stop now I'll lie awake rebuilding the notation in my head."

"You already do that."

"I do not."

He looked down at the manuscript instead of at her. "Last Tuesday you informed an imaginary review committee that it had confused depth with direction."

Heat rose into her face. "I was half asleep."

"You were indignant in your sleep."

"I'm usually indignant awake. There's no reason to waste the talent."

That got the breath of a laugh she had learned to look for and never to press. He scrubbed one hand over his mouth, considering the page and the hour as if the books might rescue him from the fact that he had already given in.

"Twenty minutes," he said.

It took closer to forty just to get the surveyor's case down.

The second gallery ran along the north wall above the reading floor, iron rail cold under Hermione's fingers and the upper shelves sunk in a dustier dark than the lamps below reached comfortably. From up there the library looked smaller and stranger. The reading table where they'd been working made a bright island in the middle of the room, cluttered with cups, parchment, and the loose domestic debris of two people who no longer remembered to stage-manage each other's presence. Their chairs sat too close. Her cardigan hung over the back of his because she'd shed it hours ago and neither of them had moved it. The view of their evening, seen from above, made it look less like research and more like a life.

He had already wedged himself into a narrow gap between folios of property disputes and seventeenth-century drainage schemes by the time she came up beside him. "You weren't meant to follow."

"You said twenty minutes. I wanted to watch you fail efficiently."

"That's ungenerous."

"It's accurate."

The leather case had been shoved behind a row of linen-backed rolls and one heavy atlas of tenant boundaries. He pulled once and nothing moved. The second attempt got the case half free and the atlas sliding toward the rail.

Hermione caught the atlas before it fell. "Do you want help or would that wound your bloodline?"

"The bloodline's been wounded plenty." He shifted to make room, which on the narrow gallery meant her stepping almost into him. "Take the left side."

The case came free with a gritty scrape. Dust shook loose over both of them. He coughed into his sleeve and looked offended, as if ancestral paper ought to have behaved with more decorum.

"What were you doing with surveyor's records at twelve?" she asked while they wrestled the weight of it between them.

"Hiding from a dinner party."

"That part I believed already."

He kept his attention on the case as they maneuvered it toward the stair. "The adults were boring and the children were worse. Theo had discovered he could set the peacocks fighting if he whistled through his teeth. Pansy was helping. Blaise was pretending not to help. I opened the wrong cabinet and found maps."

"And that was preferable?"

"The maps stayed where they'd been put." He waited a beat before adding, "Mostly."

That answer landed lower than the joke warranted. She looked at him, but he was already descending ahead of her with the case, shoulders tight under the weight.

They set it on the reading table with a thud heavy enough to upset the spoons in both teacups. The brass latches stuck for a moment before giving. Inside lay rolled vellum plans, folded linen surveys, depth tables, tenant boundaries, drainage schematics, and a brass divider in a fitted slot lined with green baize gone black at the edges. Tabs in three different hands labeled sections by century. Malfoy lifted one roll with the wary competence of someone who'd been taught archival manners before he was old enough to object to them.

"This is absurd," Hermione said softly.

"That narrows nothing."

"No, I mean this specifically. Your family indexed six hundred years of land management and then wrote the real information in code around the edges."

"If it's any consolation, we also did that to one another."

He said it lightly, but not lightly enough.

They spread the first vellum plan across the table and discovered at once that the table was too small. A second plan followed. Then a third. Within minutes the reading floor had become an improvised worksite. She sat cross-legged on the rug weighting one corner with the Latin manuscript while he knelt opposite with a stack of boundary rolls pinning another. He found the pre-war estate survey at last, linen-backed and brittle at the folds, the Manor rendered in hard ink lines that still assumed walls were honest and corridors meant what they measured.

Hermione laid a sheet of tracing vellum over it and began copying the nodi from Levett's notation while Malfoy translated depth marks from family shorthand into proper measurements. The work moved quickly once the principle clicked. Knot-points sat where plaited lines converged, not at decorative centers but at structural junctions: below the eastern library wall, under the old central stair, beneath a stretch of corridor that fed into the guest wing, and farther out under service passages that no longer existed on the present floor plan. Every point mapped to age, load, and repetition, to places people crossed often and the house had reason to keep live.

"If Levett's right, repair isn't reinforcement," Hermione said, not looking up from the vellum. "It's rethreading load through surviving structure. Which means every Ministry team working with buttresses and brace charms has been addressing symptoms."

"Not causes."

"Yes." Her pencil moved faster. "If the filum has frayed at the nodes, then all the stabilization work they've done is cosmetic. It would hold long enough to generate a report and fail again the moment the pressure returned. If we can prove that here, the rest of the network stops looking impossible."

"Comforting."

"I didn't say it was comforting."

He gave her a look over the map edge that would once have counted as dry and now carried too much tiredness to manage dryness properly. "No. You rarely do."

She found herself smiling again, brief and unwilling. The pencil kept moving. One line from the eastern knot-point ran under the library toward the deeper archive. Another fed north before bending in a way that made no sense on the present plan until she unrolled a second survey and saw the older corridor arrangement beneath later renovations. The guest wing had not been built where it now sat. The rooms had shifted functions over generations, but the lines underneath had remained stubborn about where paired traffic wanted to travel.

"Look at this," she said.

He leaned over the vellum. Their heads nearly knocked. "Guest wing junction."

"It should be farther west."

"It was, on the earlier plan."

"Not just the plan." She reached for the older survey and flattened it with her palm. "Here. The original corridor run is longer, then the later survey shortens it after the east wall alterations. But the knot-point doesn't move with the wall. It stays at the same load point. The architecture changed around it."

His eyes tracked the overlay. "Meaning?"

"Meaning the house may be routing movement toward the loaded points rather than away from them." She heard how that sounded and forced herself back into the language of work. "For maintenance. Or because those paths carry more ambient traffic. Or both."

He was still looking at the map, but the stillness in him changed. "You think it's deliberate."

She almost said, *Of course it's deliberate. I know it is.* Instead she picked up the brass divider and measured the distance between the library threshold and the guest-wing bend on the survey. "I think the plan says one thing and the house keeps doing another. There's a difference."

"There's also a difference," he said, "between a corridor shifting by a few feet and the Manor making policy choices."

"You say that as though the corridor business is ordinary."

"In this house?" He lifted one shoulder. "Less ordinary than creaking pipes, more ordinary than I'd prefer."

That irritated her for reasons too numerous and too hypocritical to sort cleanly. "How long have you known the route changes that much?"

He took too long to answer. "A while."

"And you didn't mention it."

"You were already conducting research in a sentient estate built into Britain's failing magical infrastructure. I wasn't sure 'the corridor's in a mood' would improve your morale."

"You might have let me decide that."

The reply came faster than she expected. "You might have told me if you were worried."

Silence answered that. He had not meant to say it. She had no honest answer to offer.

He looked back down first, rearranging the surveyor's rolls into needless straightness. She could feel the moment wanting to turn into apology or retreat. She did not want either.

"Come on," she said.

His eyes lifted. "Where?"

"To count."

He stared at her as if assessing whether fatigue had finally pushed her into a form of madness he ought to respect. Then he rose without argument, because curiosity had become their least dangerous shared vice.

The corridor outside the library was warm enough to make the cold brass latch sweat against Hermione's hand. Sconces had already brightened ahead of them, throwing light farther down the passage than they had when she'd come up from dinner. She marked the library threshold with the tip of her wand, a thin blue line only they would see, and checked the survey again.

"To the turn by the guest wing door," she said.

"I know where my own corridor turns."

"Good. Then you'll know if it cheats."

He made a quiet noise that might have been amusement. They started walking.

The first count came too quickly. Twenty-six paces took them to the bend where the passage should still have had another stretch of wall before Hermione's rooms began. The sconce nearest the turn lit as they approached, bright and eager. Beyond it, the carpet runner the house had laid down in November continued in the exact blue-grey Narcissa would have chosen if warmth could masquerade as restraint.

Hermione stopped and checked the survey again. The drawn distance and the lived one would not reconcile.

"Again," she said.

"That wasn't enough proof?"

"No."

They turned immediately and walked back toward the library. The return took thirty-two.

At pace twenty-nine she looked up, certain she had lost count. The blue wand-mark glimmered ahead, still three strides away.

Malfoy stopped when she did. He had come close enough that the heat of him touched the air at her back without contact. For a strange second she was more aware of that than of the corridor.

"You noticed," he said.

"That it changed under us? Yes."

"I meant before tonight."

She turned. He was holding the survey rolled under one arm, thumb pressed white against the linen edge. No irony left in his face at all.

"I've noticed the walk shorten," she said carefully. "I didn't know whether to call that architecture, fatigue, or the house trying to be useful."

"Those aren't mutually exclusive."

"No." Her voice came out thinner than she liked. "Apparently not."

The corridor held itself around them with that attentive, almost-polite warmth the Manor had been favoring for weeks. The guest-wing door stood closed at the end of the short run. Behind her, the library waited open. The house had pulled the two spaces too near each other and was acting as though proximity were kindness.

If the knot-point lay here, then the shortening was not merely convenience. It was routing through repetition, a path walked often enough to matter. Paired inhabitants brought over the same threaded span until the house could read them by movement alone.

Her stomach turned. The insight arrived dressed as theory, but it hit lower than theory.

"Granger."

She realized he had said it without the usual dry edge, almost gently.

"What?"

"You're doing that thing again."

She knew precisely which thing he meant: going still, leaving the room while her body stayed in it. "I'm thinking."

"Dangerous habit."

"Shut up."

That landed better than it should have. The corner of his mouth shifted. It wasn't quite a smile, more the memory of one.

Then his gaze dropped to the blue line by the threshold, to the impossible distance between it and where they stood, and the humor left him again. "If the route's changing around the knot-point..."

"Then the architecture isn't just sitting on the infrastructure." She forced the words into the driest shape available. "It's participating in maintenance. Or trying to."

"And that's good?"

"I didn't say that either."

He nodded once. The wand-mark threw a faint light between them.

"We need the estate cross-index," she said. "The survey tells us parcel and depth, but without the ley-line correspondence table we're guessing at which lower runs feed which rooms."

"There may be one in the east-wall records." He glanced back toward the library. "Third unit, if the house hasn't buried it behind a wall of tedious parcel ledgers."

"Tomorrow, then."

"Tomorrow."

They stood another second too long before moving. On the way back the route shortened again. Neither of them counted aloud this time. They didn't need to.

Inside the library, Hermione wrote both pace counts in the margin of her working notes and underlined them twice. Malfoy resettled the surveys with the careful irritation of a man pretending paper required all of his focus. The room had shifted during their absence. Another lamp burned at the side table though neither of them had lit it. A fresh pot waited beside the kettle. Two more cups sat out.

Hermione stared at them. "Did you ask for that?"

"No."

"Convenient."

"That's one word for it."

She wanted to banish the spare cups, then realized the gesture would be theatrical and childish and therefore tempting. Instead she sat again and pulled the Levett manuscript toward her. Malfoy poured tea into the cups the house had provided as if refusing the tea would grant the house a moral victory it had not earned.

The tea was exactly right. It always was now. Milk, brew time, the small bitter strength she preferred late at night. He had never asked. He'd simply observed until the cup he made matched the cup she made herself, and the precision of that care still landed in her body like an injury she had chosen.

They worked for another hour.

The maintenance protocol turned out to be worse than the construction notes, not because it was more obscure but because it assumed a body of family knowledge Levett had seen no reason to explain. Rethreading required access to the old load points, yes, but it also required sequence. Disturb the wrong knot first and tension redistributed badly through the remaining spans. The later Latin glosses helped only enough to make disaster legible. Hermione copied sequence markers while Malfoy hunted corroboration in property notes, his face drawn with the effort of holding archaic shorthand and live inference in the same place.

At intervals they cross-checked the maps. The eastern library node fed downward toward the archive and outward toward the corridor they'd just walked. A second line ran under older family rooms no one used now. A third vanished beneath renovations that had outlived the logic behind them. The house was not a neutral shell around the network. It was one of the knots, old enough and inhabited enough to have learned preferences of its own.

If they could map the Manor cleanly, they might have the first workable repair sequence anyone had found since the crisis became impossible to dismiss. The thought should have steadied her. It usually did. Instead each breakthrough arrived twined with the same private knowledge she still hadn't handed over: the house preferred paired inhabitants, widened awareness, pressed on attachment already present. She knew too much to greet any new proof cleanly.

Midnight passed without announcement except in their bodies. Her neck had gone stiff. The bridge of his nose held the red mark of two fingers pressed there too often. Ink darkened the side of her hand. Dust tracked his sleeve where the surveyor's case had fought him on the gallery shelf. The fire in the grate burned low and neither of them bothered rebuilding it because the Manor had been warm for days now, walls holding heat with the steady generosity of a house that wanted its inhabitants comfortable. Outside, December did what December did in Wiltshire: frost on the grounds, dark since four, wind finding old window joins and making that thin winter sound she'd started associating with these late hours.

Around them the library had settled into what she'd started thinking of as its real self. It was not the sunlit room she'd catalogued in early November, when every shelf had looked like evidence and every chair like a diplomatic problem. At night it drew inward. Lamplight shortened the room. Shelves came closer. Silence populated itself. The windows reflected them back at themselves, bent by age in the glass so that their heads hovered over maps and manuscripts like figures in a painting about scholars too tired to stop.

Across from her, his hand went to the back of his neck. Lamplight caught the edge of the Mark below his rolled sleeve, and she looked away. By touch she knew it better than by sight. Tonight it sat in its quiet register, not pain exactly, only a low awareness under her skin of his body's condition and location.

That was the problem. She knew what she knew.

Weeks ago, in the deeper archive, she'd found the bonding texts that named this sensation with clinical precision: resonance, the growing inability to keep your own physical state cleanly separate from the other person's. The Manor's architecture amplified what already existed between its inhabitants and gated access behind the strength of the bond. She had read the folio twice. She understood more of the mechanism than he did. She had not told him. Since then they had sat like this every evening, close over manuscripts, elbows almost touching, while the knowledge stayed in her like swallowed glass, present always and sharp whenever she moved wrong.

Tonight had made concealment worse. It was one thing to keep silent while the evidence stayed subtle: shortened routes, warmth, surfaced books, the low spill of his pain through the Mark. It was another to stand in a corridor that had altered beneath their feet and go back to the table without saying, *I know why the house cares whether we're side by side. I knew before you did. I let it keep happening anyway.*

"You've gone quiet," he said.

"I'm thinking about loom mechanics."

"You're a terrible liar."

"I'm an excellent liar, actually." The answer was out before she could stop it, and the truth inside it made her grip tighten on the pen until the barrel bit into her fingers. Back to the manuscript. Back to the page. "I'm thinking about sequencing. If you disturb one knot-point too early, you could pull strain through the whole house."

On that subject he did what he almost always did: asked once, accepted the deflection, let the silence stand without worrying it into injury. Harry would have circled back. Ron, once upon a time, would have circled back. Malfoy let unanswered things remain unanswered. It made the space between them more bearable. It made concealment easier. Both facts felt terrible.

Her own marginal notes had deteriorated over the past weeks, faster and smaller and full of abbreviations that only made sense if you'd been following the work from the beginning. His were neater, just as dense, and she had started reading them with the same attention she gave the primary text itself: another mind recording how it moved, trusting her to keep up.

"This section," he said at last, angling the text toward her with one hand. His index finger rested under a line of archaic notation. "I think it's maintenance protocol. The language shifts. Imperative, not descriptive. Instructions rather than theory."

Leaning in should have been nothing. His finger was on the page. The notation was dense, the glyphs cramped, and she tilted her head to catch them properly. Instead his face was there, closer than the text required. Lamplight traced his jaw. Shadow lay under his lashes. The notation blurred because he had gone very still.

He was looking at her.

He was not looking at the page. He was not looking at the text. He was looking at her.

For weeks she had been pretending not to see that expression when it flashed across his face and vanished again: the unguarded thing, neither dry nor careful nor precise, only want.

Any sensible version of herself should have leaned back. A comment about the notation would have done it. So would a remark about the hour, the maps, the cross-index, anything that could have rebuilt the distance they'd been maintaining like a bandage over a wound neither of them wanted to inspect. She knew what the Manor did. She knew what the connection between them had become. A few weeks of concealed knowledge sat behind her eyes telling her that every moment of closeness was suspect, that the warmth might be architecture rather than desire, that the pull between them might be amplification rather than the plain fact of wanting someone.

Still, she didn't lean back.

His mouth was slightly open. A dry place marked his lower lip where he bit it while concentrating. Weeks ago she'd catalogued that detail without asking why. In the quiet she could hear his breathing.

"Granger." His voice came out lower than usual, roughened by restraint rather than speech.

"What?"

"I'm not..." He stopped. Of course he stopped. Self-correction lived in him too, except his always pulled toward less. There was nowhere to retreat now, though. Her face was inches from his. The page lay between them, ignored by both of them. "Never mind."

Her hand went over his on the manuscript.

For a second neither of them breathed. Under her palm his skin felt warm and dry with chalk dust from the preservation charms. Then he turned his hand beneath hers, not away but over, until his palm met hers and his fingers closed around hers. Parchment crumpled under the joined weight of their hands. Neither of them cared.

"I don't know what I'm doing." No research voice in that. No competent, measured version of herself. "I don't, I should..."

He kissed her.

Nothing about it was smooth. His mouth found hers at the wrong angle. Teeth caught her lip. He sucked in a startled breath through his nose. One hand tightened around hers while the other came up and hovered at her jaw, not quite touching, as if he were still waiting for permission or for his body to catch up with what it had already done. A startled sound left her, low and unmanageable. She leaned in. The angle corrected. Then his mouth was on hers properly, clumsy still, honestly clumsy, nose knocking hers, tension hard in his jaw when her free hand rose to him without conscious decision.

She had imagined composure. She had imagined his precision extending here too. It didn't. When his hand finally landed on her jaw it gripped too firmly, fingers pressing into the soft skin below her ear with more need than tenderness, and she liked that immediately, liked the plain honesty of his body speaking before the rest of him could interfere. Her free hand caught in the front of his shirt. Through the cotton his heart hammered.

At the same moment the resonance along the Mark went taut.

It did not get louder. It went total. His heartbeat seemed to strike inside her own chest. Adrenaline moved through him and some part of her answered as if it were hers. Under his sleeve the Mark shifted into a strange register, not pain, not for either of them, but a live vibrating measure that matched her pulse so exactly it made her dizzy. It was the most intimate thing she had ever felt. It was also the one she trusted least.

*Is this me?* The thought split the surface of the moment. *Is this me or is this the house?*

So she kissed him harder.

His hand slid from her jaw to the back of her neck and into her hair, which had come loose from its tie hours ago. Fingers tightened there. Her scalp tugged. By the time her mind caught up, her body had already opened to him. Tea and library dust touched his mouth, and under both was the clean mineral taste that belonged to him.

The desk made the whole thing graceless. A stack of reference texts toppled sideways. One hit the floor with a flat, heavy thud that ought to have broken the moment and didn't. Half out of her chair now, hip jammed against the desk edge, she held his shirt in one hand and his other hand in the other while maintenance protocols for ancient English magic crumpled beneath their fingers.

The house stayed warm around them. The route to her room had shortened under their feet. The Mark was still beating through both of them, too exact, too alive. She couldn't sort any of it cleanly. She kissed him again anyway.

Only an inch separated them. He stayed close, forehead to hers, breathing rough, eyes closed.

"Is this..." he started.

"Don't." She did not know whether she meant the question or the whole conversation waiting behind it. "Don't. Not yet."

This time the kiss slowed. Startle had gone out of it. His mouth moved over hers with a care he denied himself everywhere else. Then the corner of her mouth. Then her jaw. Head tipped back, throat exposed, she felt his breath there and thought, suddenly sick, *I know what the house does and I'm here anyway.*

Against her throat he said, "Granger."

Still her surname. It should have put distance back. It didn't.

Her hand went to his face, thumb along the sharp hinge of his jaw, over the faint scratch of evening stubble. He turned into the touch with his eyes still closed. No dry remark, no carefulness, just him there wanting her and not hiding it fast enough.

They stayed there longer than they should have, desk between them, dying fire at their backs, lamp nearly guttered out. The fallen book lay open on the floor. His hand stayed at the back of her neck, not moving, as if letting go too quickly might make the whole thing look mistaken.

Eventually they broke apart and sat back in their separate chairs. Cool air touched the places his hands had been. Across the desk he looked wrecked by it, open and stunned and a little afraid. She knew from the shake in her own hands that she couldn't have looked much better.

"I should go."

"Right." His hand went through his hair and left it standing up wrong. For a moment he looked younger, or maybe just less assembled.

Automatically she started gathering notes, closing the manuscript, recapping the inkwell, aligning loose pages that did not need aligning. A tremor ran through both hands. He noticed. He did not comment. Even that silence lodged in her throat.

"Tomorrow," she said. "The knot-points. And the cross-index."

"Tomorrow."

They stood. The library answered with a small settling sound from the wood and stone around them, like the house taking a breath.

"Goodnight, Granger."

"Goodnight."

Outside, corridor sconces burned low. Flames dipped as she passed and rose again behind her. Lately the walk to the guest wing had been doing this, compressing itself, making five minutes into three. Tonight the turns arrived almost before she reached them. The house could tell she was tired, apparently, or wanted her back in bed as quickly as possible. The thought should have made her furious. Instead it landed with a sickening trace of gratitude.

No chance of sleep, though. Her lips still felt bruised from him. One hand carried the ghost of his hand inside it. With every step away from the library, the last hour sharpened instead of fading.

---

Her room had been prepared for her.

Fire burned in the grate though she hadn't left it lit. Bedcovers had been turned down. The lamp beside the bed glowed at its lowest setting. Lavender threaded the air, new and unmistakably deliberate. For ten long seconds she stood inside the doorway with her palm flat to the frame, caught between laughter and the urge to scream.

The door locked under her wand. After that came the deadbolt, because a piece of metal sliding into place still felt reassuring even in a house that rearranged its own corridors.

The connection between them had quieted with the distance but not disappeared. One floor above, he was still awake. She could not hear thought through it, never that. Only the uneven pace of his heart and the shock still moving through him hard enough to echo in her and keep sleep away.

She sat on the bed and pulled her jumper over her head. Under the glamour her left forearm still held the scar, letters carved there seven years ago by a dead woman in the room downstairs. Three weeks earlier his thumb had traced that word. Tonight she could still feel the place his mouth had been, his hand at her jaw, the pull in her own body when he leaned in.

Ordinarily the research breakthrough would have kept her upright until dawn. Plaiting changed everything. Repair methodology, anchoring points, the Ministry's entire approach, all of it looked different now. Tonight the notes stayed closed on her bedside table.

Instead she lay back with her fingers pressed to her mouth and replayed the kiss: the wrong angle of his mouth, the shake in his hand before it found her jaw, the way he had turned into her palm once he stopped trying to hold himself apart. The Manor could warm a room and shorten a corridor. It could not make his hand shake.

Tomorrow she could put the folio on the desk between them and tell him the rest. She pictured his face closing before he spoke, the openness from tonight sealing back over. She would not say it.

All around her the Manor held the room at the warmest point it had yet offered her. Lavender hung in the sheets like an argument. The room was too ready for her. She didn't trust a second of it. One floor above, he was still awake. Only one storey of stone separated them. The rest was what she'd read in the folio and still wasn't telling him.

She lay there wanting him and mistrusting every part of the wanting.


# Chapter 16

In the three days since the kiss, the archive had been letting him in.

Without Granger, the room held itself together reluctantly. The ceiling shifted by inches. The reading table listed to the left. Twice this week the far alcoves had gone dark while he was standing in them, and he'd backed out before the walls could remember they knew how to close.

Today it held.

At the third unit on the east wall he found the ley-line cross-index she'd mentioned yesterday. Behind it sat a folio thick as a brick, bound in black leather, older than the anchoring records beside it. An old catalogue sigil still glimmered on the inside cover: 14-C. Main library shelving. The house had moved it.

The title page was layered with family notation over Latin. His Latin was good enough.

*On the Binding Architecture of the Manor and Its Proper Maintenance for the Perpetuation of the Line.*

His hand stayed on the page.

The opening sections were legible. His great-great-grandfather had written them in the brisk, compressed hand Draco knew from family records, then added plain-English glosses in the margins for descendants who might need the information without the scholarship.

*The Manor sustains the line by strengthening correspondences between inhabitants.* A gloss beneath it: *The house presses where attachment already gathers.*

*The innermost rooms answer more readily to paired inhabitants than to one.* Another gloss: *It tests relation through proximity, recurrence, and mutual awareness at distance.*

The next page was worse. Water damage had eaten the lower half. Ink blurred into the parchment in a grey bloom that spared fragments and swallowed verbs.

*Activation requires... emotional accord...*

Underneath, in the margin:

*The house does not... where none... Yet once roused it intensifies attention, contrives repeated nearness, and may be mistaken for feeling itself by inexperienced inhabitants.*

Draco read the damaged gloss twice.

He couldn't tell what missing word had sat after *does not*, or whether the house started the fire or found a spark and blew on it until the whole room went up. The uncertainty was the point now. The house shaped conditions. It could make what was happening between them impossible to trust, and Granger had found that out weeks ago.

Five weeks ago she had gone formal overnight. He'd spent that week certain she'd finally seen him clearly and recoiled. The dates arranged themselves with humiliating ease. She found the folio. She pulled back. Then she came close again and never told him why.

After the scar she came back to him. After the kiss she still said nothing.

He put a hand flat on the damaged page and pictured her here alone, reading faster than he could, stopping at the same ruined line. She would have seen more of it than he did, perhaps enough to know, perhaps only enough to panic, but certainly enough to understand that what the house was doing should have been said aloud.

By the time the walls narrowed, he was already on his feet. The folio stayed open on the table as he left. In the main library beyond, her reading chair sat by the window with a book facedown on the arm. She had probably meant to come back to it after lunch. She was always meaning to come back.

He found her in the library an hour later.

At the cataloguing table she was bent over the St. Mungo's reports with two anchoring texts open at her elbow. She looked up as he came in, still half in the work.

"I found the break in the ward layering," she said. "The third anchoring point isn't built on the same structure as the first two, which means the decay may be running through two different systems and if that's true we need to-"

"When did you find the folio?"

The quill stopped moving. Ink gathered at the nib and dropped onto the page.

"What folio."

"Don't." His voice stayed even. "The binding folio. Black leather. Old 14-C mark. It's in the archive now. When did you find it?"

For a second she only looked at him. Then she set the quill down with too much care.

"Five weeks ago," she said. "Maybe a little over. I was looking for an explanation."

"For what."

"For the archive. The corridors. The awareness through the Mark." Her jaw went hard. "For all of it."

She said it looking straight at him. The rest stayed behind her teeth.

"And then you pulled away."

"I panicked."

"You knew enough to panic."

"I knew enough to know the house was in it. I didn't know what it meant."

"You knew it meant there was a question over everything that happened after."

She didn't answer.

He took another step into the room, stopping short of the table but close enough to smell ink and old paper and the tea she'd left untouched beside her notes.

"The activation section is damaged," she said quickly. "I couldn't read the whole clause. I couldn't tell if the house was strengthening an attachment that was already there or if it was-"

"Making it."

"Yes." Her fingers pressed into the table edge. "I didn't know."

"You still didn't tell me."

"I was trying to understand before I put that in your hands."

"It was already in my hands." His left arm had started to ache, a low warning. "It was in my house. In my body. In every room I walked into. You don't get to act as if this was yours to hold until you felt ready."

Her gaze dropped to the blot she'd made on the report. Her shoulders drew in before her jaw set again.

"I know," she said.

"No." The word came out sharper than the rest. "I don't think you do."

Silence stretched. Outside, wind scraped at the tall library windows. Deep winter light, pale and thin, lay across the floor between them.

"I found a mechanism, not an answer," she said. "I kept thinking if I read one more text I'd get certainty and then I could tell you anything useful instead of dropping-"

"Instead of dropping what." His voice flattened. "That the house might have its hand inside my- that maybe nothing between us was-" He stopped.

"Malfoy-"

"You kissed me. Knowing the whole thing was- was contaminated. You kissed me anyway."

"That's not- don't call it that."

"What should I call it."

"I was in it." The sentence came out too fast. "I wasn't- I was barely sleeping. I couldn't be in a room with you without feeling dragged toward you and doubting it while it was still-" She caught herself, pushed through. "I pulled back because I thought if I stayed near you I'd stop thinking entirely."

"And then you came back."

"Yes."

"And still said nothing."

"Yes."

He watched the ink spread through her notes. Neither of them moved to blot it.

"I let you decide when to tell me about the scar," he said.

Her head came up.

"That's not the same."

"Why."

"Because that was your body." He heard his own voice tighten and did nothing to hide it. "This wasn't only yours. This was my house and my Mark and my choice. Or what I thought was my choice."

She flinched, small but visible, her fingers digging harder into the table edge. He didn't soften the point.

"I know I took that from you."

"You did."

"I was afraid."

"So was I." He laughed once, without humor. "I just didn't know what I was meant to be afraid of."

She pushed back from the table and stood. The chair legs scraped over stone.

"You think I was protecting myself," she said. "I wasn't- I was trying to keep working because the magic is collapsing and if I'd told you- if I'd-" She looked at the far shelves instead of him. "I couldn't."

"Couldn't what."

"Watch you decide I was another thing this house had done to you."

The edge of the table pressed into his palm. He hadn't realized he'd been leaning on it.

"Is that what you thought."
