"I didn't-" Now she did look at him. "The page was ruined. Narcissa warned me without actually telling me anything. I was already feeling you through the Mark, the house kept pushing us into the same rooms, and then I kissed you because I wanted to and that made everything worse." Her voice had gone thin. "I didn't know what was mine anymore."

"I'd have liked the chance to not know it with you. Instead of after."

Her mouth opened, then closed again. When she spoke, he had to strain to hear it.

"I know."

"No, listen." He heard how formal he sounded and couldn't stop it. "My father decided where I lived. The Dark Lord decided what went on my arm. This house-" His left hand had closed into a fist without his noticing. "I'm always the last one to know. And then you found a text that said maybe even wanting you wasn't-" He swallowed. "You kept it. Five weeks."

"I know."

"You don't get to say that like it settles anything."

"It doesn't." Her hand went to her sleeve, then dropped. "But I do know."

For a moment the only sound in the room was the rattle of bare branches against glass.

"If I'd told you that day," she said, "you would've shut down."

"That's not the point."

"It is a little bit the point." She spoke over him for the first time, not loud, just quick and frayed. "You would've gone cold on me. On the research, yes, but not only that. You would've gone careful and dry and polite and I would've lost you before I understood what I'd found."

"You didn't get to make that call."

"No." The answer came immediately. "I didn't."

She didn't add anything to it. She just stood there with her hands flat on the table.
The pulse in his wrist kept knocking against his thumb.

"I should've told you the day I found it," she said.

"Yes."

"I should've told you before the scar. Before the kiss. Before any of it."

"Yes."

Her eyes went bright, though the rest of her stayed rigid.

"I was wrong."

She held his gaze. His hand stayed locked around the chair arm.

"What do you want me to do," she asked.

He had to look past her, at the darkening window, before he could answer.

"I want you to leave."

She stared at him. "Leave the room."

"Leave the Manor."

Her hand slipped on the strap and caught again.

"Malfoy, the anchoring work-"

"I know exactly what's at stake." He looked at the half-finished reports, the books she'd opened, the notes in her small cramped hand. "I don't care right now. I can't do this with you here."

He waited for the argument, for the list of reasons he was being impossible. None came.

"All right," she said.

He only caught it because the room had gone that silent.

She went upstairs to pack.

He stayed in the library because he couldn't yet trust his legs to carry him anywhere better.

Her handwriting covered the table. Cross-references. Margin notes. Corrections to the Ministry reports that made the official analysis look childish. He recognized the shape of her lowercase e, the leftward lean of her script, the pressure change when she got tired. Noticing those things felt obscene now. He noticed them anyway.

Upstairs, drawers opened and shut. A trunk lid hit wood. Floorboards sounded once, then again. The house carried small noises badly. Every one of them arrived sharpened.

By the time she came back down, the library had gone colder, and too quickly for evening or the ordinary settling of stone after sunset. The drop was abrupt, measurable, the kind the Manor chose when it was unhappy and didn't care who felt it.

He had moved to the chair by the window without remembering the walk.

She stopped in the doorway with her bag over one shoulder and her coat on. The coat was the dark Muggle one she'd arrived in, all clean lines and too many buttons. It belonged to the woman who had entered his house as a researcher. The woman in it now looked less certain of her edges.

"The notes are sorted," she said. "I duplicated the shared material. Your family texts are stacked separately."

"Fine."

"The third-point references are marked. If the archive will hold for you, the cross-index you need is in the-"

"I'll find it."

Her grip tightened on the bag strap.

"I didn't mean for this to happen like this," she said, then shut her eyes for a second as if she hated the sentence as much as he did. "No. That's useless. I made a choice every day I stayed here and kept quiet. I know that."

He said nothing.

"I was wrong," she said again. "I know that's not enough either."

He gave her nothing.

At last she nodded once, a spare movement that looked closer to acceptance than agreement.

Then she turned and walked out.

Her footsteps went down the corridor and toward the front of the house. He counted without meaning to. At the end came the sound of the main door opening for her, then closing.

The Manor reacted at once.

Warmth drained out of the walls. Shelves shifted with a long wooden groan. Through the old window glass the December grounds flattened into grey, then into the early dark that came before anyone was ready for it. The corridor outside the library shortened by several feet, then stretched again, the house failing for a moment to settle around her absence.

Pain tore through his left forearm, the old pain this time, nearer to a burn than the recent low ache, dark magic looking for a command that would never come. It hit hard enough to make him fold over in the chair, one hand locked around his wrist.

So that was back.

He stayed there until the worst of the wave passed. When he lifted his head again the library had lost the coherence he'd mistaken for architecture. The tables and shelves were still in place, but the room no longer knew what to do with itself.

On the table, her notes were still spread around the ink stain. In the archive behind the hidden wall, the damaged folio still lay open to the ruined clause that had broken them.

Maybe the missing word would've cleared her. Maybe it would've condemned both of them. It changed nothing about the fact that she'd known there was doubt in the room and let him walk into it blind.

The dark pressed harder at the windows. Somewhere deeper in the house, a door shut by itself.

Before she came, cold had been ordinary here. That was the shape of his life and he'd learned it. After five and a half weeks of her footsteps in the corridor, her books on the chairs, her voice crossing the library at all hours, the old cold no longer fit. It felt wrong on his skin, borrowed, like the house was handing him back a life he had already, stupidly, stopped knowing how to live.


# Chapter 17

The flat hadn't changed.

Three days back in London, Hermione could cross it in the dark without surprise: six steps to the kitchen, four to the bathroom, radiator knocking in the same place every time. The predictability she'd chosen after the divorce should have felt like safety. Instead it felt sealed.

The Mark still found her from Wiltshire, but only as blunted data. A dull ache while she brushed her teeth. A flare at four in the morning sharp enough to wake her. The pain came loose from place. It hit while she stood at the sink or lay in the dark and told her only that somewhere in Wiltshire he was hurting. She could not tell whether he was standing or sitting or folded over the library table with his head in his hand. Whatever sustained contact had been doing to the curse, leaving had stripped it away.

By the third night sleep had reduced itself to one usable conclusion. Draco had not been angry that the house might be amplifying them. He had been angry that she had known the terms might be false and let him go on inside them anyway.

---

Thursday morning the Floo failed outright.

The Floo skipped the warning signs. At seven forty-three Hermione stepped into her fireplace, threw the powder, said, "Ministry of Magic," and got back an ordinary orange burn, fit for heat and useless for travel. Two more tries left soot on the knees of her trousers and a dark streak across one cuff.

Apparition took her to an Atrium already sliding toward panic. Technicians crowded the grates. Voices bounced off tile in that clipped professional register people used when fear would only slow them down. The outage had spread across London, parts of Bristol, most of the West Country.

Dennis Creevey intercepted her near the lifts with a stack of incident reports hugged to his chest. Muggle trainers showed under his robes. One sleeve was scorched. He looked about nineteen until he opened his mouth.

"We've had six accidental-magic incidents since last night," he said. "Children. The youngest is four."

"Six?"

"Ambient field went strange around three. We don't know if the collapse caused the cascade or the cascade took the Floo with it." He shifted the files higher. "Two kids are in St. Mungo's. One of them set her bedroom on fire. Infrastructure wants you downstairs after you've seen these."

Her hands were steady when she took the reports. That part still worked: intake, triage, sequence, action.

The reports were not bureaucratic-bad. They were frightened-bad. A nine-year-old in Dorset had shattered every window in his house during a nightmare. Twin girls in Camden had levitated the kitchen table for forty minutes and then started breaking plates with their crying. The four-year-old in Bristol was named Ruby. She had been crying in her crib when ambient magic superheated the air around her. Ruby was in the burn ward. Her parents were Muggles. The Obliviators had told them it was an electrical fault.

Reading that on a Ministry corridor wall made the crisis leave abstraction behind. Stabilization curves and node charts were one thing. A four-year-old with burns across her body because the field meant to buffer undeclared magic had slipped its bounds was another.

Dennis led her into a windowless workroom below the Atrium where half a dozen people had already colonized the table. A charmed map of southern Britain hovered over the center, lit with weak green points and angry red ones. The red had started clustering. A woman from Magical Infrastructure Hermione knew by sight, Patel, stood with her sleeves rolled up and both hands braced on the back of a chair. Croaker occupied the corner nearest the blackboard, coat still on, eyes giving away nothing except that he had not slept.

"Tell me you brought a miracle," Patel said without preamble.

"I brought soot and no breakfast."

"Close enough." Patel pointed at the map. "London relay degradation doubled between midnight and six. Bristol's worse. The West Country is a mess. Your Manor work is the only lead anybody keeps using the word promising about, so I need you to tell the room what we actually lose if that work stays stalled."

Hermione put the incident reports down and looked at the chart. It was uglier than the overnight version she had left on her desk. The red bands around London had widened and started feathering west. Bristol was almost solid. Somewhere between Birmingham and the Severn valley, one of the filum lines had begun blinking in and out of existence so fast the map charm kept losing the edge.

She took out the notebook she had carried back from Wiltshire on the night Draco told her to leave. It contained her own transcriptions, field numbers, copied diagrams, and just enough Malfoy shorthand in the margins to be infuriating. The cover was bent where she'd shoved it into her case too hard.

"The short answer," she said, "is that the Manor work had finally moved beyond identifying the damage. We were starting to sort out how the original network was meant to be repaired."

No one interrupted. Croaker's stillness had that effect on rooms.

"Tiberius's ledger gave us the broad structure," Hermione said. "Twelve anchor sites, filum threads, pressure routes between them. The Levett manuscript gave us the repair grammar: how damaged threads are plaited back toward a knot-point, how much pressure an anchor house can take before it starts shedding the excess into the field, what order the stabilizing work has to happen in if you don't want to tear the junction wider. What it didn't give cleanly were the Manor-specific maintenance clauses. Those are buried in family shorthand and property notation in the older archive material."

Patel frowned. "And the missing clauses matter because."

"Because a general repair model is not the same thing as a usable sequence. If you know a bridge needs support and not where the support sits, you still have a river and dead people in it."

"Fine. The archive."

"The archive only holds when both of us are there."

That landed harder than she'd expected in a Ministry room. People glanced at one another the way they did when a magical condition sounded too personal to be official.

Croaker said, "Explain."

"The hidden room is threshold-sensitive. The wall in the library does not open for me alone. It opens and stays open when Malfoy and I are both in it. We tested that weeks ago. We also discovered that the useful pages require both kinds of literacy. He reads the family notation and the property abbreviations. I read the runic construction text and the structural theory. One without the other is partial at best."

"Can he copy the material for you?" Patel asked.

"Not reliably enough. Some of it is too damaged. Some of it only makes sense against other books the house chooses to surface at the same time. And even with copies, we were still cross-checking live conditions at the Manor against the national field data. The house is one of the anchors. Working there was not just archival access. It was a pilot site."

Croaker lifted a hand slightly. "Observable results."

There it was. The part she had not yet managed to carry cleanly even in her own head. She opened the notebook to the pages she had already started wearing soft with her thumb.

"For the four days before the rupture," she said, "we were matching the Levett repair sequence to the Manor's local stress points and then tracking what the national monitors did after each successful pass. We were still testing, not repairing. But every time we managed a full run in the archive and I came back with a coherent set of clauses, the field numbers stopped worsening for a while. The west Atrium grate went from dropping twice an hour to holding for most of a morning. Two Bristol relays that had been shedding travellers into the wrong fireplaces stabilized for thirteen hours. Accidental-magic reports flattened for a little over a day instead of climbing."

Patel's head came up. "You're certain that correlation tracks the Manor work and not a temporary easing in the field?"

"No," Hermione said. "I'm not certain. That's the point. We had four days of improvement in a crisis that's been getting worse for months. That is not proof. It might have been the house protecting its own anchor once it sensed we were close to the right material. It might have been coincidence in a small sample. But it was the first bend in the numbers that wasn't random noise."

Croaker said, "And now."

Hermione looked at the red bands. "Now it's gone."

The workroom stayed quiet for a beat too long.

Patel dragged a hand over her mouth. "Could the stalled component be the archive access, or you?"

"Both," Hermione said before she could dress the answer up. "The room itself requires paired presence. And the one useful pattern we had depended on both kinds of reading happening in the room while the Manor was under live load. We weren't just deciphering texts. We were comparing them against an anchor while it was actively straining."

"So if you resume, the improvement might resume."

"Might."

"And if you don't?"

"Then we have my copies, which are incomplete, and a national field collapse that no longer has even a provisional brake on it."

Patel looked at Croaker. Croaker looked at Hermione. There were more comfortable ways to be cornered.

"What prevents resumption?" he asked.

There was no usable professional answer to that question. Not one that would survive cross-examination and still belong to her.

Hermione kept her face level. "Access I don't currently have."

Patel's mouth flattened. "Can the Ministry compel it?"

The answer that came first was logistical. The real answer arrived half a breath later and stayed behind her teeth.

"You can compel people," Hermione said. "You cannot compel a sentient house not to be itself. You can put ten curse-breakers in that library and the wall will stay wall if the condition isn't met. And if you try to muscle an anchor house while the field is already unstable, you may do more than waste your time."

Croaker said, "Can we test anything from what you brought back?"

"Yes." She looked down at the notebook. "Not enough. But yes."

They moved downstairs again, deeper into the Ministry, to a service chamber off the relay maintenance corridor where one of the Atrium grates had been taken apart for inspection. The bricks behind it had been stripped back to reveal the braided channels in the wall. Under diagnostic light the channels looked like veins lit the wrong colour, most of them a tired amber, one of them spitting sick orange sparks at irregular intervals.

Patel crouched by the open panel. "This is the same stretch that's been dropping to ordinary fire mid-transit."

"Because the load is backing up at the knot-point," Hermione said.

"You can tell that from looking."

"No. I can tell it because the last eight hours of monitor data and three hundred years of dead Malfoys are all saying the same thing."

Dennis, who had followed carrying two instrument cases and a face that suggested he had not heard the word breakfast since childhood, set the cases down and popped them open. Inside were relay calipers, chalk, a brass pressure dial, and a narrow box of warding nails. He handed Hermione the chalk without asking whether she wanted it. This was why she liked him.

She knelt by the wall. Heat beat at her cheeks. The failing channel had exactly the wrong rhythm: pressure, stall, spill; pressure, stall, spill. Not random after all. That at least was something.

"If the Levett sequence is right," she said, more to herself than to the room, "the knot should take pressure again if the damaged thread is braced before the spill point instead of after. We've been trying to catch it too late."

Patel said, "We."

"Humanity."

Hermione copied the provisional sequence from her notes onto the brick with chalk. The symbols looked inadequate the moment they left her hand, too clean and recent against old Ministry stone. She adjusted the third marker, added the pressure notation Levett used for partial rethreading, then stopped over the final space.

That was where the Manor clauses had started forking. One family annotation said to bind the anchor to its own memory before feeding it load. Another, later hand had contradicted it in shorthand she could only half read without Draco's impatient translations across the table. The difference between the two had been one of the things they were supposed to settle this week.

Patel noticed her stop. "Problem."

"Missing instruction."

"Can you infer it?"

"I can guess."

Croaker said, "Do it."

Hermione drew the final sigil from her best approximation and put her palm flat to the stone. "Dennis."

He passed her the dial and fed controlled magic into the side channel. The orange flare sharpened, went white for one alarming second, then thinned. The heat in the brick dropped. One of the upper channels brightened from amber to green. Somewhere overhead a bell chimed twice as the monitor room registered the change.

Nobody in the service corridor breathed.

Hermione counted under her breath. Five. Six. Seven.

Dennis looked from the wall to the dial. "Pressure's leveling."

"For now," Hermione said.

At eleven seconds the orange came back hard enough to crack two of the chalk marks. At fourteen the side channel spat sparks into Dennis's sleeve. At sixteen the whole brace failed with a sound like a kettle screaming. Patel threw up a shield before the hottest of it hit the floor. The orange light collapsed back to its original sick pulse.

Nobody had lost skin. That made the attempt a success by current national standards.

Patel exhaled first. "That was better than we've had all week."

"That was a brace," Hermione said. "A bad one. It bought us sixteen seconds on a controlled channel and told us the failure point really is where the Manor texts say it is. That's useful. It's not repair."

Croaker asked, "What would make it repair."

Hermione was still kneeling. Her palm stung from the heat. "The actual Manor clause instead of my guess. The rest of the sequence that tells the anchor where to send the pressure once you've got its attention. And the paired work that let us identify which clauses were functional and which were ceremonial rubbish."

Patel leaned against the opposite wall and tipped her head back. "I hate that your answer keeps being the same."

"So do I."

They ran the test twice more. The second try failed faster. The third bought twenty-two seconds and scorched one of Dennis's instruments beyond recovery. Twenty-two seconds was still not a repair. It was also twenty-two seconds longer than the relay had stayed coherent that morning. By noon three departments were calling down for updates and nobody had anything fit to give them except not enough and maybe later.

Hermione spent the next three hours turning the service-corridor results into something the Ministry could use without mistaking it for hope. The brief she wrote reduced itself to a list of limits: provisional brace on degraded local channel, confirms knot-point theory, not field-ready, requires missing Manor sequence, requires in situ verification. She took out one sentence about paired presence and put it back in because it was true. She took out one sentence about the house being an active participant because there was no bureaucratic language that didn't make it sound either childish or insane. In the end she left in threshold condition, pilot-site relevance, and nothing helpful.

At half three the Mark flared so sharply she had to brace both hands against her desk. White went across her vision and receded. By the time she could straighten, the spike had flattened into the usual dull pull. He was alive. He was in pain. He was still there.

Dennis looked up from the opposite table. "You all right?"

"Fine."

"That didn't look fine."

"It wasn't dramatic enough to be anything else."

He stared another second and, to his credit, did not push. Instead he came over and dropped two new reports on the desk.

"Three more since noon," he said. "One in Croydon, two in Bath. No burns this time. One levitation event, one burst of transfiguration, one household concealment failure that took half a street with it."

Hermione closed her eyes for one second, no more. "Thank you."

"Do you want tea?"

"No."

"Do you want me to get you tea anyway."

She opened her eyes. "Yes."

He left. She looked at the reports and did not open them immediately. Across the room, two junior researchers were arguing over whether Floo instability should still be modelled as a transport problem or formally reclassified as field collapse. Somewhere above them a grate coughed green, failed, and went orange again. The Ministry was full of sounds like people pretending systems still counted as systems if you shouted the proper nouns hard enough.

By six the Floo was technically back and practically untrustworthy. Green fire kept guttering orange mid-transit. Croaker signed the brief with the expression of a man authorizing something worse than failure because failure at least ended. Patel sent three copies to Infrastructure and one to St. Mungo's so the Healers could stop asking whether the relay damage was a separate emergency.

The bottom drawer of Hermione's desk held a jumper and a pair of trainers for nights she slept on the office sofa. She looked at them for a while and shut the drawer again.

Home was colder than she remembered. The warming charm on the radiator failed twice before it held. She did not bother with dinner at first. She spread the Manor notebook, loose Ministry charts, and three copied vellum pages across the kitchen counter and then onto the floor when the counter proved too small.

At the Manor the work had looked almost respectable. One table for Tiberius's map. One for Levett's repair tables. One for the family material that only made sense after Draco read the shorthand aloud in the clipped, irritated voice he used when old relatives had been idiots on parchment. She had hated how easily the division of labour fit. He knew what every symbol had once been meant to conceal. She knew which concealments mattered to the network and which were family vanity. Together, for a few hours at a time, they had been able to turn old damage into solvable sequences.

On her kitchen floor in London, all of that coherence vanished.

She could still read the runic body text. She could still follow the plaiting instructions in the Levett pages. What she could not do was move from those abstractions into the ugly practical clauses in the margins, the bits where a Malfoy two centuries dead had noted which wall warmed first under strain, which west-facing seam took pressure badly in winter, which signs meant an anchor was answering and which meant it was about to bite. Draco had translated those pieces without ceremony, one hand flat on the page, the other on his tea. She had written them down fast enough to keep up and never once really understood how much of the bridge between theory and use was his.

She sat cross-legged in the middle of the papers and tried anyway.

By nine she had recreated three different versions of the missing relay clause from memory. One contradicted Levett. One contradicted the service-corridor result. One might have been right if she had transcribed a single abbreviation correctly the night before the rupture, which she could no longer swear she had. The notebook acquired new slashes, circles, and sidebars. Nothing in it became trustworthy enough to hand back to Croaker with her name attached.

The Mark pulled low and mean in her left forearm.

She pushed her sleeve to the elbow and looked at the skin. The glamour held: winter-pale forearm, a vein faintly blue at the wrist, no silver-white lettering breaking through. The ache worked deeper than skin, a buried hook dragging slow.

What unsettled her was not only the pain. It was the shape of it. The spikes had changed since she'd left. At the Manor, after the later examinations, the curse had started doing something almost rational under her hands. It quieted. Not fully, not cleanly, but enough that she could separate baseline pain from stress response, the old command magic from whatever new feedback had begun to sit between them. In London the old distinctions had blurred. The Mark now yanked like something half-trained and then abandoned.

That, too, was data. Miserable data, but data.

She took out a clean sheet and wrote the only version of the problem that did not lie.

Without direct contact, the Mark reverts.

Without paired archive access, the repair sequence stalls.

Without both, field variance resumes acceleration.

The three sentences sat there in her hand, bleak and plain. They made the rupture sound like mechanics. They turned his body and his house into inputs and left out the part where he had never agreed to be either. She stared at the page until the omission became impossible to tolerate.

She turned the sheet over and wrote nothing on the back.

Dinner eventually came from a tin and was eaten standing up at the counter because sitting at the table alone felt like accepting a permanence she wasn't ready for. The lentil soup tasted faintly of metal under the salt. Draco had made risotto once, late in the third week, stirring with the same patience he gave to ruined marginalia and broken spells. She had grated cheese over both bowls while he shoved books out of splash range with the back of his wrist. They had eaten with the books shoved aside and argued over a fourteenth-century annotation until she forgot to be careful for almost an hour.

She set the bowl in the rack and still had him in the kitchen with her, wrist shoving books out of splash range while the rice thickened in the pan.

Friday was worse.

The first memo hit her desk before eight. Two more outages overnight. Seven additional accidental-magic events, three of them in houses whose wards should have contained a child's surge without anyone outside the family noticing. An internal note from Infrastructure asked whether Malfoy Manor could be formally designated a strategic repair site. A second note, from someone more junior and less careful, asked whether the "paired threshold condition" could be replicated with blood, objects, or coercive magic.

Hermione read that one twice before folding it in half so sharply it tore.

By nine she was back in Croaker's office with the torn memo on his desk between them. The room smelled of dust, parchment, and the old iron filing cabinets he refused to replace with anything charmed.

"No," she said.

Croaker looked at the memo. "I assume that is the answer to the proposal, not a general statement of mood."

"Both."

"Sit down."

She sat.

He took off his glasses, cleaned them with the edge of his sleeve, and put them back on. It was one of the few indulgently human things he did.

"I need the practical version," he said. "Not the outraged one."

"The practical version is still no. We do not know enough to start substituting bodies and force for a condition we barely understand. The house is already under strain. If we try to cheat a threshold-sensitive anchor in the middle of a national collapse, we may not get a neat refusal. We may get rupture."

"And the outraged version."

Hermione looked at the memo again. "The outraged version is that Malfoy is not a reagent."

Croaker's face did not change. "I assumed you understood that before this morning."

So had she. That was the problem.

"I did," she said.

He let the answer sit. He was very good at silence when silence was the sharpest available instrument.

"Can the work continue here?" he asked after a moment.

"Not in any way that matters quickly enough."

"Then if the only serious lead remains in Wiltshire, I need to know whether there is any professional obstacle to your resuming it."

Professional obstacle. The phrase was almost kind. It gave her one last chance to lie in a language both of them could share.

Hermione said, "No."

Croaker nodded once, as if the answer confirmed a calculation he had already made. "Then the obstacle is personal."

"Yes."

"Is it likely to remain so for long."

Her laugh had no amusement in it at all. "That depends how patient you think betrayal should make somebody."

Croaker folded the memo and set it aside. "I am not asking you for confession, Granger."

"Good."

"I am asking whether I should continue making plans that assume the Manor work is paused indefinitely."

The truthful answer was I don't know. The more truthful answer was I do not know because I no longer trust my own motives near that house, and I have already proved what damage that can do. She gave him the part that could survive office air.

"No," she said. "Do not assume indefinitely."

His eyes stayed on her for another second. "All right."

That should have been the end of it. Instead he said, "The people in Infrastructure are going to keep trying to make this simpler than it is. Do not let them."

"I won't."

"And if the answer is that you need to go back, then go back before the crisis decides for you."

There were half a dozen replies available to that. Hermione used none of them.

The rest of Friday passed in the particular misery of being the person nearest a solution and not near enough. The relay brace from Thursday could be reproduced now that she knew where it failed. That made it useful to technicians and useless to the country. She spent the morning training two Infrastructure teams to buy themselves twenty seconds, thirty if the brick was kind, at degraded local knot-points. Enough to stop a transit chamber from fully collapsing while they cleared it. Enough to move a burned child from Bristol to St. Mungo's without losing the healer halfway through. Not enough to call anything stable.

At noon Dennis brought her a fresh stack of intake reports and a sandwich she had not asked for.

"You have to stop doing that," she said, looking at the sandwich.

"No, I don't."

"You are twenty-three."

"And you're impossible."

He said it without heat. Hermione almost smiled.

"Ruby's still stable," he added. "Parents are asking different questions now, which is better than the first kind of questions. There's a team with them."

"Good."

"And the west grate held twenty-eight seconds on the last run."

"That's not nothing."

"No." Dennis hesitated. "Do you think the Manor work would still do what it was doing if you went back today."

Because he was Dennis, the question contained no gossip at all. Only need.

Hermione set the sandwich down. "I think the archive would open. I think the texts would still be the texts. I think the field might ease again for a little while if we resumed the sequence we were on."

"But."

"But none of that is the only question anymore."

Dennis nodded as if he had not understood and had still, somehow, understood enough. "Right."

He left her with the sandwich. She ate half of it because he was annoying enough to make disobedience childish.

By half five the whole building had the stretched, over-bright quality institutions got when everyone in them knew the weekend would not mean rest. Green flames in the Atrium were back, but only in the temperamental way old machinery returned after being slapped. People were taking stairs rather than trust them. Three different departments had started drafting public language that all meant we have not lost control yet.

Hermione took her notebook, the relay brief, and the half-smashed remains of Dennis's scorched pressure dial back to her flat because leaving any of it at the office felt like tempting arson by bureaucracy.

Harry knocked at half six with takeaway from the Thai place near the Ministry and the look he wore when he had decided to keep showing up until the job was done.

---

"I brought food," he said. "The Floo's being strange again, so you're stuck with me till it settles."

Relief was immediate enough to irritate her. Even that she was too tired to hide.

"If you've come to stage an intervention, at least tell me whether it's the edible kind."

He lifted the paper bag. "Depends what they did to the spring rolls."

They ate on the sofa. The pad see ew was overcooked. The spring rolls had gone cold. Harry noticed she was actually eating and was decent enough not to mention it. He also noticed the papers spread over half the coffee table and one dining chair dragged in beside the sofa because she'd needed the extra flat surface.

"You've colonized the flat," he said.

"It's temporary."

"That sounds like something people say just before they end up living inside a pile of evidence."

"I already live inside a pile of evidence."

"You really do."

He took another bite, chewed, and looked at her over the container. "You look like shit."

"Thanks."

"I mean it. When did you last sleep properly?"

"Define properly."

"Hermione."

"I'm sleeping some. It's been a bad week."

"It's been three bad days and one catastrophic one. Dennis has started using the tone people use about bomb disposal."

"Dennis is twenty-three and covering three jobs. He doesn't need my sleeping habits on his list."

"He was worried." Harry set his container down. "The girl in Bristol is stable, apparently. Team's with the parents. But thirteen kids in less than two days, Hermione."

"I know."

"Kingsley's getting pressure from the Wizengamot. Infrastructure's losing their minds. Croaker looked like he was considering murder by memo." He tipped his head toward the papers. "And you brought the whole office home."

"Only the bits that aren't setting things on fire."

"That's not as reassuring as you think."

She put her own container down before she dropped it. "The decay's accelerating."

"Because."

Because the answer had spent the whole day demanding clearer language, she gave it to him in clearer language.

"Because the Manor work had finally become operational instead of theoretical," she said. "We weren't only finding old descriptions anymore. We were laying Tiberius's anchor map over current failure points and using the Levett manuscript to work out how the damaged threads are supposed to be fed back toward a live knot-point. The hidden archive only stays open when Malfoy and I are both in it. He reads the family shorthand. I read the construction text. The house only gave us the useful material when both of those things were happening at once."

Harry listened without interrupting. That alone meant he was worried.

"And?" he said.

"And for four days the numbers stopped getting worse every hour. Not fixed. Not even close. But the west Atrium grate held longer. Two Bristol relays stopped misrouting travellers for half a day. Accidental-magic reports flattened instead of climbing. Yesterday I could only reproduce a brace that bought a damaged channel twenty-two seconds."

"So the work was helping."

"It looked like it was helping."

"What's the difference."

"The difference is that one is evidence and the other is hope. We had evidence of improvement. We did not have proof of cause. It could have been the Manor protecting its own anchor once it sensed we were getting close. It could have been a temporary lull. It could have been both."

"But it stopped when you left."

"Yes."

"Because you're not there."

"Because I'm not there."

Harry looked at her for a beat. "So go back."

"I can't."

"Because of what happened."

"Yes."

He took another bite. The casualness was deliberate. It gave her somewhere to put her eyes that wasn't his face.

"Do you want to tell me what actually happened?" he said. "Because the story about disagreement over methodology is bollocks."

"Harry."

"I'm asking. That's not the same as pushing."

Hermione tucked her feet under her and tried, briefly, to think of a cleaner version. None existed.

"I found something in the archive," she said. "About the house. It amplifies emotional connections between the people living in it. It was built to do that. I found the text weeks ago, and I didn't tell Malfoy."

"How long did you sit on it."

"Weeks."

He nodded once. "And he found out himself."

"He found the same folio. Worked out when I'd read it. Worked out what changed after." Her grip tightened around the cardboard container. "I stayed. I kept working. I kept being close to him without telling him what I knew that closeness might mean."

"To you."

The word sat there and did not soften.

Harry put his dinner down. "What are you angry about?"

"What?"

"Right now. What keeps you up."

The first answer rose fast enough to feel true. He hadn't let her explain. He had decided what she'd meant. He had made her into something colder than she was.

Except he hadn't done that. He had named what she'd done.

"I'm angry that he decided I'd been manipulating him," she said. "That I'd been studying him like he was a subject."

"Hadn't you been."

"Not like that."

"What would 'like that' be."

The radiator ticked. Outside, the parked car across the road blinked its hazards into the window glass.

"The research required proximity. It required the examinations. It required both of us in the archive. That doesn't mean I was using him."

"I'm not asking about the research."

She stopped.

"So go tell him that," Harry said. "You screwed up, you know you screwed up. Go say so."

"You make that sound simple."

"Isn't it?"

"No. Because he's not entirely wrong about me." She pressed her thumbnail into the side of her index finger. "He got it partly right. And he didn't let me explain."

"Explain what."

"That I was going to tell him."

"When."

"When I'd verified."

"Right. When you were ready."

The car alarm outside gave one thin warning chirp and cut off again.

"That's not the same thing," she said.

"Yeah, it is."

She said nothing.

"He found out before you were ready," Harry said. "And that's— I mean, that's the bit, isn't it."

"That's not." The denial started and she heard it for what it was.

"You were scared," Harry said. "That makes sense. The house might be messing with your feelings, and that's, yeah. Of course you were scared."

"But."

"But you sat on it. And while you were sitting on it, he was still in it. Doing the whole thing. Not knowing."

Her face had ended up against her knees without her quite noticing. The fabric smelled faintly of soy sauce and paper.

"I was trying to be sure."

"I know." He put the fork down. "I'm just saying what it looks like from his end."

The connection in her arm pulled low and steady.

"It could all be the Manor," she said into the cloth. "Everything I feel. Everything he feels."

"Could be."

"You're not going to tell me it's real."

"How would I know? I'm not inside that house."

That almost made her laugh. Not quite.

"I know you, though," he said. "That wasn't thorough. That was scared."

"That's unfair."

"It's a bit unfair. It's also true."

Wet-eyed and too tired to care, Hermione lifted her head. Harry had seen her cry in a girl's bathroom at twelve, in a forest at seventeen, and in plenty of smaller years since. He had never once made a spectacle of it.

"Croaker asked this morning whether there was any professional obstacle to my going back," she said.

"And."

"And I said no, because professionally there isn't one. The work's there. The texts are there. The house will probably open. The country is still coming apart." She rubbed at one eye with the heel of her hand. "But if I go back for the research, then I'm doing it again. I am. I'm making it about the thing I can solve instead of the thing I can't."

Harry nodded.

"Then don't go back for the research," he said.

The sentence didn't solve anything. It only stripped the lie out of the problem. If she went back, it couldn't be with field charts under her arm and national emergency in her mouth. The first thing she said had to be about what she'd hidden. The archive could wait to learn whether he wanted her in the house at all.

After a while she said, "The pad see ew was terrible."

"I know. The spring rolls were worse."

"I ate two."

"I saw. That's how bad this is."

Later, when the conversation had loosened by a notch, Harry asked how Dennis was holding up and said he reminded him a little of Colin at that age, all stubborn elbows and accidental competence. Hermione told him Dennis was covering three desks and pretending not to be furious about it. Harry said that sounded exactly right for the Creevey line. She told him she'd be in on Monday. He told her to tell Dennis herself because he wasn't her secretary. Familiar bickering returned by reflex, thin but real.

At nine the Floo was still unreliable, so Harry Apparated from the doorstep. Cold rushed into the flat before she shut the door again.

The takeaway containers stayed on the table until the noodles stuck to the cardboard. She should have binned them, washed the forks, done one ordinary thing before bed. Instead she carried the coffee-table papers to the desk, separated the Ministry work from the Manor notes, and sat down with a clean sheet.

The first line she wrote was: The Floo failed across half the country yesterday.

She stared at it, then crossed it out so hard the nib tore the paper.

The second line was: Ruby Melling is four years old and in the burn ward because the field slipped again.

She crossed that out too.

The third attempt began as an explanation of the relay brace, of the missing clauses in the Levett sequence, of how the knot-point theory had survived testing even if the brace itself had not. Halfway through the sentence she put the pen down. It was almost impressive, the speed with which her brain kept offering the same evasion in new technical clothes.

When she picked the pen up again, she did not try for a letter. She wrote down what the first conversation would have to do if it happened at all.

Do not ask for the archive first.

Do not put field charts first.

Do not say "the country needs" before "I knew."

The bluntness of it made her wince. It also looked more honest than anything else she had put on paper since leaving Wiltshire.

She added one more line beneath the others.

Do not ask him to help before I tell him what I did without dressing it up as method.

That was ugly enough to be close to true.

Her hand hovered over the page. For a moment she considered writing the rest of it out properly, sentence by sentence, and stopped before the page could turn into a case brief. She was too practiced at cases.

Instead she folded the sheet once and slid it into the front of the Manor notebook where the provisional relay brief had been. It was not a letter or a plan. It was a condition.

The Mark tugged again, low and stubborn. She pressed her thumb to the inside of her left wrist and sat with it without trying to turn it into data for once. Somewhere in Wiltshire he was in pain. Somewhere in London Ruby slept under burn spells and St. Mungo's wards. Somewhere below the Ministry, technicians were buying half-minutes from failing brickwork because she had brought them twenty-two seconds and not more.

All of it kept pressing on the same point. If there was going to be an ask, it had to begin with what she'd hidden.

She turned out the desk lamp and went to bed before she could start writing the wrong draft again. Sleep was poor when it finally came, but it came.


# Chapter 18

The seventh day was the worst.

It was not because the Mark hurt more, though it did. The magic in it kept reaching, stupidly and repeatedly, for the diagnostic touch that was no longer there. For weeks the pain had at least possessed a direction. Hermione's hands on his forearm could quiet it. Her spells could force it to reveal its uglier habits. Without that, the ache turned feral. It burned under the skin, ran up toward his elbow, then settled into a deep grinding pull that made his left hand flex against blankets, chair arms, tabletops, air. By the seventh day even his body seemed embarrassed by how often it looked for her.

The seventh day was worst because by then the house had stopped pretending.

For six days the Manor had managed a few involuntary gestures. It had not offered the responsive architecture he had grown used to when she was here. No shortcuts surfaced. No helpful doors appeared where walls had been. No library shelves rearranged themselves under his hands. There had only been twitches: a sconce in the east wing flaring once at noon and dying by evening, a corridor outside the library tightening by three paces and then lengthening again, a door near the conservatory sticking and releasing as if the house could not decide whether to be obstinate or merely exhausted. If he was feeling generous, he could call those signs of life. If he was honest, they were last autonomic functions.

On the seventh day there was nothing.

The Manor held its shape. The library stayed at the end of the same corridor. The guest room stayed in the same place along the east wing. The walk between them measured forty-one paces every time, where during the better weeks it had sometimes been twenty-three. He counted because there was nothing else to do with the arithmetic of loss except make it literal.

A house that moved was a house with opinions. This was only a house.

At ten in the morning Draco stood at the library entrance, dressed because dressing still divided one hour from the next. He had put on trousers, a shirt, and shoes like a man with business to conduct. The performance had satisfied no one. Beyond the open library door the windows let in what December offered, which was barely anything: a flat grey wash of light and an unbroken sheet of cloud. He had lost track of whether it was the second or third week of the month. The calendar in the morning room still existed. He had stopped consulting it three days ago.

The archive wall gave him blank stone. He put his palm to it anyway, part habit and part test. Cold met skin. It was only winter in old stone, not the sharper refusal of a house choosing to lock him out. He kept his hand there until the chill reached the heel of his palm and made the bones ache. Nothing changed. The seam did not appear. The wall did not warm. There was no answering pressure from the room behind it, no sense of a held breath on the other side.

He had tried at dawn. He had tried again an hour later. He had tried after not trying for a full afternoon yesterday because pride had briefly suggested restraint might preserve dignity. The wall had been stone every time.

Warming charms would have helped in the library. He had not bothered. Lighting the grate would have helped too. He had not done that either. The room felt wrong when it was warm without her in it, as if comfort under those conditions were a trick or an insult.

What he missed was not only her touch on his arm, though his body kept arranging the week around that absence. He missed the sustained fact of her attention. For six weeks she had moved through the library and changed its use by occupying it. For six weeks she had left books open at unreasonable angles, wedged notes between volumes, abandoned her scarf over chair backs, and interrupted silence with translation or argument or annoyance. For six weeks he had been looked at closely and not treated as a problem to manage, a relic to avoid, or a name to lower one's voice around. The withdrawal from that had its own humiliating clarity. He had not known how much of his day had started to depend on hearing her swear at marginalia.

In the kitchen the tea cooperated because the kitchen always cooperated. Water boiled. Steam ghosted up from the kettle. A cream-coloured cup waited by the sink, slightly too wide and with the handle turned wrong for his grip. It was hers. He had been using it for three days before he noticed, because the kitchen had apparently developed preferences while he was busy failing at austerity. Once he saw it, he kept using it. Switching to another cup would have required him to admit that he was, in fact, choosing around her absence instead of merely enduring it.

He carried the tea back through the east corridor rather than the nearer west passage, which he told himself had been a practical decision. The east wing gave him the guest room she had used, the narrow turn toward the conservatory that neither of them had ever had patience for, the morning room his mother preferred, and halfway down the corridor, the drawing room door.

It was open.

It had been open all week. The house had not shortened the walk there or thrown the room in his path with the crude theatricality it had once shown. It had simply left the door ajar, the way a house might leave a cracked tile visible under a carpet edge. He had not gone in. He had not looked directly through the doorway either. Still, the room pressed against his awareness each time he passed, as precise and irritating as the awareness of a loose tooth.

He kept walking. The tea rippled against porcelain. The bandaged skin under his sleeve gave a small ugly throb as if it objected to the corridor, the room, the morning, or all three.

Back in the library, a seventeenth-century genealogical record sat open on the table. It was one of the accessible texts and one of the useless ones. He had spent most of yesterday in company with family trees, marriage entries, steward appointments, property notes, and the sort of smug domestic record-keeping old pure-blood houses mistook for history. A few marginal marks had helped him confirm dates on earlier bonding episodes inside the house. None of it had moved the actual work forward. None of it had answered the only question that still carried any heat.

The harder knowledge was not in the book. It lived in the fact that Hermione had found the relevant folio first, read it without him, and gone on standing close enough to touch.

For two days anger had made everything clean. She had withheld material information while examining his body. She had decided what he could bear to know. She had kissed him with knowledge in her pocket that changed the terms of the kiss. The neatness of that story had been almost a relief. By the third day the neatness had started to rot. Doubt did not make her innocent, but it made her recognizably human. Of course she had been frightened. Of course she had wanted proof before bringing him the ugliest possibility either of them had found. Of course she had told herself she was buying time for rigor when in fact she had been buying time for fear.

Understanding any of that did not reduce the damage. It only stripped away the simpler villain.

He sat, stood again, crossed to the window, and came back to the table without remembering deciding to move. His tea went untouched beside the book. By noon the skin under the gauze felt hot enough that he loosened the wrap one-handed and checked whether the old curse had finally done the courtesy of splitting him open. It had not. The Mark had simply gone redder along the outer curls, the black in its center glossy as wet ink. He tightened the bandage badly, swore under his breath when the knot slipped, and did it again with his teeth.

The house gave him no witness to the indignity. No floorboard creaked in sympathy. No corridor shortened in mockery. Even the usual settling noises seemed to have withdrawn from the walls.

He tried the archive wall once more at half past one and got only stone. He opened the genealogical record again. He read the same three lines on a Malfoy marriage from 1784 until the words blurred into shape without meaning. One of Hermione's translation notes still lay under his left hand where he had found it seven days ago, a strip of parchment with her cramped writing correcting some ancestor's Latin and then, because she had been tired, insulting him in the margin for spelling a name three different ways within the same page. He had left the note exactly where it was and resented himself for the tenderness of that.

Measured, unhurried footsteps came first: not a servant's and not Hermione's. It was his mother.

Narcissa paused in the doorway in a dark coat and gloves. One glance took in the whole room: the dead grate, the blanket over the chair arm, the untouched cup, the record left open at the same place for too long, Hermione's note still on the table, and him in the middle of it all looking exactly as bad as he felt.

"You look dreadful," she said.

That almost made him laugh. The sound reached his throat and died there. "I'm fine."

"You are not fine." She removed her gloves with precise fingers. "Has Mipsy been coming?"

"I told her not to."

"That was not what I asked."

"She tried. I sent her away."

Her gaze moved over the room again. He could see her calculating the neglect in layers. The grate had not been lit. Dust had started to gather under the library window where Hermione usually opened it for air by midafternoon. One of the side tables still held a stack of books Hermione had meant to return to a shelf and plainly never had. Narcissa draped her coat over the chair by the door and, by that act alone, made it clear she intended to stay.

"Tea?" she said.

He poured what he had already made into a clean cup and went back to the kitchen for more water. By the time he returned, she stood at the window with one hand resting on the frame, her head tilted very slightly as though listening for a pulse inside the walls. Nothing answered. After a moment she sat in Hermione's usual chair, the one with the worn velvet and the ink stain on the arm, and lifted the cup without drinking.

Silence held for a while. His mother knew how to use silence. So did he. In better years that had passed between them as breeding. In worse years it had functioned more like a weapon. Today it felt like the only form in which either of them could afford honesty.

"I did not come to ask what happened," Narcissa said at last. "I know what happened. The house told me before it stopped telling anyone anything."

He looked at her. "The house told you."

"In its way."

She said it without mysticism. That was one of the few mercies of his mother's relationship to the Manor. She had never spoken about the house as if it were enchanted beyond comprehension or somehow holy. It was only old, powerful, temperamental, and woven through too much family history to ignore.

"I have not been fully honest with you, Draco," she said.

The sentence did not sound strategic. It sounded expensive.

He set his tea down carefully. "About the house."

"About the house. About what it does and what it cannot do." Her gaze lowered to the cup and returned to him. "I told myself I was protecting you from knowledge you were not ready for. That was partly true. It was also convenient."

"Then say it now."

She met his eyes. The resemblance was most evident when she was deciding whether to wound someone for their own good.

"You have read the bonding texts," she said.

"I've read them."

"Then you know the mechanism in outline. What you do not know, because the damaged pages assume any Malfoy child worth educating would already have heard it at table, is the limitation." Her thumb moved once around the rim of the cup. "The house amplifies. It gates. It opens certain passages and withholds others. It rewards concordance. All of that is true."

"I know."

"What the house cannot do is fabricate."

He said nothing.

She went on because she had decided, at last, to be direct. "It cannot create a connection where none exists. It can make an existing bond louder. It can alter conditions around that bond. It can do for attachment what glass and warmth do for a winter plant. It cannot put a seed into bare stone."

The words took a moment to arrange themselves into meaning. He was still staring at her when she added, more quietly, "What the house responded to between you and Ms. Granger was already there before the first corridor shortened."

Furniture shifted in his head, heavy and slow. Hermione's hands around his forearm during the first examination. Her face bending over the Mark with clinical concentration that had never once been cold. The library late at night with only two lamps lit because she said the rest made the room impossible to think in. Her mouth on his in the dim space between chair and table, frightened and clumsy and better than anything he had expected to have again. The house had turned up the volume. The sound had been there first.

"How do I know what belongs to the base level?" he asked. The question came out harsher than he intended. "How do I separate it?"

"You do not."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the only one." She held his gaze. "There is no untouched version available to you, Draco. Not in this house and not outside it either. We all love under conditions. Family is a condition. War is a condition. A house like this is only a more vulgar one."

He let out a breath that did nothing useful for his lungs.

"You are asking for a control specimen," Narcissa said, and almost smiled at the expression that crossed his face. "I know Ms. Granger's language has infected this house. But even in her terms, you cannot have one. The question is not whether the Manor altered what you felt. Of course it altered it. The question is whether it invented it. It did not."

That should have felt like relief. It did not. Relief implied closure. This was only rearrangement, the floor plan of his uncertainty altered under his feet.

"You knew this the whole time."

"Yes."

"You let us both go on without saying it."

"Yes."

He looked at her a long time. She did not flinch. The cup in her hand stayed level, but he could see the work of composure at her wrist.

"Why?"

The answer did not come at once. That, more than the content, told him she was done with easy evasions.

"Because I knew what saying it would cost me," she said finally. "And because I was not certain I could bear paying it while I was still deciding whether I had the right to interfere."

He frowned. "That is not clearer."

"No," she said. "It is simply truer."

He might have snapped at her for that on another day. Today the effort required to be annoyed felt larger than the reward. He leaned back in the chair and felt the worn wood press into his shoulders.

"Explain," he said.

Narcissa set the cup down. Her hand went, almost unconsciously, to the edge of her cuff and then away again.

"When I came to this house as a bride," she said, "the architecture altered. Not dramatically. A few rooms reopened. Some doors ceased sticking. The east corridor shortened when I was expected in the dining room. The temperature changed in winter. It was enough for me to understand that the house had taken notice."

She paused, and he understood with a cold prickling clarity that he was being invited into a part of her life she had never previously considered his business.

"I assumed it would deepen," she said. "The way family accounts suggested it sometimes had for earlier generations. I assumed that if I learned the rhythms of the house, if I stayed, if the marriage settled into itself, more would follow."

He did not interrupt.

"It did not." She folded her hands, then unfolded them again. "The house respected your father. He was a Malfoy. It obeyed bloodline rules around him. But the deeper resonance never came. Not once in twenty-five years. The paired awareness, the gated rooms that would only hold for two, the way it has been reading your body through hers and hers through yours. None of that happened for us."

The room did not have enough air in it.

"Mother."

"This is not a complaint," she said, and her voice sharpened just enough to make the distinction stand. "Your father and I had a real marriage. We chose it. We survived with it. There were years when surviving with it required all the skill either of us possessed, and we managed that too. I am not speaking against what I had. I am telling you the house never answered us the way it answers what it sees between you and her."

The phrase between you and her landed as fact, not speculation.

He looked down at the table because there was nowhere else to look. Hermione's note sat under his fingertips. The strip of parchment had absorbed a faint brown stain from old tea. He had not even realized he was touching it until then.

"Lucius knew?" he asked.

"Enough to know that the house was more alive with me than without me. Not enough to name the absence." Narcissa's expression shifted by a degree and became, for one brief second, nakedly tired. "He was not interested in absences he could not command."

He almost asked whether that had hurt. The question stopped at his teeth. Of course it had hurt. The form of the hurt was what she had just spent ten minutes laying at his feet.

"Why tell me now?" he said instead.

"Because you are old enough to do your own damage with the truth." The dry edge in the sentence softened before it could become cruel. "And because I have had a week to consider whether I wished to go on being a woman who watched her son repeat a confusion she recognized and said nothing because silence was more comfortable."

The library settled around them. Outside, wind moved against the windowpanes without enough force to become a storm.

"She kept the folio from you," Narcissa said. "That was her error, and I will not excuse it. But she did not create the thing that frightened her. She found it already in progress and handled it badly. People do."

People do. The phrase had the shape of a maxim and the fatigue of one learned the expensive way.

"It still matters that she hid it."

"Yes."

"It still matters that the house is involved."

"Yes."

"That does not tell me what to do."

"I did not come to tell you what to do." She stood, smoothing her skirt beneath careful palms. "Your choices remain your own. The house will have opinions whether invited or not."

He stayed seated. "Why did you not tell me sooner?"

The pause at the doorway was real. He could see her deciding how much of her private life she was willing to hand over and in what state.

"Because explaining how I knew meant admitting that the house gave you a form of answer it never gave me," she said. "I needed time with that."

The honesty in it struck harder than the revelation itself. His mother, who had spent half her life converting pain into manners, had just told him she envied him.

By the time her coat was buttoned, the room felt rearranged even though nothing had moved.

"Your arm," she said, glancing at the gauze.

He looked at it too. "It'll be fine."

"It will not." She pulled on the right glove, finger by finger. "You may survive that. It is not the same claim."

Then she was gone, footsteps receding through the entrance hall with the soft authority of someone who never hurried even when wounded. The front door opened and closed. A pipe knocked somewhere upstairs. The house offered no further commentary.

Draco sat with what she had given him: the grief of a woman telling her son that her marriage, real and chosen and lived, had never once moved the house the way this messy, unverified, damaged attachment had. She had carried that knowledge for years. Today she had laid it in front of him and declined to tidy it.

He picked up the genealogical record and saw none of it. He put it down again. He crossed to the archive wall and pressed his palm against the stone not because he expected it to open but because he suddenly needed an argument with matter. The wall stayed shut. For a foolish second he was almost offended. Then he saw the absurdity of asking the house for confirmation while trying to reclaim his life from its testimony.

He laughed once, without humor, and let his forehead rest against the cold stone.

If the house had not invented it, then the week had not been a cleanse. It had not burned away falsehood and left truth behind. It had only removed proximity and left him alone with the parts that remained when no one touched him, no one argued with him, no one moved through the corridor with a satchel banging lightly against her hip.

He missed her.

The clarity of it annoyed him at once. Missing her did not absolve her. Wanting her did not answer the influence question. Narcissa had not handed him certainty. She had only removed one lie from the center of the room. Everything else still stood around it, solid and awkward and impossible to ignore.

He went back to the table and finally drank the tea. It had gone cold. By the time the knock came, dusk had thickened at the windows and the library had sunk into that late-afternoon half-light in which every object looked a little farther away than it was.

---

It was the side entrance off the garden, not the front door.

That registered before the knock had quite stopped echoing. Hermione knew which door opened closer to the library. She would know which one spared them the entrance hall and his mother's portraits and the long performance of hospitality. The knowledge landed somewhere under his ribs before he had time to decide what it meant.

He crossed the corridor at a pace that wanted to be a run and arrived trying not to look as if he had. Outside stood Hermione in a heavy dark coat with the collar turned up against the cold, a scarf wound high at her throat, and a leather satchel hanging from one shoulder with enough weight in it to drag the strap across her coat. The satchel came first in his notice because of course it did. She would bring work even to an apology. Then the rest of her assembled: hair coming loose around the temples, cheeks raw from the weather, mouth set too carefully, one gloved hand still lifted from knocking.

Her gaze moved over his face, the poor bandaging at his wrist, and the general state of him. She did not comment on any of it. The restraint was probably mercy.

"Hi," she said.

She did not say Malfoy. She only said hi, as if the week between them had not invented a new form of distance.

"Hi."

Cold air held between them for one beat too long. He stood back. She stepped inside. The house did not warm the corridor, shorten the distance to the library, or shut the door behind her with any self-important flourish. He closed it himself and listened to the latch catch.

Hermione stayed where she was, just inside the threshold. He could feel the effort it cost her not to start with the satchel. Her hand remained on the strap as if work might keep trying to climb into her mouth first.

"I didn't come back to ask for the archive," she said, confirming that he had not imagined the struggle. "I know that's what the bag suggests. The work is in there because I didn't stop being me in London, not because I thought I could use it as an excuse to get through the door."

He said nothing. Silence had been the coin of the house all week. Let it work for once on her behalf rather than his.

"I should've told you," she said. "About the folio. About the damaged clause. About all of it. I should've told you the day I found it, or at worst the next morning when I still had time to pretend I had simply been slow rather than cowardly."

"You should have."

The plainness of that made her flinch. She accepted the hit.

"I told myself I needed to verify before I said anything," she went on. "Some of that was true. I did need more context, and the page really was damaged, and I still don't know the full mechanics. All of that is true." Her mouth tightened. "It wasn't the main truth. The main truth was that if I said it out loud too soon, I'd have to hear it out loud too. I'd have to stand there with you not knowing whether any of this was ours or the house's, and I kept deciding that was a burden I could manage better on my own."

She looked at him directly then, perhaps because the sentence had finally reached the point where looking elsewhere would have been too obvious.

"That was the ugliest part," she said. "Not the research. Not caution. The managing."

The corridor remained obstinately ordinary around them. Stone lay underfoot, bad light came through the windows, and a cold draft ran along the skirting from some old crack in the frame. He had half expected the house to perform a reaction to her return and hated himself for the expectation.

"My mother was here," he said.

Hermione's grip on the satchel tightened. "Did she say why the house has been dead?"

"She said why it was never alive in the way we thought."

That made her stillness sharpen. It was a familiar kind of stillness, the one that meant she was taking in information too quickly to move while she did it.

"What did she tell you?"

"That the house cannot fabricate." Saying it aloud altered the shape of the words in his own mouth. "It can amplify. It can gate rooms. It can make a nuisance of itself whenever two people are trying to ignore facts. It cannot manufacture a bond from nothing."

A long second passed.

Hermione let out a breath that clouded faintly in the cold corridor. "All right," she said, and then, because one sentence was not enough for her when the ground moved, "All right. That matters."

"Yes."

"It doesn't make what I did less wrong."

"No."

"And it doesn't settle the influence question."

"No. It does not."

Her chin dipped once in acknowledgment. "No clean control condition," she muttered, almost to herself.

Against his will, a little of the week's bitterness turned wry. "That was almost word for word my mother's objection."

Brown eyes lifted again, surprised despite herself. "Your mother said control condition."

"She said you have infected the house with your language."

For the first time since crossing the threshold Hermione looked as if a laugh might exist somewhere in the same world as this conversation. It did not quite make it out, but the possibility was there. Then it went.

"Did she tell you anything else?"

He could have delayed. He could have parceled the information out for effect, made her wait inside the same uncertainty she had given him. The impulse arrived and shamed him so quickly that he almost missed it.

"She told me my father never resonated with the house," he said. "Not the way this has. She said the architecture noticed her when she arrived, but it never did any of the deeper work for them. No paired rooms. No mutual awareness. Nothing like the archive."

Hermione's face changed in a way he could not have named if pressed. It was not pity or relief. It looked more like recognition that the price of Narcissa's silence had been larger than they knew.

"That must have cost her," she said.

"Yes."

The satchel strap creaked under her hand.

"Why are you here?" he asked.

He did not mean the corridor. He did not mean Wiltshire. She heard the question correctly.

Hermione answered more slowly than she had apologized, and that slowness made the answer feel truer.

"Because being apart wasn't neutral either," she said. "Because I kept acting as if uncertainty was the only dangerous condition and it wasn't. London is worse. The Ministry is worse. Your arm is worse. The work is stalled. And I spent seven days finding new ways to tell myself that I was being principled when really I was just trying not to stand beside the mess I'd made." She stopped, restarted. "That's not me saying the practical stakes mattered more than you. It's me saying I was still trying to split those categories apart in my own head, and they're not separate anymore."

The words were not tidy. They arrived with their own corrections still attached. That, more than eloquence would have, convinced him she had not scripted them on the train.

"Harry said the part I was avoiding, though not in those terms," she added. "He was less polite."

"He usually is."

"Yes."

Another silence opened, but it was not the same one as before. The first had been a waiting room. This one contained actual material.

He could have let them sink back into analysis, into the old endless argument about whether the house had contaminated each touch, each look, each late-night conversation in the library. He knew how to do that. Hermione looked ready to help. Thinking was where she went when the ground was bad. A week ago he might have followed her into it until both of them disappeared.

Instead he said, "If you stay, it can't be like before."

That stopped her. For one beat she looked almost blank, as if she had prepared herself for anger and not for terms.

He had not planned the sentence. The rest came rough, without the polish fear sometimes gave him.

"No more finding something that changes the terms and deciding alone how much of it I can—" He stopped. The sentence had been about to arrive polished, and he didn't trust it that way. "What you did with the folio. That can't happen again."

Hermione did not interrupt. That alone told him she was listening with her whole body.

"And I don't get to do my version either." He heard how careful he sounded and hated it, but the precision mattered. "I don't get to go silent for a week and call it principle."

She swallowed. The movement of her throat was visible above the scarf. "Together, then."

"If the archive opens, we read whatever it gives us in the same room. If one of us finds something—"

"That day," she said, and then caught herself finishing his sentence. "Sorry. Yes. That day. Not after I've—" She stopped. Restarted, more carefully. "Not after I've already built a case in private and decided what you can handle."

He heard her arriving at her own accusation without his having to deliver it.

"No managing," he said.

Her answer took a second because she was checking it for weakness before she let it out. "And if one of us wants out, or can't do it, we say that too. Out loud."

He looked at her. "Fine."

A breath went out of her that was not relief exactly. It sounded more like surviving the first cut without pretending it had not happened.

"Good," she said.

There was still more between them. Damage remained. Want remained. Doubt remained. The corridor held all of it and offered no assistance. Yet the terms had changed in one important way. The damage was no longer happening in parallel private rooms.

"How bad is it outside?" he asked.

Hermione closed her eyes briefly, perhaps because the answer forced the world back into the hall with them. "Bad enough that no one at the Ministry is using the word temporary anymore. The Floo was down for eight hours yesterday. St. Mungo's is rationing restorative draughts again. Accidental magic reports keep coming in from all over. People are pretending not to panic because panic is inconvenient."

That was enough to set the scale again. Britain was still failing, and the work was still waiting.

He glanced at the satchel. "You really did bring work."

"I told you. I didn't stop being me in London."

That answer was almost dry and almost ordinary. The shape of their old exchanges flickered in it and then steadied.

He nodded once. "I want to show you the room first."

Her face lost what little warmth had returned. She knew which room before he turned. Still, she followed without asking him to say it.

They walked the east corridor in full length. The house did not fold the distance for them. The guest room door stayed where it had always been. The morning room stood closed. The conservatory passage remained narrow and underused. Their footsteps answered cleanly off stone.

The drawing room door stood open where it had stood all week.

No stagecraft attended it now, and no pressure came from the threshold. No false warmth leaked from within. The room simply waited beyond the frame, ajar and cold and ordinary in the most offensive way.

He stopped beside the doorway. Hermione stopped too.

Dim corridor light reached the marble floor and the lower arms of the chandelier. Dust showed at the baseboards. A crack ran through one tile near the far wall, hairline but undeniable. The fire had not been lit in days, perhaps longer. Nothing in the room declared itself sacred or cursed. It was only a room in an old house. That had always been part of the obscenity.

"It has been open all week," he said. "I kept walking past it and not going in."

Hermione looked through the doorway without moving. He could not know all of what she saw, but he knew enough of the room's older contents to understand her stillness. He saw them too: the chandelier from floor level, the marble under her back, Bellatrix's bracelets clicking softly each time she reset her grip on the knife, the first red line opening on Hermione's forearm and the blood running toward her wrist because the floor sloped slightly there, and his own seventeen-year-old body against the far wall, every muscle locked so hard he had come away shaking.

His mouth filled abruptly with saliva. He swallowed against it. Beside him Hermione's right hand tightened on the satchel strap until the knuckles blanched under wool.

"I didn't go in alone," he said. The admission scraped on the way out. "I wasn't going to let the house choose that for me again."

For a moment she did not answer. Her gaze remained fixed past the threshold. Then her left hand lifted and adjusted the sleeve over the scar in the small automatic motion he had started noticing every time she was frightened enough to forget she was doing it.

"All right," she said.

They entered together.

The first thing he had to fight was the instinct to take up his old place. His body remembered the war more quickly than his mind did.
